Change of Fatwa on Agent's Right to Receive Due to Treachery
Ruling statement with fatwa adjustment due to change in circumstances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the later Hanafi fatwa on an agent for litigation's authority to receive payment and explain how custom ('urf) and change in circumstances caused the shift from the earlier position.

Core Idea
A fatwa can change when trust conditions erode, even without new textual evidence.

The original Hanafi position (zahir al-riwayah) held that an agent appointed to litigate also has authority to receive the debt. But due to widespread treachery among agents, later jurists revised the fatwa: the agent does not have the right to receive unless the custom of merchants in that town grants it. This lesson examines that shift and the role of custom in Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Contrast the earlier position from zahir al-riwayah (agent for litigation is also agent for receipt) with the later fatwa (agent does not possess the right to receive unless custom provides).
The later fatwa is from al-Fatawa al-Sirajiyya and al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.
2
Note the reason for change
The text explicitly states 'lizuhūr al-khiyānah fī al-wukalā': the emergence of treachery among agents justifies restricting the agent's authority.
This is a circumstantial factor, not a textual abrogation.
3
Understand the role of custom
Both quoted sources (al-Fatawa al-Sughra and al-Fatawa al-Sirajiyya) refer the matter to custom: if the custom among merchants is that the litigator receives the debt, then the agency includes receipt; otherwise not.
Custom ('urf) is a recognized source in Hanafi law for determining implied terms.
4
Recognize the authority weight
The later fatwa (mufta bihi) is from the fatawa mutakhkhirin (later jurists) and overrides the zahir al-riwayah position due to change in circumstances.
This shows that fatwa can adapt to social realities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زبدة النهاية لعمدة الرعاية وللوكيل لكنَّ الفتوى في هذا الزَّمان على أنَّ الوكيل بالخصومة والوكيل بالقاضي لا يملكان القبض؛ لظهور الخيانة في الوكلاء. قوله: لظهور الخيانة في الوكلاء؛ وقد يؤتمن على الخصومة من لا يؤتمن على المال، وفي «البحر» نقلاً عن «الفتاوى الصغرى»: التوكيل بالتقاضي يعتمد العرف إن كان في بلد كان العرفُ بين التجار أنَّ المتقاضي الذي يقبضُ الدين، كان التوكيل بالتقاضي توكيلاً بالقبض وإلا فلا، ذكره عن الفضل. انتهى. وفي «الفتاوى السراجية»: الوكيلُ بالتقاضي وكيل بالقبض في ظاهر الرواية، والفتوى على أنه ينظر إن كان التوكيلُ بذلك في بلد كان العرف بين التجار أنَّ المتقاضي هو الذي يقبضُ الدين كان توكيلاً بالقبض، وإلا فلا. انتهى.
Cream of the conclusion for the Support of Care. And for the agent. But the fatwa in this time is that the agent for litigation and the agent for the judge do not possess the right to receive [the debt] due to the emergence of treachery among agents. His saying: due to the emergence of treachery among agents; and one may be entrusted with litigation who is not entrusted with property. And in al-Bahr, quoting from al-Fatawa al-Sughra: Agency for litigation depends on custom; if in a town the custom among merchants is that the litigator is the one who receives the debt, then the agency for litigation is an agency for receipt, otherwise not; he mentioned it from al-Fadl. End. And in al-Fatawa al-Sirajiyya: The agent for litigation is an agent for receipt in the apparent narration, and the fatwa is that it depends; if the agency was in a town where the custom among merchants is that the litigator receives the debt, then it is an agency for receipt, otherwise not. End.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement addressing a change in fatwa due to societal corruption. The text states that in the author's time, the fatwa is that an agent appointed for litigation (وكيل بالخصومة) or an agent appointed by the judge (وكيل بالقاضي) does not have the authority to receive the debt (القبض). The reason given is the prevalence of treachery among agents—people entrusted with arguing the case may not be trustworthy with money. The commentator then quotes from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq and al-Fatawa al-Sughra that the matter is actually tied to custom (العرف). In commercial settings where the custom is that the litigator also receives the debt, the agency for litigation inherently includes authority to receive. Otherwise, it does not. Al-Fatawa al-Sirajiyya confirms this: the apparent narration (ظاهر الرواية) holds that the agent for litigation is also an agent for receipt, but the later fatwa (الفتوى) looks to custom. This shows the Hanafi method of taking custom into account when applying legal rules, and also how fatwa can shift from the zahir al-riwayah position when circumstances change. The authority weight of this ruling is from later fatawa (fatawa mutakhkhirin), not from the foundational zahir al-riwayah texts.

Key Terms
الوكيل بالخصومة (al-wakīl bi al-khuṣūmah): An agent appointed to litigate on behalf of the principal in court.
القبض (al-qabḍ): Taking physical possession of the debt payment from the debtor.
الخيانة (al-khiyānah): Betrayal of trust by agents, leading to loss of property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why has the fatwa changed regarding the agent for litigation's right to receive?
A) Because of a new hadith that abrogates the old ruling
B) Due to the prevalence of treachery among agents
C) Because the earlier jurists made a mistake
D) Because all agents now refuse to receive payments

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school allows fatwa to shift in response to changing social realities, specifically the prevalence of betrayal, without abandoning the original textual basis.

Agent for Debt Receipt: Does He Have Litigation Authority?
Ruling that agent for debt receipt has litigation authority according to Abu Hanifa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding litigation authority of a debt receipt agent and apply the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) to a given scenario.

Core Idea
According to Abu Hanifa, debt receipt implies litigation because it involves ownership transfer.

This passage examines a subtle but important intra-Hanafi disagreement about the scope of an agent's authority. The matn states that an agent appointed to receive a debt also has the right to litigate before receiving, but the Sahibayn disagree. We must understand the reasoning behind each view and the practical difference in court proceedings.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling in the matn and the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn. The matn says the agent for debt has litigation authority; Abu Hanifa affirms this, the Sahibayn deny it.
2
Identify the opposing proof
Understand the reasoning of the Sahibayn: receipt and litigation are distinct acts; not everyone fit for receipt is skilled in litigation; so the principal's consent cannot be presumed.
3
Analyse the Hanafi response
Examine Abu Hanifa's justification: receiving a debt involves taking ownership of a substitute, which is a legal act requiring litigation. Debts are paid by equivalents, and ownership cannot be effected without litigation.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: when the debtor brings proof that the principal already received or discharged the debt, Abu Hanifa accepts it (since the agent has litigation authority), the Sahibayn do not.
5
Note the scope restriction
This ruling applies only before the agent actually receives the debt; after receipt, the agent has no litigation authority by consensus. Also, the agent for a specific asset never has litigation authority by consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللوكيل بقبض الدين الخصومة لا للذي يقبض العين فلو قام حجة ذي اليد على الوكيل بقبض عبد أن موكله باعه منه تقصر يده ولا يثبت البيع فتقام ثانياً على البيع إذا حضر الغائب. قوله: وللوكيل... الخ؛ يعني أنَّ الوكيل بقبض الدين يملك الخصومة قبل القبض عند الإمام، وأما عندهما لا يملك الخصومة، وهو قول الأئمة الثلاثة، ورواية الحسن عن الإمام. وجه قولهما: إنَّ القبض غير الخصومة، فلا يكون الوكيل بالقبض وكيلاً بالخصومة، وأيضاً ليس كلُّ من يصلح للقبض يعرف الخصومة، ويهتدي إلى المحاكمة، فلا يحصل الرِّضاء من الموكل. ووجه قول الإمام: إنَّ الموكَّلَ وكَّل بقبض الدين بتملك المقبوض بما في ذمة المديون؛ لأنَّ قبض نفس الدين لا يتصوَّر؛ ولذا قلنا: إنَّ الديون تقضى بأمثالها، فإنَّ المقبوض ملك المطلوب حقيقة، وبالقبض يتملك بدلاً عن الدين، فيكون وكيلاً في حق التمليك، وذلك لا يكون إلا بالخصومة. وثمرة الخلاف تظهر فيما إذا أقام الخصم البينة على استيفاء الموكل، أو إبرائه، فعند الإمام تقبل، وعندهما: لا، وقيَّدنا بقوله: قبل القبض؛ لأنه بعد القبض لا يكون له الخصومة اتفاقاً، كما في «مجمع الأنهر»، وغيره. قوله: بقبض الدين؛ قيَّد بالدين؛ لأنَّ الوكيل بقبض العين لا يكون وكيلاً بالخصومة بالإجماع. والأصل: إنَّ التوكيل إذا وقع باستيفاء عين حقه لم يكن وكيلاً بالخصومة؛ لأنَّ التوكيل وقع بالقبض لا غير وإذا وقع التوكيل بالتملك كان وكيلاً بالخصومة؛ لأنَّ التملك إنشاء تصرُّف، وحقوق العقد تتعلق بالعاقد، فكان خصماً فيها. كذا في الكفاية. ولذا قال المصنف بعد هذا: لا للذي يقبض العين؛ يعني أنَّ من وكل بقبض العين لا يملك الخصومة؛ لأنه أمين محض، فإنه ليس بوكيل بالمبادلة، فلم يتعلق الحقوق بالقابض، فلا ينتصب خصماً، فأشبه ذلك الوكيل الرسول. قوله: فلو قام... الخ؛ تفريع على قوله: لا للذي يقبض العين؛ يعني لو برهن ذو اليد على الوكيل بقبض عبد أن موكله باعه منه، تقصر يد الوكيل عنه، ولا يثبت البيع، فيلزم على ذي اليد إعادة البينة إذا حضر الموكل أو من يقوم مقامه.
And for the agent for receiving the debt is litigation, not for the one who receives the [specific] asset. So if the one in possession proves against the agent for receiving a slave that his principal sold it to him, his hand is restricted, and the sale is not established, so the proof is presented a second time regarding the sale when the absent one arrives. His saying: And for the agent... etc.; meaning that the agent for receiving the debt possesses litigation before receipt according to the Imam, but according to them [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] he does not possess litigation, and it is the saying of the three imams, and a narration of al-Hasan from the Imam. The reasoning of them two: Receipt is not litigation, so the agent for receipt is not an agent for litigation; also, not everyone fit for receipt knows litigation and is guided to court, so the consent of the principal is not achieved. The reasoning of the Imam: The principal appointed [him] for receiving the debt to take ownership of the received thing in exchange for what is in the debtor's liability; because receipt of the debt itself is inconceivable; hence we said: debts are paid by their equivalents, for the received thing is the property of the obligated one in reality, and by receipt he [the agent] takes ownership as a substitute for the debt, so he is an agent regarding ownership, and that does not happen except by litigation. The fruit of the difference appears when the opponent establishes proof of the principal's receipt or his discharge; according to the Imam it is accepted, according to them it is not. And we restricted [the Imam's view] by his saying: before receipt; because after receipt he does not have litigation by agreement, as in Majma' al-Anhur and others. His saying: for receiving the debt; he restricted it to debt because the agent for receiving a specific asset is not an agent for litigation by consensus. The principle: If the agency occurs for collecting a specific right, he is not an agent for litigation because the agency is only for receipt; and if the agency occurs for taking ownership, he is an agent for litigation because taking ownership is an act of disposition, and the rights of the contract attach to the contracting party, so he becomes a litigant in it. As in al-Kifaya. Hence the author says after this: not for the one who receives the [specific] asset; meaning that whoever is appointed to receive a specific asset does not possess litigation because he is a mere trustee, for he is not an agent for exchange, so rights do not attach to the receiver, so he does not become a litigant, so he resembles the agent who is a messenger. His saying: 'so if...' etc.; this is a branch of his saying: 'not for the one who receives the [specific] asset'; meaning if the one in possession proves against the agent for receiving a slave that his principal sold it to him, the hand of the agent is restricted from it, and the sale is not established, so it is incumbent upon the one in possession to re-present the proof when the principal appears or his representative.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether an agent appointed to receive a debt (وكيل بقبض الدين) also has the authority to litigate (الخصومة) before receiving the debt. The matn states that such an agent does have the authority for litigation, unlike an agent appointed to receive a specific asset (وكيل بقبض العين). The commentary explains the disagreement: Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the agent for receiving a debt also has the authority to litigate before actual receipt, while his two companions Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn) deny this. There is also a narration from Hasan ibn Ziyad that Abu Hanifa's view is the same as the Sahibayn. The Sahibayn argue that receipt and litigation are distinct acts; appointing for receipt does not imply appointment for litigation, and not everyone capable of receiving is skilled in litigation, so the principal's consent cannot be presumed. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that receiving a debt involves taking ownership (تمليك) of a substitute for the debt, which is a legal act that inherently requires litigation. Debts are paid by equivalents; the agent takes ownership of the payment, and ownership cannot be effected without litigation if the debtor disputes. The practical consequence (ثمرة الخلاف) appears when the debtor brings proof that the principal already received payment or discharged the debt: according to Abu Hanifa that proof is accepted (since the agent has authority to litigate), but according to the Sahibayn it is not accepted because the agent's litigation authority is not established. This ruling applies only before the agent actually receives the debt; after receipt, the agent no longer has litigation authority by consensus. The text then distinguishes the case of an agent for receiving a specific asset (like a slave): such an agent does not have litigation authority by consensus, because he is a mere trustee (أمين محض) and not an agent for exchange. A subcase is given: if the person in possession of the slave proves that the principal sold the slave to him, the agent's hand is restricted but the sale is not proven against the principal; the proof must be presented again when the principal returns. This illustrates the principle that the agent for a specific asset cannot be a litigant. The authority weight here: the matn position (agent for debt has litigation) is from zahir al-riwayah, but the Sahibayn's view is also transmitted. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is not stated explicitly here, but the commentary presents both with detailed reasoning.

Key Terms
الوكيل بقبض الدين (al-wakīl bi qabḍ al-dayn): An agent authorized to collect a debt from the debtor.
الخصومة (al-khuṣūmah): Litigation or legal argumentation in court to establish a right.
التمليك (al-tamlīk): The act of transferring ownership; here, the agent takes ownership of the substitute for the debt.
العين (al-ʿayn): A specific, tangible asset (e.g., a slave, a house).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the practical consequence (ثمرة الخلاف) between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the agent for debt receipt?
A) Whether the agent can delegate to another agent
B) Whether the debtor's proof of payment to the principal is accepted in court
C) Whether the agent can receive the debt in installments
D) Whether the principal can dismiss the agent after receipt

Key Takeaway
The authority of an agent for debt receipt to litigate before receiving is disputed within the Hanafi school, hinging on whether receipt is a physical act or a legal transfer of ownership.

Agent's Litigation Authority: Debt vs. Object in Hanafi Fiqh
Ruling on litigation authority of agents for debt vs. object

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on whether an agent for receiving a debt or an object has litigation authority, and explain the reasoning behind each position.

Core Idea
An agent for receiving a debt has litigation authority according to Abu Hanifa, but not according to the Sahibayn – the distinction hinges on ownership vs. trusteeship.

In Hanafi agency law, the authority to litigate is not automatically assumed; it depends on the nature of the agency. This passage from the classical text presents an intra-madhhab disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two foremost disciples on whether an agent appointed to collect a debt has the right to represent the principal in court. Understanding this nuance is crucial for anyone dealing with legal representation in Islamic finance or dispute resolution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of agents
The passage contrasts two agents: one appointed to receive a debt (وكيل بقبض الدين) and one to receive a specific object (وكيل بقبض العين). Note the difference in the nature of the receipt.
Debt receipt involves transfer of ownership; object receipt is mere custody.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and reasoning
Abu Hanifa rules that the agent for debt has litigation authority because he is engaged in taking ownership (تملك), a creative disposition. Rights of contract attach to him as contractor, making him a litigant.
This is based on the talil (reasoning) given in the commentary.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and reasoning
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the agent for debt does not have litigation authority. They view his role as limited to receipt, not involving the broader rights of a party to a contract.
The passage does not elaborate their dalil, but the implication is that they consider the agent a mere custodian.
4
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
If the debtor denies the debt, under Abu Hanifa the agent can sue and defend; under the Sahibayn, the principal himself must appear in court. This affects litigation strategy and costs.
This is the operational consequence of the ikhtilaf.
5
Note the consensus on object receipt
Both parties agree that an agent for receiving a specific object has no litigation authority. Such an agent is a mere trustee (أمين), not involved in exchange.
This part is not disputed within the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوكالة باب الوكالة بالخصومة والقبض وعزل الوكيل بقبض الدين الخصومة، لا للذي يقبضُ العين. هذا عند أبي حنيفة له، وأما عندهما: لا يملك الخصومة.
The Book of Agency, Chapter on Agency for Litigation and Receiving and Dismissal of the Agent. [The agent appointed] for receiving a debt [has] litigation [authority], not the one who receives a specific object. This is according to Abu Hanifa: he has it. As for according to the two [disciples]: he does not possess litigation [authority].
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage states a ruling on the litigation authority of an agent. The text distinguishes two types of agents: one appointed to receive a debt (وكيل بقبض الدين) and one appointed to receive a specific object (وكيل بقبض العين). For the agent receiving a debt, Abu Hanifa holds that he does have the authority to litigate (خصومة) on behalf of the principal, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that he does not. For the agent receiving a specific object, there is consensus that he has no litigation authority. The reasoning, as explained in the commentary, is that an agent for receiving a debt is engaged in a transfer of ownership (تملك), which is a creative disposition (إنشاء تصرف), and rights of contract attach to the contractor, making him a litigant. In contrast, an agent for receiving a specific object is a mere trustee (أمين محض), not involved in exchange, so rights do not attach to him, and he cannot be a litigant.

Key Terms
وكيل بالخصومة (wakīl bi al-khuṣūmah): An agent authorized to represent the principal in legal proceedings and litigation.
بقبض الدين (bi qabḍ al-dayn): Appointed to receive a debt, i.e., to collect a monetary obligation.
بقبض العين (bi qabḍ al-ʿayn): Appointed to receive a specific physical object (عين), such as a slave or a piece of property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), does an agent appointed to receive a debt have the authority to litigate?
A) Yes, he does.
B) No, he does not.
C) Only if the debt is disputed.
D) Only if the agent is also a lawyer.

Key Takeaway
An agent for receiving a debt is granted litigation authority only by Abu Hanifa; the Sahibayn (and the relied-upon later view) deny this without explicit authorization, distinguishing debt receipt from object receipt.

Restraining Agent's Hand on Third-Party Proof
Ruling on restraining agent's hand when a third party proves the principal sold the object

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on restraining an agent's hand when a third party proves the principal sold the object, and explain the istihsan reasoning that distinguishes possession from ownership.

Core Idea
Istihsan restrains the agent's hand but does not prove the sale — possession and ownership are separated.

In Hanafi agency law, a third party may bring proof that the principal sold an object to him while the agent is handling it. By strict analogy (qiyas), such proof should not affect the agent because he is not a litigant regarding the sale. However, the Hanafis apply istihsan to partially accept the proof—restraining the agent's hand—while postponing the sale's confirmation until the principal appears. This lesson unpacks that nuanced ruling and its application to analogous cases.

Guided Path
1
State the facts of the case
Identify the parties: principal (absent), agent (in possession), third party (dhū al-yad) who claims the principal sold the object to him and brings proof (bayyinah) to the agent.
The proof is brought against the agent, not the principal.
2
Explain the qiyas (analogy) ruling
By strict analogy: a claim brought against a non-litigant (the agent regarding the sale) is not accepted—proof has no effect. Therefore, the agent's hand would not be restrained.
This is the general principle: litigation requires proper parties.
3
Identify the istihsan override
Imam Abu Hanifa applies istihsan: the agent is a litigant regarding the hand (possession), so proof is accepted to restrain the hand but not to prove the sale.
The agent's control over the object makes him a proper party for possession issues.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The proof restrains the agent (he cannot deliver the object), but does not prove the sale. The third party must re-establish the proof when the principal appears.
This protects the principal's right to be heard.
5
Extend to analogous cases
The same principle applies if a wife claims divorce or a slave claims manumission: the agent's hand is restrained but the right is not proven until the principal appears.
These cases all involve a claim based on the principal's action, not the agent's.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلو قام حجة ذي اليد على الوكيل بقبض عبد أن موكله باعه منه، يقصر يده، ولا يثبت البيع، فتقام ثانياً على البيع إذا حضر الغائب. كما يقصر يد الوكيل بنقل المرأة والعبد بلا طلاق وعتق، لو قامت حجتهما عليه حتى يحضر الغائب.
If the proof of the possessor (of the object) is established against the agent for receiving a slave that his principal sold it to him, his hand is restrained, and the sale is not proven; then the proof is established again regarding the sale when the absent person [the principal] appears. Similarly, the agent's hand is restrained regarding the transfer of a wife and a slave without divorce and manumission, if their proof is established against him until the absent person appears.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage presents a case where a third party (ذو اليد, the one in possession of the object) claims that the principal sold the object to him and brings proof (بينة) to the agent. The ruling is based on istihsan (juristic preference). By qiyas, the proof should not be accepted because it is presented against a non-litigant (the agent has no litigation authority for the object). But by istihsan, the agent's hand is restrained (قصر اليد) — meaning he cannot take possession or act further — but the sale itself is not proven against the principal. The agent is considered a litigant only regarding the hand (possession), not regarding the substantive right. Therefore, when the principal returns, the proof must be presented again to establish the sale. The same principle applies to a wife claiming divorce and a slave claiming manumission: the agent's hand is restrained but the divorce/manumission does not take effect until the principal appears and the proof is re-established.

Key Terms
قصر اليد (qaṣr al-yad): Restraining the agent's hand from taking possession or acting further with the object.
ذو اليد (dhū al-yad): The person currently in possession of the object; the third party claiming ownership.
بينة (bayyinah): Proof or evidence, typically by two witnesses or one witness and an oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where a third party proves that the principal sold the object, what is the immediate effect on the agent?
A) The sale is confirmed and the agent must hand over the object.
B) The agent's hand is restrained but the sale is not proven.
C) The agent is dismissed immediately.
D) The agent becomes the owner of the object.

Key Takeaway
Istihsan allows the agent's hand to be restrained on third-party proof of sale, but the sale itself remains unproven until the principal appears.

Validity of the Agent's Confession in Litigation
Ruling that an agent's confession in litigation is valid before the judge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for an agent's confession in litigation and identify the condition that makes it valid.

Core Idea
A litigation agent's confession before the judge is valid and binds the principal.

This passage from a Hanafi legal text addresses the validity of an agent's confession during litigation. The agent is appointed to represent the principal in court. The key question is whether such a confession has legal effect. The text clearly states that the confession of a litigation agent is valid before the judge, whether the agent represents the plaintiff or the defendant.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the confession of a litigation agent. The ruling is that it is sahih (valid).
The text begins with وصح إقرار الوكيل بالخصومة (and the confession of the agent for litigation is valid).
2
Identify the condition
The agent must be appointed specifically for litigation (وكيل بالخصومة). This is the key condition that distinguishes litigation agency from other types of agency.
3
Distinguish between plaintiff's and defendant's agent
The text gives examples: the plaintiff's agent (وكيل المدعي) confesses that the principal received the money (claim satisfied); the defendant's agent (وكيل المدعى عليه) confesses that the principal owes the money (liability).
4
Note the scope
The confession is binding on the principal only within the scope of the agency. The agent's authority to litigate is essential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصح إقرار الوكيل بالخصومة عند القاضي، سواء كان وكيل المدعي أو وكيل المدعى عليه، وإقرار وكيل المدعي هو أن يقر أن موكله قبض هذا المال، وإقرار وكيل المدعى عليه هو أن يقر بوجوب المال على المدعى عليه.
And the confession of the agent for litigation is valid before the judge, whether he is the agent of the plaintiff or the agent of the defendant. The confession of the plaintiff's agent is that he acknowledges that his principal received this money, and the confession of the defendant's agent is that he acknowledges the obligation of the money upon the defendant.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage states a ruling regarding the validity of an agent's confession (إقرار) when the agent is authorized for litigation (وكيل بالخصومة). The confession is valid before the judge and has legal effect. The text specifies that this applies to both the agent of the plaintiff and the agent of the defendant. For the plaintiff's agent, his confession would be that the principal has already received the money (thereby admitting the claim is satisfied). For the defendant's agent, his confession would be that the money is owed (thereby admitting liability). The commentary notes that the confession is valid only if the agent is authorized for litigation; an agent without such authority cannot make a valid confession regarding the dispute.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrār): A confession or admission made by a party (here the agent) that is binding on the principal in legal proceedings.
وكيل المدعي (wakīl al-muddaʿī): The agent of the plaintiff who brings the claim.
وكيل المدعى عليه (wakīl al-muddaʿā ʿalayh): The agent of the defendant who defends the claim.

B) Only when the agent is the plaintiff's agent.
C) Only when the agent is the defendant's agent.
D) For all types of agency.

Key Takeaway
A confession by an agent authorized for litigation is valid and binding on the principal before the judge.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Agent's Admission in Litigation
Intra-Hanafi dispute on whether agent's admission binds principal outside court

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf on the agent's admission in litigation, explain the proof for each, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
An agent in litigation may admit only in court because his authority is limited to responding before the judge.

In Hanafi law, when a principal appoints an agent for litigation (al-tawkil bi al-khusumah), a key question arises: can the agent make a binding admission outside the court? This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute where Abu Yusuf argues for unrestricted authority, while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad restrict admission to the court session. Understanding this ikhtilaf clarifies the limits of agency in judicial proceedings.

Guided Path
1
State the issue
Identify the exact legal question: whether the agent's admission (iqrar) binds the principal when made outside the court session.
The core is the scope of the agent's authority: does it extend to admission, or only to denial?
2
Present Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf holds that the agent's admission binds the principal anywhere, because the agent stands in the principal's shoes and admission is self-effectual.
His proof: principal's admission is not restricted to court; admission requires no court involvement.
3
Present Abu Hanifa and Muhammad's position
They hold that the agent's admission binds only within the court session, because agency is for responding (jawaab) and response is due before the judge.
They argue that 'khusumah' (litigation) metaphorically means any response in court.
4
Analyze the key evidence
Compare the proofs: Abu Yusuf relies on the self-effectual nature of admission; Abu Hanifa/Muhammad rely on the judge's command 'Answer your adversary' and linguistic analysis.
The text concludes with a preference for the latter view, calling it a sufficient explanation.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: the agent's admission is valid only inside court.
This is the zahir al-riwayah position and is cited as the school's operative ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ثبت أنَّ التوكيل بالخصومة ينصرف إلى مطلق الجواب، فقال أبو يوسف ه : إنَّ الوكيل قائم مقامَ الموكل، وإقرار موكله لا يختص بمجلس القضاء، فكذا إقرار وكيله، والسر فيه : إنَّ الشيء إنّما يختص بمجلس القاضي إذا لم يكن موجباً إلاً بانضمام القضاء إليه، كالبينة والنكول، وأمّا الإقرار فهو موجب بنفسه ، فلا يختص بمجلس القضاء. وقال أبو حنيفة ومحمد له : إنَّ التوكيل يتناولُ ما يملكه الموكَّلُ وهو الجواب، إذ الخصومة يراد بها مطلق الجواب عرفاً مجازاً ، والجواب يكون بما يسمى خصومة حقيقة ، وهو الإنكار، وبما يسمى خصومة مجازاً، وهو الإقرار في مجلس القضاء، فإنه يسمى خصومة ؛ لأنه خرج في مقابلة الخصومة كما في تسمية جزاء السيئة سيئة، أو لأنَّ الخصومة سبب له. فيكون من إطلاق اسم السبب على المسبب، أو لأنَّ مجلس القضاء مجلس الخصومة فيما يجري فيه يسمّى خصومة، والخصومة تتناول الإقرار، والإنكار من عموم المجاز، لا من استعمال اللفظ في حقيقته ومجازه، فيملك الوكيل الإقرار من حيث إنّه جواب لا من حيث أنه إقرار، والجواب يستحق عند القاضي. والدليل على أنَّ المراد بالخصومة هاهنا الجواب مطلقاً أن القاضي يأمر بالجواب ، فيقول له : أجب خصمك لا بالخصومة، وفي حملها على الجواب هاهنا يصح التوكيلُ به على كل تقدير، وفي حملها على حقيقتها وهو الإنكار لا يصح به التوكيل إلا على تقدير أن يكون محقاً في الإنكار لا مبطلاً فيه. وأنت عرفت أن قول الشارح : ولنا : إنَّ الخصومة... الخ دليل مجمل في مقابلة ما استدل به زفره لا على مذهب أبي يوسف أو الطرفين معيناً، ونحن بينا دليلهم بيانا شافياً، فتذكر وتشكر
When it is established that agency for litigation refers to absolute response, Abu Yusuf said: The agent stands in the place of the principal, and the principal's admission is not restricted to the court session, so too the admission of his agent. The secret is that a thing is only restricted to the court session if it is not effectual except by joining the court to it, like evidence and refusal; but admission is effectual by itself, so it is not restricted to the court session. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad said: Agency includes what the principal possesses, which is the response, because litigation refers metaphorically to absolute response customarily. The response includes what is called litigation literally, which is denial, and what is called litigation metaphorically, which is admission in the court session, for it is called litigation because it occurs in response to litigation, like naming the punishment of an evil deed an evil deed, or because litigation is its cause, so it is from the usage of the name of the cause upon the effect, or because the court session is the place for litigation, so what occurs there is called litigation. And litigation includes admission and denial from the generality of metaphor, not from using the word in its literal and metaphorical sense together. So the agent has the authority to admit insofar as it is a response, not insofar as it is an admission. And the response is required before the judge. The evidence that what is meant by litigation here is absolute response is that the judge orders the response, saying: 'Answer your adversary,' not 'Litigate.' Interpreting it as response here makes the agency valid in all circumstances, while interpreting it as its literal meaning, denial, does not make the agency valid except when the agent is truthful in denial, not false. And you have known that the commentator's statement 'Our evidence is that litigation...' etc. is a general proof against what Zufar cited, not specifically against Abu Yusuf's or both sides. And we have explained their evidence sufficiently, so remember and be grateful.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the scope of agency in litigation (التوكیل بالخصومة). The issue: if a person is appointed as an agent to litigate, does the agent have the authority to make an admission (إقرار) that binds the principal, and is that admission valid only within the court session? Abu Yusuf argues that the agent stands in the principal's shoes, and since the principal's admission is effective anywhere, the agent's admission is also effective anywhere, not limited to the court. He reasons that admission is self-effectual, unlike evidence (بینة) or refusal (نکول) which only have effect when joined with the court's decision. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad take a more restricted view: agency for litigation only authorizes the agent to respond (جواب) to the adversary's claims, and the response can be either denial (إنکار) or admission (إقرار) but only within the court session. They argue that the term 'خصومة' (litigation) is used metaphorically for any response in court, including admission, because admission is the counterpart of the adversary's claim. The evidence they provide is that the judge says 'Answer your adversary' not 'Litigate', showing that the required action is response, and agency for response is valid universally. They further argue that if 'خصومة' were taken literally as denial, agency for denial would only be valid if the principal is truthful, which is not always known. Thus, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that the agent may admit in court but not outside, because his authority is limited to responding in court proceedings.

Key Terms
خصومة (khusumah): In this context, litigation or contestation. The passage discusses whether it refers to denial only or to any response in court, including admission.
إقرار (iqrar): Admission or confession. Here it specifically refers to an admission of a claim made by the adversary in the court session.
وكيل (wakil): Agent or representative appointed by the principal to perform a specific task. Here, the agent is appointed for litigation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, why does the agent's admission only bind inside court?
A) Because admission is not effectual without court.
B) Because the agent's authority is limited to responding, and response is required before the judge.
C) Because the principal did not authorize admission.
D) Because the judge must be present.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi restricts the agent's admission in litigation to the court session, limiting agency to the scope of responding before the judge.

Invalidity of Appointing the Guarantor as Agent for Debt Collection
Invalid agency when appointing guarantor as agent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state why an agency contract is void when the agent is the guarantor of the debt and identify the essential condition of agency that is violated.

Core Idea
Agency requires working for another; appointing the guarantor as agent voids it.

In Hanafi law, agency (wakalah) is a contract where the agent acts on behalf of the principal. A key condition is that the agent must work for the principal, not for himself. This lesson examines a specific case: can a creditor appoint the guarantor (kafil) as his agent to collect the debt from the principal debtor? The answer is no, because the guarantor’s collection would discharge his own liability, making him act for himself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and parties
Consider the scenario: a creditor has a debt owed by a debtor, and a third party guarantees that debt. The creditor then appoints the guarantor as his agent to collect the debt from the debtor.
This is a specific application of general agency rules.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The ruling is that this agency is batil (void). The contract of agency is invalid from the outset.
Batil means no legal effect; the agent has no authority.
3
Explain the reasoning
The essential element (rukn) of agency is that the agent must act for another (al-‘amal li ghayrih). Here, the guarantor, by collecting, would be discharging his own liability as guarantor, thus acting for himself. Therefore, the rukn is absent, and the contract is void.
The same reasoning applies if the agent benefits personally.
4
Contrast with the valid case
The text contrasts this with appointing the debtor himself to discharge the debt. That is valid because it is considered a transfer of ownership (tamlik), not agency. In tamlik, the person acts for himself, but it is not agency.
The analogy is like telling a wife 'divorce yourself' – it is an empowerment, not agency.
5
Summarize the principle
Agency requires the agent to act for the principal. If the agent's act primarily benefits himself or discharges his own obligation, the agency is void.
This ensures the fiduciary nature of agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوكالة باب الوكالة بالخصومة والقبض وعزل الوكيل
كتوكيل رب المال كفيلَهُ بقبض ماله عن المكفولِ عنه ، ومصدق الوكيل بقبض دينِهِ إن كان غريماً أُمر بدفع دينه إلى الوكيل
كتوكيل" رب المالِ كفيلهُ بقبض ماله عن المكفول عنه) : أي كما لا يصح توكيل ربِّ المال الكفيل بقبض المكفول به عن المكفول عنه ؛ لأنَّ الوكيل مَن يعملُ لغيره وهنا يعمل لنفسه .
( ومصدق الوكيل بقبض دينِهِ إن كان غريماً أُمر" بدفع دينه إلى الوكيل) : أي ادعى رجل أنه وكيل الغائب بقبض دينه من الغريم ؛ فصدقه الغريم، أُمر بتسليم الدين إلى الوكيل ١ ] قوله : كتوكيل...الخ ؛ صورة المسألة : إنه إذا كان لرجل على رجل دين ، وكفل بـه رجـل ، فوكَّلَ الطالبُ الكفيل بقبض ذلك الدين من الذي عليه فلم يصح هذا التوكيل ؛ لأنَّ الوكيل هو الذي يعمل لغيره.
ولو صححنا هذه الوكالة صار عاملاً لنفسه، ساعياً في براءة ذمته، فانعدم ركنُ الوكالة، وهو العمل للغير، فبطل عقد الوكالة، وإن اختلج في قلبك أنَّ الدائن إذا وكَّل المديون بإبراء نفسه عن الدين يصح ، وإن كان عاملاً لنفسه ساعياً في براءة ذمته فأرحه بأن هذا تمليك وليس بتوكيل ، كما في قوله لامرأته : طلقي نفسك.
(٢) قوله : أمر...الخ ؛ لأن تصديق الغريم إقرار على نفسه ؛ لأنَّ ما يدفع خالص حقه، إذ الديون تقضى بأمثالها ، ومن أقرَّ على نفسه بشيءٍ أُمر بتسليمه إلى المقر له ، حتى لو ادعى أنه أوفى الدين إلى الدائن لا يُصدَّق ؛ إذ لزمه الدفع إلى الوكيل بإقراره، ولم يثبت الإيفاء بمجرد دعواه ، فلا يؤخِّرُ حقه.
وهاهنا إشكال قوي، وهو أنَّ التوكيل بقبض الدين توكيل بالاستقراض معنى ؛ لأنَّ الديون تقضى بأمثالها ، فما قبضه ربُّ الدَّين من المديون يصير مضموناً عليه، وله على الغريم مثل ذلك، فالتقيا قصاصاً، والتوكيل بالاستقراض لا يصح. والجواب : إِنَّ التوكيل بقبض الدَّينِ رسالة بالاستقراض من حيث المعنى، وليس بتوكيل بالاستقراض ؛ لأنه لا بدَّ للتوكيل بقبض الدين من إضافة القبض إلى موكله بأن يقول : إنَّ فلاناً وكلني بقبض ما له عليك من الدين، كما لا بُدَّ للرسول في الاستقراض من الإضافة إلى المرسل ، بأن يقول : أرسلني إليك وقال لك : أقرضني
Book of Agency, Chapter of Agency for Litigation and Collection and Dismissal of the Agent. [Ruling:] Like the creditor appointing his guarantor to collect his money from the guaranteed person; and the agent is believed regarding collection of his debt if he is a debtor, he is ordered to pay his debt to the agent. [Commentary on 'like the creditor appointing his guarantor...'] i.e., just as it is invalid for the creditor to appoint the guarantor to collect the guaranteed money from the guaranteed person because the agent is one who works for another, and here he works for himself. [Commentary on 'and the agent is believed...'] i.e., if a man claims he is the agent of an absent person to collect his debt from the debtor, and the debtor believes him, he is ordered to hand over the debt to the agent. [Note on the first ruling:] The scenario: if a man has a debt on another, and a third person guarantees it, and the creditor appoints that guarantor as agent to collect that debt from the debtor, this agency is invalid because the agent is supposed to work for someone else, but here he would be working for himself. If we validated this agency, he would become an agent for himself, seeking to discharge his own liability, so the essential element of agency—working for another—would be absent, thus the agency contract is void. If it occurs to your mind that if the creditor appoints the debtor to discharge himself from the debt it is valid, even though he acts for himself seeking discharge, relieve yourself by recognizing that this is a transfer of ownership (tamlik) not agency, like saying to his wife: 'Divorce yourself.' [Note on the second ruling:] He said: 'ordered...' etc. because the debtor's acknowledgment is an admission against himself, since what he pays is purely his right, as debts are discharged by equivalents. Whoever admits something against himself is ordered to hand it over to the one he admitted for. So even if he claims he paid the debt to the creditor, he is not believed, because he became obligated to pay to the agent by his admission, and the payment is not proven by mere claim, so his right is not delayed. Here there is a strong objection: agency for collection of debt is essentially agency for borrowing (istiqrad) because debts are discharged by equivalents; what the debt-owner (creditor) collects from the debtor becomes a liability on him, and he has an equivalent claim against the debtor, so they offset (qisas), and agency for borrowing is invalid. The answer: agency for collection of debt is a message (risalah) conveying a request for borrowing in terms of meaning, not an agency for borrowing, because agency for collection requires attribution of collection to the principal—e.g., 'So-and-so authorized me to collect what he has as a debt from you'—just as a messenger for borrowing must attribute the request to the sender, saying: 'He sent me to you and told me to ask you to lend me.'
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents two rulings regarding agency for debt collection (التوكیل بقبض الدین). First: if a creditor appoints a guarantor (كفیل) as his agent to collect the debt from the principal debtor, this agency is invalid. The reason is that the agent, by collecting, would be discharging his own liability as guarantor, thus acting for himself, not for the principal. This is contrasted with the case where the principal appoints the debtor himself to discharge the debt; that is valid because it is a form of tamlik (transfer of ownership), not agency. Second: if a man claims to be an agent of an absent creditor to collect a debt, and the debtor acknowledges him (verifies his claim as agent), the debtor is ordered to pay the debt to the alleged agent. This is because the debtor's acknowledgment is an admission (إقرار) against himself, obligating him to pay. If the debtor later claims he already paid the principal, he is not believed unless he can prove it. The passage then addresses a potential objection: collecting a debt is like borrowing because the creditor effectively loans the collected amount back to himself? Actually, the collection is treated as a message (رسالة) to request a loan, not an agency for borrowing, because the agent expressly attributes the collection to his principal. Thus the parallel to borrowing fails and the agency is valid.

Key Terms
توكيل بقبض الدين (tawkīl bi qabḍ al-dayn): Agency for collection of a debt. The agent is authorized to receive the debt payment from the debtor on behalf of the creditor.
كفيل (kafīl): Guarantor or surety. One who is liable for the debt if the principal debtor defaults.
رسالة (risālah): Message or sending. Here it refers to the agent being a messenger (rasūl) conveying the request for loan, not an agent (wakīl) in the full sense of making contracts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is it invalid for a creditor to appoint the guarantor as his agent to collect the debt from the principal debtor?
A) Because the guarantor is a minor
B) Because the guarantor works for himself, not for the creditor
C) Because the principal debtor is absent
D) Because the debt is not yet due

Key Takeaway
Appointing a guarantor as agent to collect the debt is void because the agent works for himself, violating the essence of agency.

Agent's Credibility in Debt Collection upon Acknowledgment
Agent's credibility in collection when debtor acknowledges him

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a debtor must pay an alleged agent when he acknowledges the agency, and why subsequent claims of payment to the principal are not believed.

Core Idea
Acknowledgment of agency binds the debtor to pay the agent; later claims of payment are unproven.

When a person claims to be the agent of an absent creditor, the debtor may verify this claim. If he acknowledges the agency, Hanafi law holds him to his admission. This lesson explores the ruling that the debtor must pay the alleged agent, and the effect of his acknowledgment. It also addresses a potential objection that agency for collection is akin to agency for borrowing, which is invalid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A man claims to be the agent of an absent creditor with authority to collect a debt from the debtor. The debtor believes him (acknowledges the agency).
The debtor's acknowledgment is key.
2
State the ruling
The debtor is ordered to pay the debt to the alleged agent. His acknowledgment binds him.
The ruling is based on iqrar (admission).
3
Understand the reasoning
The debtor's acknowledgment is an admission (iqrar) against himself. In Hanafi law, an admission of a right obligates the admitter to fulfill it. Here, he admits that the claimant is the agent, so he must pay him.
This is a general principle: iqrar is binding.
4
Note the consequence of later claims
If the debtor later claims he already paid the creditor directly, he is not believed because his obligation to pay the agent was established by his own admission, and the claimed payment is unproven.
The burden of proof is on the debtor to prove payment to the agent or principal, but the admission shifts the burden.
5
Address the objection about borrowing
The text anticipates an objection: collection of debt is like borrowing (istiqrad) because debts are discharged by equivalents, and agency for borrowing is invalid. The response: agency for collection is actually a message (risalah) conveying the principal's request, not an agency for borrowing, because the agent must attribute the collection to the principal explicitly.
This distinction preserves the validity of the agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوكالة باب الوكالة بالخصومة والقبض وعزل الوكيل
كتوكيل رب المال كفيلَهُ بقبض ماله عن المكفولِ عنه ، ومصدق الوكيل بقبض دينِهِ إن كان غريماً أُمر بدفع دينه إلى الوكيل
كتوكيل" رب المالِ كفيلهُ بقبض ماله عن المكفول عنه) : أي كما لا يصح توكيل ربِّ المال الكفيل بقبض المكفول به عن المكفول عنه ؛ لأنَّ الوكيل مَن يعملُ لغيره وهنا يعمل لنفسه .
( ومصدق الوكيل بقبض دينِهِ إن كان غريماً أُمر" بدفع دينه إلى الوكيل) : أي ادعى رجل أنه وكيل الغائب بقبض دينه من الغريم ؛ فصدقه الغريم، أُمر بتسليم الدين إلى الوكيل ١ ] قوله : كتوكيل...الخ ؛ صورة المسألة : إنه إذا كان لرجل على رجل دين ، وكفل بـه رجـل ، فوكَّلَ الطالبُ الكفيل بقبض ذلك الدين من الذي عليه فلم يصح هذا التوكيل ؛ لأنَّ الوكيل هو الذي يعمل لغيره.
ولو صححنا هذه الوكالة صار عاملاً لنفسه، ساعياً في براءة ذمته، فانعدم ركنُ الوكالة، وهو العمل للغير، فبطل عقد الوكالة، وإن اختلج في قلبك أنَّ الدائن إذا وكَّل المديون بإبراء نفسه عن الدين يصح ، وإن كان عاملاً لنفسه ساعياً في براءة ذمته فأرحه بأن هذا تمليك وليس بتوكيل ، كما في قوله لامرأته : طلقي نفسك.
(٢) قوله : أمر...الخ ؛ لأن تصديق الغريم إقرار على نفسه ؛ لأنَّ ما يدفع خالص حقه، إذ الديون تقضى بأمثالها ، ومن أقرَّ على نفسه بشيءٍ أُمر بتسليمه إلى المقر له ، حتى لو ادعى أنه أوفى الدين إلى الدائن لا يُصدَّق ؛ إذ لزمه الدفع إلى الوكيل بإقراره، ولم يثبت الإيفاء بمجرد دعواه ، فلا يؤخِّرُ حقه.
وهاهنا إشكال قوي، وهو أنَّ التوكيل بقبض الدين توكيل بالاستقراض معنى ؛ لأنَّ الديون تقضى بأمثالها ، فما قبضه ربُّ الدَّين من المديون يصير مضموناً عليه، وله على الغريم مثل ذلك، فالتقيا قصاصاً، والتوكيل بالاستقراض لا يصح. والجواب : إِنَّ التوكيل بقبض الدَّينِ رسالة بالاستقراض من حيث المعنى، وليس بتوكيل بالاستقراض ؛ لأنه لا بدَّ للتوكيل بقبض الدين من إضافة القبض إلى موكله بأن يقول : إنَّ فلاناً وكلني بقبض ما له عليك من الدين، كما لا بُدَّ للرسول في الاستقراض من الإضافة إلى المرسل ، بأن يقول : أرسلني إليك وقال لك : أقرضني
Book of Agency, Chapter of Agency for Litigation and Collection and Dismissal of the Agent. [Ruling:] Like the creditor appointing his guarantor to collect his money from the guaranteed person; and the agent is believed regarding collection of his debt if he is a debtor, he is ordered to pay his debt to the agent. [Commentary on 'like the creditor appointing his guarantor...'] i.e., just as it is invalid for the creditor to appoint the guarantor to collect the guaranteed money from the guaranteed person because the agent is one who works for another, and here he works for himself. [Commentary on 'and the agent is believed...'] i.e., if a man claims he is the agent of an absent person to collect his debt from the debtor, and the debtor believes him, he is ordered to hand over the debt to the agent. [Note on the first ruling:] The scenario: if a man has a debt on another, and a third person guarantees it, and the creditor appoints that guarantor as agent to collect that debt from the debtor, this agency is invalid because the agent is supposed to work for someone else, but here he would be working for himself. If we validated this agency, he would become an agent for himself, seeking to discharge his own liability, so the essential element of agency—working for another—would be absent, thus the agency contract is void. If it occurs to your mind that if the creditor appoints the debtor to discharge himself from the debt it is valid, even though he acts for himself seeking discharge, relieve yourself by recognizing that this is a transfer of ownership (tamlik) not agency, like saying to his wife: 'Divorce yourself.' [Note on the second ruling:] He said: 'ordered...' etc. because the debtor's acknowledgment is an admission against himself, since what he pays is purely his right, as debts are discharged by equivalents. Whoever admits something against himself is ordered to hand it over to the one he admitted for. So even if he claims he paid the debt to the creditor, he is not believed, because he became obligated to pay to the agent by his admission, and the payment is not proven by mere claim, so his right is not delayed. Here there is a strong objection: agency for collection of debt is essentially agency for borrowing (istiqrad) because debts are discharged by equivalents; what the debt-owner (creditor) collects from the debtor becomes a liability on him, and he has an equivalent claim against the debtor, so they offset (qisas), and agency for borrowing is invalid. The answer: agency for collection of debt is a message (risalah) conveying a request for borrowing in terms of meaning, not an agency for borrowing, because agency for collection requires attribution of collection to the principal—e.g., 'So-and-so authorized me to collect what he has as a debt from you'—just as a messenger for borrowing must attribute the request to the sender, saying: 'He sent me to you and told me to ask you to lend me.'
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents two rulings regarding agency for debt collection (التوكیل بقبض الدین). First: if a creditor appoints a guarantor (كفیل) as his agent to collect the debt from the principal debtor, this agency is invalid. The reason is that the agent, by collecting, would be discharging his own liability as guarantor, thus acting for himself, not for the principal. This is contrasted with the case where the principal appoints the debtor himself to discharge the debt; that is valid because it is a form of tamlik (transfer of ownership), not agency. Second: if a man claims to be an agent of an absent creditor to collect a debt, and the debtor acknowledges him (verifies his claim as agent), the debtor is ordered to pay the debt to the alleged agent. This is because the debtor's acknowledgment is an admission (إقرار) against himself, obligating him to pay. If the debtor later claims he already paid the principal, he is not believed unless he can prove it. The passage then addresses a potential objection: collecting a debt is like borrowing because the creditor effectively loans the collected amount back to himself? Actually, the collection is treated as a message (رسالة) to request a loan, not an agency for borrowing, because the agent expressly attributes the collection to his principal. Thus the parallel to borrowing fails and the agency is valid.

Key Terms
توكيل بقبض الدين (tawkīl bi qabḍ al-dayn): Agency for collection of a debt. The agent is authorized to receive the debt payment from the debtor on behalf of the creditor.
كفيل (kafīl): Guarantor or surety. One who is liable for the debt if the principal debtor defaults.
رسالة (risālah): Message or sending. Here it refers to the agent being a messenger (rasūl) conveying the request for loan, not an agent (wakīl) in the full sense of making contracts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a debtor acknowledges an alleged agent for debt collection, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The debtor may refuse payment until the principal confirms
B) The debtor must pay the agent
C) The debtor must pay only if the agent produces written authority
D) The debtor can ignore the claim

Key Takeaway
Acknowledgment of agency obligates the debtor to pay the agent, and subsequent claims of payment to the principal are not accepted without proof.

Invalidity of Deputizing a Guarantor for Debt Collection
Ruling with objection and response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state why a principal cannot deputize a guarantor to collect the debt from the principal debtor, and distinguish between tawkil and risalah in this context.

Core Idea
A guarantor acting as agent works for himself — the agency contract is void because the pillar of working for another is missing.

This passage from the commentary clarifies a key principle in Hanafi agency law: the agent must act for the principal. When a creditor appoints a guarantor to collect the debt from the original debtor, the guarantor is effectively collecting to discharge his own liability, not acting for the creditor. Thus the agency is invalid. The text also addresses objections and distinguishes between agency (tawkil) and a mere message (risalah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled? The principal deputizing his guarantor to collect debt. Ruling: invalid (batil) because the guarantor works for himself, not for the principal.
2
Understand the objection and response
Objection: Deputizing the debtor to discharge himself is valid even though he works for himself. Response: That is tamlik (transfer of ownership), not tawkil.
Tamlik empowers the debtor to own the debt and discharge it; agency requires acting for the principal.
3
Analyze the stronger problem
Deputizing for debt collection is like deputizing for borrowing because debts cancel. The response: It is a risalah (message) for borrowing, not a tawkil, and risalah is valid if attributed to the principal.
A messenger merely conveys the request; the principal remains the real party.
4
Consider al-Zayla'i's critique
Al-Zayla'i notes that even as a messenger, the guarantor cannot litigate, so the answer is not conclusive according to Abu Hanifah.
This shows the depth of the discussion and the need for careful analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتوكيل رب المال كفيله بقبض ماله عن المكفول عنه) : أي كما لا يصح توكيل رب المال الكفيل بقبض المكفول به عن المكفول عنه؛ لأن الوكيل من يعمل لغيره وهنا يعمل لنفسه. ولو صححنا هذه الوكالة صار عاملاً لنفسه، ساعياً في براءة ذمته، فانعدم ركن الوكالة، وهو العمل للغير، فبطل عقد الوكالة، وإن اختلج في قلبك أن الدائن إذا وكَّل المديون بإبراء نفسه عن الدين يصح، وإن كان عاملاً لنفسه ساعياً في براءة ذمته فأرحه بأن هذا تمليك وليس بتوكيل، كما في قوله لامرأته : طلقي نفسك. وهاهنا إشكال قوي، وهو أن التوكيل بقبض الدين توكيل بالاستقراض معنى؛ لأن الديون تقضى بأمثالها، فما قبضه رب الدين من المديون يصير مضموناً عليه، وله على الغريم مثل ذلك، فالتقيا قصاصاً، والتوكيل بالاستقراض لا يصح. والجواب : إن التوكيل بقبض الدين رسالة بالاستقراض من حيث المعنى، وليس بتوكيل بالاستقراض؛ لأنه لا بد للتوكيل بقبض الدين من إضافة القبض إلى موكله بأن يقول : إن فلاناً وكلني بقبض ما له عليك من الدين، كما لا بد للرسول في الاستقراض من الإضافة إلى المرسل، بأن يقول : أرسلني إليك وقال لك : أقرضني. فصح ما ادعينا أن هذا رسالة معنى، والرسالة بالاستقراض جائزة. هكذا ذكره في النهاية معزياً الى الذخيرة، وقال الزيلعي : هذا سؤال حسن، والجواب غير مخلص على قول أبي حنيفة له، فإنه لو كان رسولاً لما كان له أن يخاصم. انتهى.
Like the principal deputizing his guarantor to collect his wealth from the one for whom he is guarantor: meaning, just as it is not valid for the principal to deputize the guarantor to collect the guaranteed amount from the principal debtor, because the agent is one who works for another, and here he works for himself. And if we validate this agency, he becomes working for himself, striving for the discharge of his own liability, so the pillar of agency (working for another) is absent, and the contract of agency becomes void. And if it occurs to your mind that if the creditor deputizes the debtor to discharge himself from the debt, it is valid, even though he works for himself striving for the discharge of his liability, then remove this by saying that this is a tamlik (transfer of ownership) and not an agency, as in his saying to his wife: 'Divorce yourself.' And here there is a strong problem: that deputizing for debt collection is deputizing for borrowing in meaning, because debts are repaid by their equivalents, so what the creditor receives from the debtor becomes a liability upon him, and he has an equivalent against the debtor, so they cancel each other out, and deputizing for borrowing is not valid. The answer: that deputizing for debt collection is a message (risalah) for borrowing in a metaphorical sense, not a deputizing for borrowing, because for deputizing for debt collection it is necessary to attribute the collection to the principal, by saying: 'So-and-so deputized me to collect what he has against you of debt,' just as for a messenger in borrowing it is necessary to attribute to the sender, by saying: 'He sent me to you and said: Lend me.' So what we claimed is correct, that this is a message in meaning, and the message for borrowing is permissible. Thus it is mentioned in al-Nihayah, attributing to al-Dhakhirah, and al-Zayla'i said: This is a good question, but the answer is not conclusive according to Abu Hanifah, because if he were a messenger, he would not have the right to litigate. End.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary on al-Hidayah, explaining the invalidity of a principal (rabb al-mal) deputizing his guarantor (kafil) to collect the debt from the principal debtor (makful 'anhu). The reasoning is that the agent must work for the principal, but here the guarantor is working for himself because collecting the debt discharges his own liability as guarantor. The commentator addresses a potential objection: if a creditor deputizes the debtor himself to discharge the debt, it is valid even though he works for himself. The distinction is that this is tamlik (transfer of ownership), not agency, analogous to a husband telling his wife 'divorce yourself' which is an empowerment to act on his behalf. Then a strong problem is raised: deputizing for debt collection is in meaning deputizing for borrowing (istiqrad), because debts are repaid by equivalents, and the principal receiving from the debtor becomes liable for the equivalent, leading to a set-off, and deputizing for borrowing is invalid. The answer distinguishes: deputizing for debt collection is a message (risalah) for borrowing, not a deputizing, because the agent must attribute the collection to the principal, just as a messenger for borrowing must attribute the request to the sender. Thus it is permissible. Al-Zayla'i critiques this, saying on Abu Hanifah's view the answer is not conclusive because a messenger cannot litigate.

Key Terms
توكيل (tawkil): Deputizing another to act on one's behalf; here, the principal appointing an agent specifically for debt collection.
كفيل (kafil): A guarantor who undertakes to pay a debt if the principal debtor fails; here, the person being deputized to collect the debt from the principal debtor.
رسالة (risalah): A message; here, the agent is considered a messenger (rasul) for the purpose of requesting a loan, not an agent with authority to collect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the debt is not yet due.
D) Because the principal debtor does not consent.

Key Takeaway
The pillar of agency is working for the principal; when a guarantor collects a debt, he works for himself, invalidating the agency.

Debtor's Verification of an Agent and Subsequent Denial by Principal
Ruling on debtor paying an alleged agent and subsequent denial by principal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the obligations of a debtor who verifies an alleged agent of an absent principal, and determine the debtor's recourse if the principal later denies the agency.

Core Idea
Verification of an alleged agent is an admission binding the debtor, but denial by principal requires repayment.

This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah addresses a common scenario in agency: a person claims to be an agent of an absent creditor. If the debtor verifies this claim, he must pay the agent. However, if the absent principal later denies the agency, the debtor must pay the principal again, and his recourse against the agent depends on whether the payment is still in the agent's possession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Distinguish between the initial scenario (debtor verifies and pays the agent) and the subsequent scenario (principal denies and debtor must pay again).
2
Understand verification as admission
Recognize that the debtor's verification (tasdiq) of the agent's claim is treated as an admission (iqrar) against himself, obligating him to pay the agent.
Even if the debtor later claims to have paid the principal, he is not believed because the admission created a new obligation.
3
Analyze the effect of principal's denial
If the absent principal appears and denies the agency, his denial is believed with his oath, invalidating the first payment. The debtor must then pay the principal again.
The principal's denial is given weight because the debt was originally established, and the debtor claims an incidental discharge.
4
Determine the debtor's recourse against the agent
After paying the principal a second time, the debtor can recover from the agent only what remains in the agent's possession (or its value if consumed). If the payment was lost, the debtor has no recourse against the agent.
The principle: istirdad (recovery) is easier than tadmin (imposing liability); the debtor's earlier admission limits his claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومصدق الوكيل بقبض دينه إن كان غريما أمر بدفع دينه إلى الوكيل) : أي ادعى رجل أنه وكيل الغائب بقبض دينه من الغريم؛ فصدقه الغريم، أمر بتسليم الدين إلى الوكيل ] قوله : أمر...الخ؛ لأن تصديق الغريم إقرار على نفسه؛ لأن ما يدفع خالص حقه، إذ الديون تقضى بأمثالها، ومن أقر على نفسه بشيء أمر بتسليمه إلى المقر له، حتى لو ادعى أنه أوفى الدين إلى الدائن لا يصدق؛ إذ لزمه الدفع إلى الوكيل بإقراره، ولم يثبت الإيفاء بمجرد دعواه، فلا يؤخر حقه. ثم إن كذبه الغائب دفع الغريم إليه ثانيا، ورجع به على الوكيل فيما بقي، وفيما ضاع لا) : أي ثم إن حضر الغائب وكذبه في دعوى الوكالة، دفع الغريم إلى الدائن الدين ثانيا؛ لفساد الأداء؛ لأنه لم يثبت الاستيفاء، حيث أنكر الغائب الوكالة، والقول في إنكار الوكالة قول رب الدين مع يمينه؛ لأن الدين كان ثابتا، والمديون يدعي أمرا عارضا، وهو سقوط الدين بأدائه إلى الوكيل، والموكل ينكر الوكالة، والقول قول المنكر مع يمينه، وإذا لم يثبت الاستيفاء فسد الأداء، وهو واجب على المديون، فيجب الدفع ثانيا. ] قوله : فيما بقي؛ أي إذا كان ما قبضه الوكيل باقيا في يده، ولو كان بقاؤه حكما بأن استهلكه الوكيل، فإنه باق ببقاء بدله؛ ولذا قال في الخلاصة: وإن استهلكه ضمن مثله، فإن ادعى الوكيل هلاكه أو دفعه إلى الموكل حلفه على ذلك، وإن مات الموكل وورثه غريمه، أو وهبه وهو قائم في يد الوكيل أخذ منه في الوجوه كلها، وإن كان هالكا ضمنه، إلا إذا صدقه على الوكالة. كذا في المنح ] قوله : وفيما ضاع لا؛ يعني إن ضاع المال في يد الوكيل لا يرجع المديون على الوكيل. ] قوله : لأنه اعترف ... الخ؛ يعني إذا صدق المديون، فقد اعترف أن الوكيل محق في القبض، والاسترداد أسهل من التضمين، فله ولاية ذلك، لا ولاية هذا.
And the agent is believed regarding collection of his debt if the debtor is a debtor, he is ordered to pay his debt to the agent) : i.e., if a man claims that he is the agent of an absent person to collect his debt from the debtor, and the debtor verifies him, he is ordered to deliver the debt to the agent. [His saying: ordered...] because the debtor's verification is an admission against himself, because what he pays is purely his right, since debts are paid by their equivalents, and whoever admits something against himself is ordered to hand it over to the person in whose favor he admitted, even if he claims that he paid the debt to the creditor, he is not believed, because payment to the agent became obligatory by his admission, and the payment is not proven by mere claim, so his right is not delayed. Then if the absent person denies him, the debtor pays to him a second time, and recovers from the agent what remains, and what is lost no) : i.e., then if the absent person appears and denies his claim of agency, the debtor pays the debt to the creditor a second time, because the payment is void, since collection was not proven, as the absent person denied the agency, and the saying in denying the agency is that of the principal with his oath, because the debt was established, and the debtor claims an incidental matter, which is the discharge of the debt by payment to the agent, and the principal denies the agency, and the saying is that of the denier with his oath, and if collection is not proven, the payment is void, and it is obligatory upon the debtor, so he must pay a second time. [His saying: what remains] i.e., if what the agent collected remains in his hand, even if its remaining is by judgment that the agent consumed it, then it remains by the remaining of its substitute; hence in al-Khulasah: and if he consumed it, he is liable for its equivalent, and if the agent claims its destruction or that he paid it to the principal, he will be made to take an oath on that, and if the principal dies and the debtor inherits from him, or he gives it as a gift while it exists in the hand of the agent, he takes it in all cases, and if it is destroyed, he is liable, unless he verifies him regarding the agency. Thus in al-Minhah. [His saying: and what is lost no] i.e., if the wealth is lost in the hand of the agent, the debtor does not recourse against the agent. [His saying: because he admitted...] i.e., if the debtor verified, he admitted that the agent is correct in collection, and recovery is easier than imposing liability, so he has authority for that, not for this.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary on al-Hidayah, dealing with two scenarios: first, when someone claims to be an agent of an absent principal to collect a debt, and the debtor verifies this claim, the debtor is ordered to pay the agent. The reasoning is that the debtor's verification is an admission against himself (iqrar), so he must hand over the debt. Even if he later claims he paid the principal, he is not believed because the admission obligated payment to the agent. Second, if the absent principal later appears and denies the agency, the debtor must pay the principal again because the first payment was invalid due to lack of proven collection. The principal's denial is believed with his oath because the debt was originally established, and the debtor claims an incidental discharge. After paying the principal, the debtor can recover from the agent what remains in his possession (even if consumed, as the substitute remains), but not if it was lost. The reasoning is that by verifying the agent, the debtor admitted the agent's right to collect, and recovery (istirdad) is easier than imposing liability (tadmin). Key distinctions: the agent's liability only if the property (or its value) remains; loss absolves the agent from the debtor's recourse.

Key Terms
مصدق (musaddiq): The debtor who verifies the claim of the alleged agent; this verification is considered an admission (iqrar) that creates legal obligations.
غريم (gharim): Debtor; the person who owes the debt and is being asked to pay.
استيفاء (istifa'): Collection or receipt of payment; here, the agent's collection from the debtor on behalf of the principal.
استرداد (istirdad): Recovery of property from the agent; the debtor's right to reclaim what he paid to the agent if the principal denies the agency and the property remains.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where the absent principal denies the agency, under what condition can the debtor recover from the agent what he paid?
A) Always, because the agent collected without right.
B) Only if the payment (or its value) remains with the agent.
C) Only if the agent admits the agency was false.
D) Never, because the debtor's verification absolves the agent.

Key Takeaway
A debtor who verifies an alleged agent must pay him, but if the principal denies, the debtor must pay the principal again, and can only recover from the agent what remains in his possession.
