Debtor's Recourse When Principal Denies Agency
Commentary expanding the ruling on debtor's recourse after denial of agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a debtor's recourse when the absent principal denies agency, and distinguish between cases where the agent still holds the payment (or its substitute) versus where it is lost.

Core Idea
A debtor must pay twice if the principal denies agency, but can only recover from the agent what remains, not what is lost.

This passage from a Hanafi commentary clarifies a tricky scenario in debt collection through an agent. When the principal later denies the agent's authority, the debtor faces a dilemma: he must pay the debt again, but can he recover from the agent? The text provides a nuanced answer based on whether the agent still has the money (or its substitute) or it is lost. We'll walk through the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the primary ruling
The key scenario: a debtor pays an agent authorized by the principal to collect a debt. Later, the principal denies agency. The debtor must pay the debt a second time to the principal because the original payment is considered ineffective (the receipt not proven).
This primary ruling is based on the principle that the debtor's purpose—discharge of liability—was not achieved.
2
Recognize the two sub-rulings on recourse
The debtor can recover from the agent what remains in the agent's hand (whether the actual property or its substitute if the agent consumed it). But if the property is lost (e.g., destroyed or stolen without agent's negligence), the debtor cannot recover from the agent.
The commentary clarifies that 'remaining' includes the substitute (like when the agent consumed it and is liable for its equivalent).
3
Understand the rationale for the distinction
For remaining property: the debtor's payment to the agent is rescinded because the purpose failed, so he can recoup what is still in the agent's possession. For lost property: the debtor, by paying the agent, implicitly admitted the agent was right in receiving; thus, after the loss, it would be unjust to make the agent liable—the debtor's admission prevents recourse.
The text states: 'revocation is easier than claiming damages, so he has authority over the former, not the latter.'
4
Note the intra-Hanafi critique
Al-Zayla'i notes that the answer is not fully satisfactory on Abu Hanifah's view. If the agent is considered a mere messenger (rasul), he would not have the capacity to litigate. This suggests a potential inconsistency, but the relied-upon position is as stated.
This critique shows the depth of Hanafi legal discussion.
5
Apply the ruling to a simple example
A owes B 100 dirhams. A pays agent C appointed by B. B denies agency. C still has 30 dirhams. A must pay B 100 again, but can recover 30 from C. If C lost the money (no fault), A cannot recover from C.
This illustrates the practical application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثمَّ إن كذَّبَهُ الغائب دفع الغريم إليه ثانياً، ورجع به على الوكيل فيما بقي، وفيما ضاع لا) ؛ لأنَّ غرضَهُ من دفعِهِ براءة ذمَّتِه ، فإذا لم يحصل غرضُهُ ؛ ينقض الدفع ، أما إذا ضاعَ لا يضمنه ؛ لأَنَّهُ اعترف أنه محق في القبض، والاسترداد أسهل من التضمين ، فله ولاية ذلك، لا ولاية هذا. فصح ما ادعينا أنَّ هذا رسالة معنى، والرسالة بالاستقراض جائزة. هكذا ذكره في «النهاية» معزياً الى «الذخيرة»، وقال الزيلعي ) : هذا سؤال حسن، والجواب غير مُخلص على قول أبي حنيفة له ، فإنه لو كان رسولاً لما كان له أن يخاصم. انتهى. 11 ] قوله : ثم إن كذبه ... الخ ؛ يعني ثم إن حضر الغائب وصدق فيها، وإن كذَّبه دفع الغريم إلى الدائنِ الدِّينَ ثانياً ؛ لفسادِ الأداء ؛ لأنه لم يثبت الاستيفاء، حيث أنكر الغائب الوكالة، والقول في إنكار الوكالة قول ربِّ الدين مع يمينه ؛ لأنَّ الدين كان ثابتاً، والمديون يدعي أمراً عارضاً ، وهو سقوط الدين بأدائه إلى الوكيل ، والموكل ينكر الوكالة، والقول قول المنكر مع يمينه ، وإذا لم يثبت الاستيفاء فسد الأداء، وهو واجب على المديون، فيجب الدفع ثانياً. [٢] قوله : فيما بقي ؛ أي إذا كان ما قبضه الوكيلُ باقياً في يده ، ولو كان بقاؤه حكماً بأن استهلكه الوكيل ، فإنّه باق ببقاء بدله ؛ ولذا قال في «الخلاصة»: وإن استهلكه ضمن مثله ، فإن ادَّعى الوكيل هلاكه أو دفعه إلى الموكل حلفه على ذلك، وإن مات الموكل وورثه غريمه، أو وهبه وهو قائم في يد الوكيل أخذ منه في الوجوه كلّها، وإن كان هالكاً ضمنه ، إلا إذا صدَّقه على الوكالة. كذا في «المنح » (۳) قوله : وفيما ضاع لا ؛ يعني إن ضاع المالُ في يد الوكيل لا يرجع المديون على الوكيل. (٤) قوله : لأنه اعترف ... الخ ؛ يعني إذا صدَّق المديون، فقد اعترف أن الوكيل محق في القبض وهو مظلوم في أخذ ربِّ الدين ثانياً، والمظلوم لا يظلم غيره، فلا يأخذ من الوكيل بعد الهلاك ؛ لأنَّ الوكيل في زعمه محق في قبض الدِّينِ . عنه، وبعدما هلك المدفوع الأخذ منه ظلم، فإن قبل هذا الوجه يقتضي أن لا يرجع عليه إذا كان العين في يدو أيضاً. قلنا : إذا كان العين باقية في يدِهِ أمكن نقض قبضه، فيرجع بنقضه إذا لم يحصل غرضُهُ من التسليم، وأما إذا هلكت فلم يمكن نقضه فلم يرجع عليه، بقي هاهنا شيء وهو أن أحد الابنين إذا صَدَّقَ المديون في دعواه الإيفاء للميت، وكذبه الآخر، ورجع المكذب عليه بالنصف، فإنَّ للمديون الرجوع على المصدق بالنصف إن كان للميت تركة غير الدين، مع أنه في زعمِهِ أن المكذِّب ظالم، وقد يجاب عنه بأنَّ الرجوع على المصدق ؛ لكونه أقر على أبيه بالدين.
Then if the absent one denies, the debtor pays him a second time, and he recovers from the agent for what remains, but not for what is lost. Because his purpose in paying is the discharge of his liability; if his purpose is not achieved, the payment is rescinded. But if it is lost, he does not make him liable because he admitted that the agent was right in receiving, and revocation is easier than claiming damages, so he has authority over the former, not the latter. Thus what we claimed is correct, that this is a message in meaning, and a message by borrowing is permissible. This is mentioned in al-Nihayah, attributed to al-Dhakhirah. Al-Zayla'i said: This is a good question, but the answer is not satisfactory according to Abu Hanifah, because if he were a messenger, he would not have the right to litigate. End. His statement: 'Then if he denies him...' meaning if the absent one appears and confirms, but if he denies, the debtor pays the creditor a second time due to the invalidity of payment because the receipt is not proven since the absent one denied the agency. The ruling in denying agency is the statement of the owner of the debt with his oath because the debt was established, and the debtor claims an intervening matter—the dropping of the debt by payment to the agent—while the principal denies the agency, and the statement of the denier is accepted with his oath. If the receipt is not proven, the payment is invalid, and it is obligatory upon the debtor, so he must pay a second time. His statement: 'for what remains' means if what the agent collected is still in his hand, even if its remaining is [by] ruling, such as if the agent consumed it, then it remains by the remaining of its substitute. Hence it is stated in al-Khulasah: If he consumed it, he is liable for its equivalent. If the agent claims its destruction or delivery to the principal, he is to be believed on oath. If the principal dies and his creditor inherits from him, or he gifts it while it exists in the agent's hand, he takes it from the agent in all cases, but if it is destroyed, he is liable, except if he confirms the agency. Thus in al-Manh. His statement: 'and for what is lost, no' means if the property is lost in the agent's hand, the debtor does not recourse against the agent. His statement: 'because he admitted...' means if the debtor affirms, he has admitted that the agent is right in receiving and he is wronged in taking from the creditor a second time, and the wronged does not wrong others, so he does not take from the agent after destruction because the agent in his view is right in collecting the debt. And after the paid [property] is destroyed, taking from him is wrong. If this face is accepted, it would imply that he should not recourse against him if the property is also in his hand. We say: If the property remains in his hand, it is possible to rescind his receipt, so he recourses by rescission when his purpose of delivery is not achieved. But if it is destroyed, rescission is not possible, so he does not recourse against him. There remains here something: If one of two sons affirms the debtor in his claim of payment to the deceased, and the other denies him, and the denier recourses against him for half, then the debtor has recourse against the affirmer for half if the deceased has estate other than the debt, even though in his view the denier is wrong. It may be answered that the recourse against the affirmer is because he acknowledged a debt against his father.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage from the commentary expands on the ruling stated in the matn. The ruling is: if the absent principal denies the agency, the debtor must pay the creditor a second time. However, the debtor can recover from the agent what the agent still possesses (whether in kind or as a substitute if consumed). But if the property lost in the agent's hand, the debtor cannot recover from the agent because by admitting the agency, the debtor has acknowledged that the agent was rightful in receiving the payment. The commentary provides the reasoning: the debtor's purpose in paying the agent was to free his liability; if the principal denies, that purpose fails, so the payment is rescinded and the debtor must pay again. But the debtor cannot claim from the agent for lost property because that would be wronging the agent who acted in good faith. The text also discusses an edge case where two sons—one affirming, one denying—complicates recourse. The commentary references other authoritative Hanafi works like al-Nihayah, al-Dhakhirah, and al-Khulasah, and notes a critical remark by al-Zayla'i that on Abu Hanifah's view the answer may not be fully satisfactory.

Key Terms
وكيل (wakīl): Agent; the person appointed by the principal to collect the debt on his behalf.
غريم (gharīm): Creditor or debtor depending on context; here it refers to the debtor who pays the debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If an agent collects a debt but the principal denies the agency, and the agent has already consumed the money, can the debtor recover from the agent?
A) Yes, because the agent was not authorized.
B) No, because the debtor admitted the agent was right in receiving.
C) Yes, because the agent consumed it, so it remains by substitute.
D) No, because the debtor should have confirmed the agency first.

Key Takeaway
The debtor must pay again if the principal denies agency, but can only recover from the agent what remains (including substitutes); lost money is not recoverable due to the debtor's admission of the agent's rightfulness.

Exception: Debtor's Recourse When Agent is Made Guarantor at Time of Payment
Explains an exception for recourse when agent is made guarantor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the exceptional condition under which a debtor can recover from an agent, distinguish the two readings of 'damanahu', and explain the legal basis for the recourse.

Core Idea
A debtor can recover from the agent only if the agent explicitly agreed to be a guarantor at the time of payment, not for the trust held initially.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya on Sharh al-Wiqayah discusses an exception in the law of agency. When a debtor pays an agent but fears the principal will deny the agency and demand payment again, he can make the agent a guarantor. The commentator clarifies two possible readings of the key term and chooses the one that makes the agent a surety for the potential second payment, not the first trust. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning behind this exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule and the exception
The general rule is that the agent is not liable for the property received as a trust. The matn states an exception: 'except if he made him a guarantor at the time of payment.' Locate this exception and note that it allows the debtor to recover from the agent.
The general rule is that the agent is an amīn (trustee). The exception creates a special liability.
2
Distinguish the two readings of 'damanahu'
The word 'damanahu' can be read with tashdid (making him a guarantor) or without tashdid (becoming a guarantor). The first reading has the debtor actively making the agent a guarantor; the second has the agent undertaking the guarantee. Both lead to the exception, but the mechanism differs.
The commentator prefers the reading without tashdid, where the agent himself becomes the guarantor.
3
Understand why the first payment is a trust
The first payment received by the agent is a trust (amānah) because both parties agree that he is an agent. Trusts cannot be guaranteed by suretyship (kafālah). Therefore, the guarantee can only apply to a potential future loss.
The trust is established by mutual confirmation of the agency.
4
Explain how the guarantee attached to the time of receipt makes the second payment recoverable
The agent's guarantee is added to the time of receipt of the first payment, but it covers the second payment if the principal denies. This is valid because the guarantee is for a future obligation that arises from the contingency. The debtor can then recover from the agent the amount he paid twice.
The guarantee is conditional on the principal's denial.
5
Appreciate the commentator's choice and its effect
The commentator selects the reading without tashdid, making the agent the active guarantor. This clarifies that the agent's consent is essential. The recourse is based on a valid suretyship contract.
The chosen reading becomes the muftā bihī (operative position).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : إلا إذا كان ... الخ ؛ يعني إلا إذا كان الغريمُ ضَمَّنَ الوكيل عند دفعه، فحينئذ يــرجـع الـغــريـم علـى الوكيل بمثل ما دفعه، فقوله : ضَمِنَه ؛ يجوز أن يكون بالتشديد أو بالتخفيف، فالمعنى بالتشديد إلا إذا كان الغريم جعل الوكيل ضامناً بأن قال عند دفعه : إن حضر الغائب أنكر وكالتك ، وأخذ مني ثانياً، فأنت ضامن بهذا المال ، فقال : أنا ضامن. والمعنى بالتخفيف : إلا إذا كان الوكيلُ صارَ ضامناً بهذا المال، بأن قال : إن حضر الغائب وأنكر التوكيل وأخذ عنه ثانياً، فإنّى ضامن بهذا المال، واختاره الشارح فيصير الوكيل كفيلاً بمال قبضه الدائن المنكر ثانياً ؛ لأنَّ إضافةَ الصَّمَانِ إلى زمانِ القبض جائز لا بمال قبضه الوكيل أولاً ، لأنه أمانةٌ في يده بتصادقهما على أنه وكيل، والأمانات لا تجوز بها الكفالة. كما صرحوا به). ووجه الرجوع : إن المأخوذ ثانياً مضمون على رب الدين في زعم الوكيل والمديون وهذه كفالة أضيفت إلى حالة القبض فتصح واعلم أن ظاهر المتن أنه لا
His statement: except if he made him a guarantor at the time of payment... meaning except if the creditor made the agent a guarantor at the time of payment, then the creditor recourse against the agent for the like of what he paid. His word: 'damanahu' can be with tashdid or without. The meaning with tashdid: except if the creditor made the agent a guarantor by saying at the time of payment: If the absent one comes and denies your agency and takes from me a second time, then you are guarantor for this property, and he said: I am guarantor. The meaning without tashdid: except if the agent became a guarantor for this property by saying: If the absent one comes and denies the appointment and takes from him a second time, then I am guarantor for this property. The commentator chose [the second] so the agent becomes a surety for the property received by the denying creditor secondarily, because the addition of the guarantee to the time of receipt is permissible, not for the property received by the agent first, because it is a trust in his hand by their mutual confirmation that he is an agent, and suretyship is not permitted for trusts. As they have declared. The basis of recourse: the amount taken secondarily is guaranteed against the owner of the debt in the view of the agent and the debtor, and this is a suretyship that is added to the state of receipt, so it is valid. Know that the apparent meaning of the matn is that it is not...
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage deals with an exception to the previous ruling. The matn says "except if he made him a guarantor at the time of payment." The commentator explains that if the debtor, when paying the agent, stipulates that the agent will be liable if the principal denies the agency and the debtor has to pay again, then the debtor can recover from the agent. The word 'damanahu' can be read with tashdid (making him a guarantor) or without (becoming a guarantor). The commentator prefers the latter, making the agent a surety not for the first payment (which was a trust) but for the second payment that the debtor might have to make. This is because the guarantee is attached to the time of receipt, making it valid, whereas the initial receipt was a trust, which cannot be guaranteed.

Key Terms
ضامن (ḍāmin): Guarantor; the person who assumes liability for a debt if the principal fails to pay.
كفالة (kafālah): Suretyship; a contract whereby a person becomes liable for another's obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When can the debtor recover from the agent even if the property is lost?
A) If the agent was negligent.
B) If the debtor made the agent a guarantor at the time of payment.
C) If the principal later confirms the agency.
D) If the agent consumed the property.

Key Takeaway
The debtor can recover from the agent only if the agent explicitly guaranteed at the time of payment, and the guarantee is valid only for potential future loss, not for the trust initially held.

Exception to Agent's Liability: Debtor's Guarantee
Commentary on an exception in agency law.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which a debtor can recover from an agent after the principal denies the agency, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the depositee's duty to hand over to a claimed agent.

Core Idea
An agent is not liable after payment is destroyed unless the debtor made him a guarantor or paid expressly to discharge the debt.

This passage examines an exception to the general rule that an agent collecting a debt is not liable if the creditor later denies the agency and takes payment again. The commentator explains that the debtor can recover from the agent only if the agent was made a guarantor at the time of payment or if the payment was expressly made to discharge the debtor's liability. The passage also addresses a related dispute within the Hanafi school about whether a depositee must hand over a deposit to a person claiming to be an agent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
What is the default ruling regarding the agent's liability after the payment is destroyed?
The matn implies no recovery unless an exception applies.
2
Recognize the exception
What condition must be present for the debtor to recover from the agent?
The key word is 'damina' – guarantee.
3
Parse the term 'damina'
Understand the two possible readings (with/without shadda) and their legal implications.
Both readings lead to the agent being liable as a guarantor.
4
Trace the reasoning
Why does the guarantee make the agent liable? What is the role of the second payment?
The second payment is considered guaranteed; the agent becomes a surety.
5
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
On the related issue of depositees, what is the difference between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf?
The madhhab says depositee is not ordered to hand over; Abu Yusuf says he is.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوكالة باب الوكالة بالخصومة والقبض وعزل الوكيل

إلا إذا كان ضَمِنَهُ عند دفعه

في القبض وهو مظلوم في أخذ ربِّ الدين ثانياً، والمظلوم لا يظلم غيره، فلا يأخذ من الوكيل بعد الهلاك ؛ لأنَّ الوكيل في زعمه محق في قبض الدِّينِ . عنه، وبعدما هلك المدفوع الأخذ منه ظلم، فإن قبل هذا الوجه يقتضي أن لا يرجع عليه إذا كان العين في يدو أيضاً.

قلنا : إذا كان العين باقية في يدِهِ أمكن نقض قبضه، فيرجع بنقضه إذا لم يحصل غرضُهُ من التسليم، وأما إذا هلكت فلم يمكن نقضه فلم يرجع عليه، بقي هاهنا شيء وهو أن أحد الابنين إذا صَدَّقَ المديون في دعواه الإيفاء للميت، وكذبه الآخر، ورجع المكذب عليه بالنصف، فإنَّ للمديون الرجوع على المصدق بالنصف إن كان للميت تركة غير الدين، مع أنه في زعمِهِ أن المكذِّب ظالم، وقد يجاب عنه بأنَّ الرجوع على المصدق ؛ لكونه أقر على أبيه بالدين.

[١] قوله : إلا إذا كان ... الخ ؛ يعني إلا إذا كان الغريمُ ضَمَّنَ الوكيل عند دفعه، فحينئذ يــرجـع الـغــريـم علـى الوكيل بمثل ما دفعه، فقوله : ضَمِنَه ؛ يجوز أن يكون بالتشديد أو بالتخفيف، فالمعنى بالتشديد إلا إذا كان الغريم جعل الوكيل ضامناً بأن قال عند دفعه : إن حضر الغائب أنكر وكالتك ، وأخذ مني ثانياً، فأنت ضامن بهذا المال ، فقال : أنا ضامن. والمعنى بالتخفيف : إلا إذا كان الوكيلُ صارَ ضامناً بهذا المال، بأن قال : إن حضر الغائب وأنكر التوكيل وأخذ عنه ثانياً، فإنّى ضامن بهذا المال، واختاره الشارح فيصير الوكيل كفيلاً بمال قبضه الدائن المنكر ثانياً ؛ لأنَّ إضافةَ الصَّمَانِ إلى زمانِ القبض جائز لا بمال قبضه الوكيل أولاً ، لأنه أمانةٌ في يده بتصادقهما على أنه وكيل، والأمانات لا تجوز بها الكفالة. كما صرحوا به).

زبدة النهاية لعمدة الرعاية

أو دفع إليه على ادعائه غير مصدق وكالته ، وإن كان مودِعاً لم يؤمر بدفعها إليه أو دفع إليه على ادعائه غير مصدق وكالَتَه ، بأن قال الوكيل : إذا حضر الغائب، وأنكر التوكيل، فإنِّي ضامن لهذا المال أو الغريم دفعه بناءً على دعوى الوكيل من غير أن يصدق وكالتَهُ ، ففي هاتين الصورتين إن أنكر الغائبُ فالغريم يضمن الوكيل إن ضاع المال.

( وإن كان مودعاً لم يؤمر بدفعها إليه)

رجوع على الوكيل حالة الهلاك، إلا إذا ضَمِن ، وليس كذلك، بل الحكم كذلك إذا قال : قبضتُ منك على أنّي أبرأتك من الدين. كما في «التنوير»

(1)

وقال في «المنح» (۲) نقلا عن «الخلاصة» و«البزازية»: إلا إذا كان الغريم قال : أخاف إن حضر الدائن أن يكذبك فيها ، وضَمِنَه أو قال مدعي الوكالة : أقبض منك على أني أبرأتك من الدين، كما إذا قال الأب للختنِ عند أخذ صداق بنته : آخذ منك على أني أبرأتك من مهر بنتي ، فإن أخذت البنتُ من الختنِ الصداق، رجع الختنُ على الأب. انتهى.

[1] قوله : أو دفع إليه...الخ ؛ معطوف على قوله : كان ضَمِنَه ؛ يعني إذا كان الغريم لم يصدق وكالته ، ودفع المالَ إليه على ادّعائه للتوكيل، فإن رجع صاحب المال على الغريم رجع الغريم على الوكيل ؛ لأنه لم يصدقه في الوكالة، وإنما دفع المال إليه على احتمال أن يكون وكيلاً ، ولم يرض بقبضه إلا لقضاء دينه تحصيلاً لبراءة ذمته ، فإذا لم يحصل وانقطع الرجاء رجع به عليه.

ولا فرق في ذلك بين أن يكذبه صريحاً أو يسكت ؛ لأنَّ عدم التصديق يشمل الصورتين، وزعمه فيما إذا كذَّبه أنه قبض بغير حق، وإن قبضه يوجب الضمان، وكذا إن لم يصدقه ولم يكذبه ؛ لأنَّ الأصل عدم التصديق.

(٢) قوله : وإن كان ...الخ ؛ أي وإن كان الوكيل قال : إنّي وكيل بقبض الوديعة

فصدقه لم يؤمر المودع بدفع الوديعة إلى ذلك الوكيل.

[٣] قوله : لم يؤمر ؛ ؛ هذا

(۱) تنوير الأبصار» (ص ١٦٠). (۲) (منح الغفار» (ق٢ : ١٥٣/ب).

هو المذهب، وروي عن أبي يوسف : إنه يؤمر بدفع
The Book of Agency. Chapter on Agency in Litigation and Taking Possession and Dismissal of the Agent. Except when he made him a guarantor at the time of payment. Regarding taking possession, and he is wronged in taking the second time from the creditor, and the wronged person does not wrong others, so he does not take from the agent after destruction, because the agent in his opinion is correct in taking the debt. And after the payment is destroyed, taking from him is injustice. If this view is accepted, it entails that he does not recover from him if the property is still in his hand as well. We said: If the property remains in his hand, it is possible to rescind his taking, so he recovers by rescinding it if his purpose of delivery is not achieved. But if it is destroyed, it cannot be rescinded, so he does not recover from him. There remains here something: one of the two sons if he confirms the debtor in his claim of payment to the deceased, and the other denies him, and the one who denies recovers half from the one who confirms, then the debtor can recover the half from the one who confirms if the deceased has an estate other than the debt, although in his opinion the denier is unjust. It may be answered that the recovery from the one who confirms is because he acknowledged a debt against his father. [1] His statement: 'Except when he made him a guarantor...' etc.; meaning except when the debtor made the agent a guarantor at the time of payment, then the debtor recovers from the agent the like of what he paid. So his word 'damina' can be with shadda or without. With shadda means the debtor made the agent a guarantor by saying at payment: 'If the absent one comes and denies your agency and takes from me a second time, you are guarantor for this money,' and he said: 'I am guarantor.' Without shadda means the agent became guarantor for this money by saying: 'If the absent one comes and denies the agency and takes from him a second time, then I am guarantor for this money.' The commentator chose this, so the agent becomes a surety for the money taken by the denying creditor a second time, because adding guarantee to the time of taking is permissible, not for the money taken by the agent first, because it is a trust in his hand due to their mutual agreement that he is an agent, and suretyship for trusts is not permissible, as they stated. And the reason for recovery: The second taken is guaranteed against the creditor in the opinion of the agent and the debtor, and this is a suretyship added to the state of taking, so it is valid. Know that the apparent meaning of the matn is that there is no recovery from the agent in case of destruction, except when he guaranteed, and it is not so; rather the ruling is such when he said: 'I took from you on condition that I discharge you from the debt,' as in 'al-Tanwir'. And he said in 'al-Minhaj' quoting from 'al-Khulasa' and 'al-Bazzaziyya': Except when the debtor said: 'I fear if the creditor comes he may deny you regarding it,' and he made him a guarantor, or the claimant of agency said: 'I take from you on condition that I discharge you from the debt,' as when a father says to the son-in-law when taking his daughter's dowry: 'I take from you on condition that I discharge you from my daughter's dowry.' Then if the daughter takes the dowry from the son-in-law, the son-in-law recovers from the father. End. [1] His statement: 'Or he paid him upon his claim without verifying his agency...' etc. is conjoined with his statement: 'he made him a guarantor'; meaning if the debtor did not verify his agency and paid the money upon his claim of agency, then if the owner of the money recovers from the debtor, the debtor recovers from the agent, because he did not verify him in the agency, and only paid the money on the possibility that he might be an agent, and did not consent to his taking except to pay his debt to obtain discharge of his liability. So when it is not achieved and hope is cut off, he recovers from him. There is no difference in this between whether he explicitly denies him or remains silent, because lack of verification covers both cases. And his opinion when he denies him is that he took without right, and his taking necessitates liability. Similarly if he does not verify him nor deny him, because the default is lack of verification. (2) His statement: 'And if he was a depositee, he was not ordered to hand it over to him...' etc.; meaning if the agent said: 'I am an agent for receiving the deposit,' and he verified him, the depositee is not ordered to hand over the deposit to that agent. [3] His statement: 'he was not ordered'; this is the madhhab, and it is narrated from Abu Yusuf that he is ordered to hand over.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This is a commentary on a ruling from the matn regarding the agent's liability when the principal (the original creditor) denies the agency and collects the debt again from the debtor. The matn states that the debtor cannot recover from the agent after the payment is destroyed, except when the debtor made the agent a guarantor (damina) at the time of payment. The commentator explains that 'damina' can be read with shadda (shaddah) meaning the debtor actively made the agent a guarantor, or without shadda meaning the agent voluntarily became a guarantor. In either case, the debtor can recover from the agent if the principal denies the agency and collects again, because the second payment is considered guaranteed. The commentator reasons that if the property is still in the agent's hand, the debtor can rescind the payment, but if it is destroyed, he cannot. Then there is an illustrative case of two sons where one confirms the debtor's payment and the other denies, and the debtor recovers from the confirming son. This shows that the ruling is based on the principle that one who acknowledges a liability can be held responsible. The text then clarifies that the exception applies only when the debtor either made the agent a guarantor or expressly stated that the payment is on condition of discharge from the debt. Other sources add that if the debtor paid without verifying the agent's claim of agency, he can still recover if the principal denies it. Finally, there is a discussion about a depositee: if an agent claims to be an agent for receiving a deposit, the depositee is not required to hand over even if the agent is truthful, according to the relied-upon position (al-madhhab). Abu Yusuf holds that the depositee is ordered to hand over. This intra-Hanafi dispute is noted.

Key Terms
ضَمِنَهُ (damina): Can mean either: the debtor made the agent a guarantor (with shadda) or the agent became a guarantor (without shadda).
وكيل (wakīl): An agent appointed to collect a debt or perform other tasks on behalf of the principal.
غريم (gharīm): The debtor who pays the debt to the agent.
مذهب (madhhab): The relied-upon position of the school (here, the majority view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What condition is necessary for a debtor to recover from an agent after the principal denies the agency and the payment is destroyed?
A) The debtor must have explicitly denied the agent's authority.
B) The debtor must have made the agent a guarantor or paid on condition of discharge.
C) The agent must have admitted to being a guarantor after the payment.
D) The principal must have acknowledged the agency initially.

Key Takeaway
A debtor can only recover from an agent after the principal's denial if the agent was made a guarantor or the payment was conditional on discharge; otherwise, the agent is not liable for destroyed funds.

Deposit Claims: Inheritance vs. Purchase
Explains two deposit claim scenarios with depositee confirmation and legal reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the inheritance and purchase deposit claim scenarios and apply the ruling based on whether the depositor is deceased.

Core Idea
Depositee's confirmation only triggers handover when both agree the depositor is dead.

This passage presents a pair of contrasting cases regarding a deposit (wadi'ah). The claimant may assert either inheritance or purchase. The depositee's confirmation has different legal effects depending on whether the depositor is alive or dead. Understanding the illa (legal reason) is crucial for correct application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two claims
The claimant makes one of two claims: inheritance (mīrāth) or purchase (shirā').
These are mutually exclusive scenarios.
2
Note the depositee's response
In both cases, the depositee confirms (saddaqa) the claimant.
Confirmation is key; without it, the analysis differs.
3
Observe the ruling difference
For inheritance claim: depositee is ordered to hand over (umira bil-dafʿ). For purchase claim: no such order.
The ruling is opposite despite identical confirmation.
4
Analyse the legal reason (talīl)
The text explains: in inheritance, both agree the depositor died, so ownership transfers. In purchase, the depositor is alive and owns the property, so confirmation cannot override.
The illā is the death or life of the depositor.
5
Apply the principle
A depositee's confirmation is legally effective only when it aligns with a fact that removes the depositor's ownership (death).
This prevents wrongful transfer of a living owner's property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال : تركها المودعُ ميراثاً لي، وصدقه المودع ، أُمِرَ بالدفع إليه، ولو ادعى الشراء منه لم يؤمر (أي إن ادعى أنَّ المودع مات، وترك الوديعة ميراثاً لي وصدَّقَهُ المودع ، أُمِرَ بالدَّفع إليه). (ولو ادعى الشِّراءَ منه لم يؤمر) : أي ادعى أنَّه اشترى من المودع، وصدقه المودع ، لم يؤمر بدفع الوديعة إلى المدعي ; لأنَّ المدعي أقرَّ بملك الغير، والغير أهل للملك ; لأنَّه حي فلا يصدق في دعوى البيع على ذلك الحي، بخلاف مسألة الإرث ; لأنهما اتفقا على موتِ المودع ، فكان هذا اتفاقاً على أنَّه ملك الوارث.
And if he said: 'The depositor left it as inheritance for me,' and the depositee confirmed him, he is ordered to hand it over. And if he claimed purchase from him, he is not ordered. (i.e., if he claimed that the depositor died and left the deposit as inheritance for me, and the depositee confirmed him, he is ordered to hand it over). (And if he claimed purchase from him, he is not ordered): i.e., he claimed that he bought from the depositor, and the depositee confirmed him, he is not ordered to hand the deposit to the claimant; because the claimant affirmed ownership of another, and the other is capable of ownership because he is alive, so he is not believed in the claim of sale on that living person, unlike the case of inheritance; because they agreed on the death of the depositor, so this is an agreement that it is the property of the heir.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage discusses two scenarios regarding a deposit (wadi'ah) when a claimant approaches the depositee. First, if the claimant asserts that the depositor died and the deposit is his inheritance, and the depositee confirms this, the depositee is ordered to hand over the deposit to the claimant. This is because the depositee's confirmation is effectively an admission that the deposit belongs to the heir. Second, if the claimant asserts that he purchased the deposit from the depositor while the depositor is still alive, and the depositee confirms, the depositee is not ordered to hand over. The reason is that the depositee's confirmation would be an admission that the property belongs to someone else (the depositor) who is alive and capable of ownership. The depositee cannot transfer ownership away from a living owner. The key distinction is that in the inheritance case, both parties agree the depositor is dead, so ownership has transferred to the heir. In the purchase case, the depositor is still alive, so the claim cannot override his ownership.

Key Terms
المودع (بكسر الدال) (al-mudi'): The person who deposits something (depositor).
المودع (بفتح الدال) (al-mudda'): The person with whom the deposit is left (depositee).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where a claimant says 'the depositor left the deposit as inheritance for me' and the depositee confirms, what is the ruling?
A) The depositee is not ordered to hand over.
B) The depositee is ordered to hand over.
C) The depositee must wait for the depositor to return.
D) The claim is rejected because it is an admission of another's ownership.

Key Takeaway
Depositee's confirmation only compels handover when both agree the depositor is dead; confirmation cannot transfer ownership of a living owner's property.

Agent Collecting Debt When Debtor Claims Prior Payment
Ruling on agent's collection when debtor claims prior payment; who takes oath.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to STATE the procedure when a debtor claims prior payment to the creditor and IDENTIFY who takes the oath.

Core Idea
The debtor must pay the agent even if he claims prior payment; the oath is on the creditor, not the agent.

A common problem in debt collection: the creditor sends an agent, but the debtor says "I already paid the creditor." Who should the debtor pay? Who must swear? This lesson clarifies the Hanafi procedure, distinguishing between the roles of the creditor and the agent. The ruling protects the agent's authority while ensuring the debtor can later challenge the creditor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actors and their roles
The principal (creditor) appointed an agent (wakil) to collect a debt. The debtor (gharim) owes the debt but claims he already paid the creditor. The agent now demands payment.
The agent is a deputy (na'ib) of the creditor, not a party to the original debt.
2
Determine the debtor's obligation
The debtor must hand over the debt to the agent despite his claim of prior payment. The agent's authority to collect is not voided by the debtor's claim.
This is based on the principle that the agent stands in the place of the creditor for collection.
3
Identify who takes the oath
The debtor can later swear the creditor (make him take an oath) that he did not receive payment. The debtor cannot swear the agent about the agent's knowledge of the creditor's receipt.
The claim is about the creditor's own action, not the agent's. The agent's job is to collect, not to know about prior payments.
4
Apply the same rule to deposits (optional)
The text also notes a narration from Ibn Sama'ah from Imam Muhammad: the same rule applies to a deposit (ayn). If the depositee confirms the agent's authority, he must hand over the item.
This extends the ruling beyond debts to physical items held in trust.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومَن وُكِّلَ بقبض مال، وادَّعى الغريم قبض دائيه ، دفع إليه ، واستحلف دائنَهُ على قبضهِ لا الوكيل على العلم بقبض الموكل الدين. الوديعة، وروى ابن سماعة له عن محمد الله : إنَّ الوكيل يقبض العين إذا صدقه صاحب اليد، يجيبه بالتسليم إليه كالدين. والتفصيل في «شرح الجامع الصغير» لقاضي خان
And whoever is appointed as agent to collect wealth, and the debtor claims payment of his creditor, it is handed to him, and his creditor is sworn regarding his receipt, not the agent regarding knowledge of the principal's receipt of the debt. The deposit, and Ibn Sama'ah narrated from Muhammad (al-Shaybani): the agent collects the specific item (ayn) if the possessor confirms him, he is obliged to hand it over like the debt. Detail is in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir by Qadikhan.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage addresses a situation where a creditor appoints an agent (wakil) to collect a debt from a debtor. If the debtor claims that the creditor himself has already received payment, the agent is still entitled to receive the debt from the debtor. The debtor must hand over the debt to the agent. Later, when the creditor appears and denies receipt, the debtor can swear the creditor (making him take an oath that he did not receive), but the debtor cannot swear the agent about his knowledge of the creditor's receipt. The reason is that the agent is a deputy (na'ib) of the creditor, and the claim of payment is about the creditor's own action, not the agent's. The agent's task is to collect, not to know about prior payments. The text also notes a narration from Ibn Sama'ah from Imam Muhammad that in the case of a deposit (ayn), the same rule applies: the depositee hands over to the agent if he confirms the agency.

Key Terms
الوكيل (al-wakil): The appointed agent.
الغريم (al-gharim): The debtor, the one who owes the debt.
الدائن (al-da'in): The creditor, the one to whom the debt is owed.
العين (al-'ayn): Specific item, a physical asset (as opposed to a debt).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a debtor claims he already paid the creditor, and the creditor's agent comes to collect, what does the debtor do according to the Hanafi school?
A) Refuse payment until the agent swears.
B) Pay the agent and then have the creditor sworn.
C) Pay only if the creditor confirms.
D) Reject the agent's claim.

Key Takeaway
The debtor must pay the agent despite a claim of prior payment; the oath regarding receipt is only on the creditor, not the agent.

Ruling on Agent Spending His Own Money: Istihsan vs Qiyas in Agency
Ruling on agent's own spending offset by sum held, with distinction from debt case and ikhtilaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an agent's own spending offsetting the principal's money, distinguish it from the debt case, and explain the role of istihsan and the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the judge's ruling.

Core Idea
An agent's own spending on the principal's family offsets the money held, not a donation — istihsan overrides qiyas.

In this lesson, we examine a nuanced ruling in Hanafi agency law: when an agent spends his own money on the principal's family instead of the money entrusted to him, is he a donor or is his expenditure offset? The passage presents the ruling and the proof, including the distinction from a debt case and the ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the effect of a judge's ruling. By the end, you will understand how istihsan operates to produce a practical outcome that avoids hardship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
Read the matn: the agent receives money to spend on the family but spends his own. The ruling: 'fahi biha' – it is offset. Grasp the core outcome.
2
Locate the dalil – istihsan vs qiyas
The commentary states that by strict qiyas the agent would be a donor, but istihsan overrides because the agent for expenditure is also an agent for purchase. Understand the reasoning.
Istihsan is a Hanafi source of law that may override qiyas.
3
Analyze the distinction from the debt case
In the debt case, rectification is possible if the creditor refuses to swear; in the family spending case, it is not. This difference justifies the offset ruling.
Focus on the phrase 'التدارك ممكن' vs 'غير ممكن'.
4
Evaluate the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the judge's ruling
Abu Hanifa holds the judge's ruling is effective both externally and internally, so it cannot be undone. The Sahibayn hold it is only external, allowing reversal if error appears. See how this applies to the analogy.
This ikhtilaf is about the rescission of sale applied by analogy to the debt case.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that the agent's spending offsets the money. The commentary notes Abu Yusuf's view as 'al-asahh' (more correct) regarding postponing return until oath.
The tarjih not explicitly resolved; both positions are transmitted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Book of Agency. Chapter on Agency for Litigation and Taking Possession, and Dismissal of the Agent. And whoever gives to another ten [dirhams] to spend on his family, and he spends ten of his own on them, then it is offset by it. And the difference between this issue and the issue of debt: that rectification is possible in the debt issue by recovering what the agent had taken when the error becomes apparent upon the refusal of the creditor to take an oath, but here it is not possible because the judge's ruling of rescission of the sale is valid even if the error becomes apparent according to Abu Hanifa, because the ruling is effective both externally and internally according to him, so the buyer is not required to take an oath after that. But according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), they said: he must return due to the defect as in the debt issue, because rectification is possible according to them due to the nullity of the ruling. And it is said: the more correct view according to Abu Yusuf is that he may postpone the return in both cases until he takes an oath. ... It is said: this is istihsan (equity), and by qiyas (analogy) he becomes a donor by spending what is his property. The istihsan: that the agent for expenditure is an agent for purchase, and the ruling is as we mentioned.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage from the commentary expands on a ruling from the matn of Al-Wiqayah. The ruling states: If a principal gives an agent ten dirhams to spend on his family, and the agent spends ten of his own money on them, then the agent's own spending is offset by the ten he holds from the principal. In other words, the agent is not considered a volunteer donor; his expenditure is treated as if it were the principal's money. The commentator then explains the distinction between this case and a similar debt case. In the debt case, if the agent mistakenly pays a debt he thought he was authorized to pay, and the creditor refuses to swear that no payment was made, the agent can recover the money from the creditor because the judge's ruling can be corrected. However, in the expenditure case, the agent's spending on the family cannot be undone. The commentator notes a difference of opinion: According to Abu Hanifa, the judge's ruling is effective both externally and internally, so once a ruling of rescission is made, it cannot be undone. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), the ruling is only external, so if an error appears, the judge can rescind his ruling. Thus, they say the agent should return the goods if a defect appears, similar to the debt case. There is also a narration from Abu Yusuf that he may postpone the return until the buyer is sworn. The passage then discusses the basis of the ruling: by qiyas, the agent would be a donor, but istihsan prevails because the agent for expenditure is effectively an agent for purchase, so the spending is treated as the principal's transaction.

Key Terms
وكيل (wakil): An agent appointed by the principal (muwakkil) to perform a specific task, here to spend money on the principal's family.
استحسان (istihsan): A juristic preference that overrides a strict analogy (qiyas) due to necessity, equity, or custom. Here, the agent's spending is treated as if it were the principal's money.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning from an established case to a new case. The strict qiyas would make the agent a donor because he spent his own money without explicit order.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A principal gives an agent 10 dirhams to spend on his family. The agent spends 10 dirhams from his own money on the family. According to the Hanafi ruling in the passage, what is the result?
A) The agent is considered a donor and must return the 10 dirhams to the principal.
B) The agent's 10 dirhams are offset by the 10 dirhams he holds; he is not a donor.
C) The agent is entitled to a reward for his spending.
D) The principal must repay the agent with interest.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling uses istihsan to treat an agent's own spending as offset against the principal's money, avoiding the hardship of qiyas that would make him a donor.

The Agent's Spending: Qiyas and Istihsan
Commentary on agent's spending ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between qiyas and istihsan in the context of an agent's spending and identify the ruling when an agent uses his own money.

Core Idea
Qiyas says the agent is a volunteer; istihsan says he is also an agent for purchase.

This passage explores a key dispute in Hanafi fiqh regarding the authority of an agent (wakil) who is authorized to spend. The commentary contrasts the ruling by qiyas (analogy) and by istihsan (juristic preference), and clarifies the scope of the agent's authority. Understanding this distinction is vital for correctly applying agency law in everyday transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conflicting rulings
Read the passage and note the two outcomes: by qiyas the agent becomes a volunteer (mutabarri'), and by istihsan the agent is also authorized to purchase.
The istihsan is the preferred view (mufta bihi).
2
Understand the basis of qiyas
Qiyas compares the agent spending his own money to a stranger doing a favour without command; hence he cannot claim reimbursement.
The agent's own money is not the principal's property.
3
Understand the basis of istihsan
Istihsan looks at the purpose of the agency: spending implies purchase, so the agent is implicitly authorized to buy.
This is a concession (rukhsa) based on custom.
4
Apply the ruling to the agent's liability
If the agent spends his own money, he must return the dirhams he took from the principal. If he consumed them, he is liable.
Dirhams are specified in agency; if they perish before spending, the agency becomes void.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل : هذا استحسان ، وفي القياس يصير متبرعاً بإنفاق ما هو ملكه ، الاستحسان : أنَّ الوكيل بالإنفاق، وكيل بالشّراء، والحكم فيه ما ذكرنا . [١] قوله : قيل : هذا ... الخ ؛ وقيل : القياس والاستحسان في قضاء الدين ؛ لأنه ليس بشراء، وأما الإنفاق يتضمن الشراء، فلا يدخلانه. كذا في «الهداية). [٢] قوله : يصير متبرعاً ...الخ ؛ أي فيما أنفق من ماله، ويردُّ الدرهم المأخوذة من الموكل عليه، وإن استهلكها ضمن ؛ لأنَّ الدَّراهم تتعيَّن في الوكالات، حتى لو هلكت قبل الإنفاق بطلت الوكالة، فإذا أنفق من مال نفسه فقد أنفق بغير أمره ، فيكون متبرعاً.
It was said: This is istihsan, and in qiyas he becomes a volunteer by spending what is his property. The istihsan: that the agent for spending is an agent for purchase, and the ruling therein is what we mentioned. [1] His statement: 'It was said: This... etc.' It was also said: Qiyas and istihsan apply to repayment of debt because it is not purchase. As for spending, it includes purchase, so they do not enter it. Thus in al-Hidayah. [2] His statement: 'He becomes a volunteer... etc.': i.e., regarding what he spent from his money, and he returns the dirham taken from the principal, and if he consumes it he is liable, because dirhams are specified in agencies. Even if they perished before spending, the agency is void. So if he spends from his own wealth, he spent without his command, thus he is a volunteer.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a previous matn concerning the agent's authority to spend. It first presents a qiyas (analogy): if the agent spends his own money instead of the principal's, he becomes a volunteer (mutabarri'), meaning he cannot claim reimbursement. Then it presents the istihsan (preferred legal reasoning): an agent authorized to spend is also authorized to purchase, because spending money typically involves purchasing something. The commentator clarifies from al-Hidayah that the qiyas and istihsan are specifically about repaying debts (qada' al-dayn), not about direct purchases. The second part explains the practical ruling: if the agent uses his own money, he must return the dirhams he took from the principal. If he consumed those dirhams, he is liable to replace them. This is because dirhams are considered specific (ta'ayyun) in agency contracts; if they are destroyed before spending, the agency becomes void. By spending his own money without authorization, the agent has acted without command and is thus a volunteer.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): A method of legal reasoning that prefers a different ruling from qiyas due to a stronger evidence or need.
متبرع (mutabarri'): A person who performs an act voluntarily without expectation of compensation or reimbursement.
تتعين الدراهم (tata'ayyan al-darahim): The dirhams given to the agent become specified (not fungible) in the agency; if lost, the agent may be liable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the istihsan mentioned in the text, what is the ruling on an agent who is authorized to spend?
A) He is only authorized to spend, not to purchase.
B) He is also authorized to purchase because spending implies purchase.
C) He becomes a volunteer if he spends his own money.
D) He must always use the principal's money.

Key Takeaway
The agent who spends his own money becomes a volunteer by qiyas, but by istihsan he is also authorized for purchase; the mufta bihi is the istihsan view.

Dismissal of the Agent: Knowledge as a Condition
Matn and commentary on dismissal of agent contingent on knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that the dismissal of an agent requires the agent's knowledge and contrast this with the Shafi'i position, identifying the practical consequences.

Core Idea
Dismissal of an agent is not effective until the agent knows—harm prevention and the nature of legal address require it.

This passage tackles a practical and principled question: when a principal fires his agent, does the dismissal take effect immediately or only when the agent finds out? The answer determines whether the agent's subsequent actions bind the principal. The Hanafi school argues that knowledge is essential to avoid harming the agent and because a legal command cannot bind without awareness. Other schools, notably Shafi'i, disagree. Let's unpack the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position clearly
Identify the two parts of the ruling: the principal may dismiss, but dismissal is contingent on the agent's knowledge. Find the Arabic phrase 'ووقف على علمه'.
This is the core rule.
2
Identify the opposing view and its proof
The text mentions Shafi'i (and others) who say no knowledge needed. Note their analogy to divorce and manumission, which are effective without the other party's knowledge.
The opponent's proof is qiyas to unilateral repudiation.
3
Analyse the Hanafi response
The text gives two arguments: (1) harm to the agent (idrar) and (2) the nature of khitab (address) requiring knowledge, analogised to abrogation of divine law.
The Hanafi response distinguishes dismissal from divorce based on potential harm.
4
Note the exception to the principal's right to dismiss
The text notes that when the agent is appointed at the request of an absent plaintiff, the principal cannot dismiss, because it would harm the plaintiff's right.
This is an additional layer: the right to dismiss is not absolute.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
If a principal dismisses without telling the agent, and the agent continues to act, under Hanafi law the agent's actions are valid (he is not liable), while under the opposing view the agent acts without authority and is liable.
Liability turns on knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب عزل الوكيل للموكل عزل وكيله، ووقف على علمه [١] قوله : باب عزل الوكيل؛ أخَّرَ العزل عن الوكالة؛ لأنه يقتضي سبق ثبوتها، وهو رافعها فناسب ذكره آخراً. [٢] قوله : للموكل عزل وكيله متى شاء؛ لأنَّ الوكالة منعقدة، وله أن يسقطه إلا إذا كان الوكيل وكيلاً بالخصومة بالتماس الطالب، فكان الطالب غائباً لما في عزله حينئذ من إبطال حق الطالب، وصارت هذه الوكالة كالوكالة المشروطة في عقد الرهن، من حيث أنَّ الراهن لا يملك عزل الوكيل لما فيه من إبطال حق المرتهن. بخلاف ما لو كان الطالب حاضراً، فإنَّ حقه لا يبطل؛ لأنه إن لم يمكنه طلبه مع الوكيل أمكنه مع الموكل، وبخلاف ما لو كان التوكيل بغير التماس الطالب؛ لأنه رضي ببطلان حقه، حيث لم يلتمس وكيلاً مع علمِهِ أنَّ الموكَّلَ رَبِّما يغيب فلا يمكنه الخصومة معه. [٣] قوله : ووقف على علمه؛ أي توقف انعزال الوكيل على علمه، وقال الشافعي له في الأصح، ومالك في رواية، وأحمد له في رواية: لا يتوقف عزلُ الوكيل على علمه؛ لأنَّ الموكل بعزل الوكيل مسقط لحق نفسه؛ لأنَّ الوكالة حق الموكل، والمرء ينفرد بإسقاط حق نفسه، وصار كالطلاق والعتاق، فإنّه يجوزُ بدونِ علمِ المرأة والعبد. ولنا: إنَّ في عزله بدون علمه إضرار به; لأنه رُبَّما يتصرف بناء على أنه وكيل، وينقد الثمن من مال الموكل، أو يُسَلّم المبيع فيضمنه; ولأنَّ العزل خطاب ملزم للوكيل التصرف، وحكم الخطاب لا يثبتُ في حق المخاطب ما لم يعلم به. كخطاب الشرع، فإنّه إذا نسخ لا يثبت حكمُ النَّسخ حتى يبلغ المكلف، ويستوي في ذلك الوكيل بالنكاح وغيره، وينعزل الرسولُ قبل علمه بالعزل؛ لأنه مبلغ عبارة المرسل، وناقل لها، فيكون عزله رجوعاً لمرسله عن الإيجاب.
Chapter on Dismissal of the Agent. It is permissible for the principal to dismiss his agent, and it is contingent on his knowledge. [1] His statement: 'Chapter on Dismissal of the Agent.' He postponed dismissal after agency because it requires prior establishment of agency, and it is its remover, so it is appropriate to mention it last. [2] His statement: 'It is permissible for the principal to dismiss his agent whenever he wishes' because the agency is valid, and he has the right to revoke it, except if the agent is an agent for litigation at the request of the plaintiff and the plaintiff is absent, because his dismissal then would nullify the right of the plaintiff. This agency becomes like an agency stipulated in a mortgage contract, in that the mortgagor cannot dismiss the agent because it would nullify the right of the mortgagee. Unlike if the plaintiff is present, for his right is not nullified; because if he cannot sue through the agent, he can sue the principal directly. And unlike if the agency was made without the plaintiff's request, because he consented to the nullification of his right since he did not request an agent knowing that the principal might be absent and he cannot litigate with him. [3] His statement: 'and it is contingent on his knowledge' meaning the dismissal of the agent is contingent on his knowledge. Al-Shafi'i said in the most correct view, Malik in one narration, and Ahmad in one narration: the dismissal of the agent is not contingent on his knowledge, because the principal by dismissing the agent is dropping his own right; agency is the principal's right, and a person is free to drop his own right. It is like divorce and manumission, which are valid without the knowledge of the woman or the slave. Our evidence: In his dismissal without knowledge there is harm to him; because he might act based on being an agent, spend the principal's money, or deliver the sold item, and thus be liable. Also, dismissal is an address (khitab) binding the agent to refrain from acting, and the ruling of an address is not established for the addressee unless he knows of it, like the address of the Lawgiver: when abrogated, the ruling of abrogation takes effect only after notification to the responsible person. This applies equally to the agent for marriage and others. The messenger (rasul) is dismissed before his knowledge of the dismissal because he is merely a conveyer of the sender's words and a transmitter; his dismissal is the sender's retraction of the offer.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is the chapter on dismissal of the agent (wakil), combining the matn and its commentary. The matn states two rules: (1) the principal may dismiss his agent, and (2) the dismissal is contingent on the agent's knowledge. The commentary explains why this chapter is placed after the chapter on agency: dismissal presupposes agency and is its termination. The first part of the commentary elaborates on the exception to the principal's right to dismiss: when the agent is appointed for litigation at the request of a plaintiff who is absent, the principal cannot dismiss the agent because doing so would harm the plaintiff's right to sue. This is likened to an agent in a mortgage contract, where the mortgagor cannot dismiss the agent because it would harm the mortgagee's right. If the plaintiff is present, the principal can dismiss because the plaintiff can still sue the principal directly. If the agency was not at the plaintiff's request, the plaintiff is deemed to have accepted the risk of dismissal. The second part addresses the dispute: Shafi'i, Malik (in one narration), and Ahmad (in one narration) hold that dismissal is effective without the agent's knowledge, analogizing it to divorce and manumission where the right is dropped unilaterally. The Hanafi response gives two arguments: (1) harm to the agent, who may act in good faith and incur liability; (2) the legal nature of dismissal as an address (khitab) requires knowledge for its effect, similar to how abrogation of divine commands takes effect only after notification. An exception is noted for a messenger (rasul) who conveys a specific offer; his dismissal is effective before knowledge because he is merely a transmitter and the dismissal is the principal's retraction of the offer.

Key Terms
عزل ('azl): The act of removing an agent from his agency by the principal.
توقف على علمه (tawaqquf 'ala 'ilmihi): The legal effectiveness of dismissal depends on the agent's knowledge of it.
بالتماس الطالب (bi-al-timās al-ṭālib): At the request of the plaintiff (in litigation agency).
إضرار (iḍrār): Harm; here, the harm caused to the agent if he acts without knowledge of dismissal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in the text, what is the effect of dismissing an agent without his knowledge?
A) The dismissal is effective immediately and the agent is liable for any actions.
B) The dismissal is not effective until the agent learns of it.
C) The dismissal is effective only if the agent agrees.
D) The dismissal is never effective once the agent has acted.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the dismissal of an agent takes effect only when the agent knows of it, because of the potential harm to the agent and the principle that legal address requires knowledge.

Dismissal of the Agent: Does Knowledge Matter?
Ruling on dismissal of agent with condition of knowledge and inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the timing of an agent's dismissal, explain the opposing view, and evaluate the Hanafi proof based on analogy to legal abrogation.

Core Idea
Dismissal of an agent only takes effect upon the agent's knowledge—a legal address binds only when conveyed.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah addresses a key dispute in agency law: when does the principal's dismissal of an agent become effective? The Hanafi school requires the agent's knowledge, while other schools hold it is immediate. Understanding this difference is crucial for determining the legal consequences of the agent's actions after dismissal.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: the principal may dismiss his agent, but the dismissal is contingent upon the agent's knowledge. Until the agent learns of the dismissal, he remains an agent.
The word ‘وقف’ (contingent) is key.
2
Identify the opposing view
Note that the Shafi'i school (most correct view), Maliki (one narration), and Hanbali (one narration) hold that knowledge is not required. They compare dismissal to divorce and manumission, which take effect without the knowledge of the spouse or slave.
Opposition is not unanimous within each school but a valid ikhtilaf.
3
Analyze the opposing proof
Examine the opponent's analogy: agency is the principal's right, and he may waive it unilaterally, just as one may divorce or free a slave without informing the other party. This analogy suggests that knowledge is irrelevant to validity.
Opponent's dalil is qiyas to known unilateral waivers.
4
Examine the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafi response has two parts: (1) Dismissal without knowledge harms the agent because he may continue acting and become liable. (2) A legal address (khiṭāb) only binds the addressee after it is conveyed, analogous to the abrogation of a Shariah ruling which requires the mukallaf's knowledge.
The qiyas to shariah abrogation is a powerful argument in Hanafi usul.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: Under the Hanafi view, any actions taken by the agent before knowledge are valid and bind the principal. Under the opposing view, such actions are invalid, and the principal bears no liability.
This difference affects risk allocation in agency transactions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب عزل الوكيل
للموكل عزل وكيله، ووقف على علمه

[1] قوله : باب عزل الوكيل ؛ أخَّرَ العزل عن الوكالة ؛ لأنه يقتضي سبق ثبوتها، وهو رافعها فناسب ذكره آخراً.
[2] قوله : للموكل عزل وكيله متى شاء ؛ لأنَّ الوكالة منعقدة، وله أن يسقطه إلا إذا كان الوكيل وكيلاً بالخصومة بالتماس الطالب، فكان الطالب غائباً لما في عزله حينئذ من إبطال حق الطالب، وصارت هذه الوكالة كالوكالة المشروطة في عقد الرهن، من حيث أنَّ الراهن لا يملك عزل الوكيل لما فيه من إبطال حق المرتهن.
بخلاف ما لو كان الطالب حاضراً، فإنَّ حقه لا يبطل ؛ لأنه إن لم يمكنه طلبه مع الوكيل أمكنه مع الموكل ، وبخلاف ما لو كان التوكيل بغير التماس الطالب ؛ لأنه رضي ببطلان حقه، حيث لم يلتمس وكيلاً مع علمِهِ أنَّ الموكَّلَ رَبِّما يغيب فلا يمكنه الخصومة معه.
[3] قوله : ووقف على علمه ؛ أي توقف انعزال الوكيل على علمه، وقال الشافعي له في الأصح ، ومالك في رواية ، وأحمد له في رواية : لا يتوقف عزلُ الوكيل على علمه ؛ لأنَّ الموكل بعزل الوكيل مسقط لحق نفسه ؛ لأنَّ الوكالة حق الموكل، والمرء ينفرد بإسقاط حق نفسه، وصار كالطلاق والعتاق، فإنّه يجوزُ بدونِ علمِ المرأة والعبد.
ولنا : إنَّ في عزله بدون علمه إضرار به؛ لأنه رُبَّما يتصرف بناء على أنه وكيل، وينقد الثمن من مال الموكل ، أو يُسَلّم المبيع فيضمنه ؛ ولأنَّ العزل خطاب ملزم للوكيل التصرف، وحكم الخطاب لا يثبتُ في حق المخاطب ما لم يعلم به.
كخطاب الشرع، فإنّه إذا نسخ لا يثبت حكمُ النَّسخ حتى يبلغ المكلف، ويستوي في ذلك الوكيل بالنكاح وغيره، وينعزل الرسولُ قبل علمه بالعزل ؛ لأنه مبلغ عبارة المرسل، وناقل لها، فيكون عزله رجوعاً لمرسله عن الإيجاب.
Chapter on dismissal of the agent. The principal may dismiss his agent, and it is contingent upon his knowledge. He said: Chapter on dismissal of the agent – he delayed dismissal after agency because it necessitates prior establishment of agency, and it is its revoker, so it was appropriate to mention it last. He said: The principal may dismiss his agent whenever he wishes, because agency is a contract that he may revoke, except if the agent is an agent for litigation at the request of the plaintiff and the plaintiff is absent, because dismissing him then would nullify the plaintiff's right, and this agency becomes like the agency stipulated in the mortgage contract, in that the mortgagor cannot dismiss the agent due to nullifying the mortgagee's right. Unlike if the plaintiff is present, for his right is not nullified because if he cannot sue the agent, he can sue the principal. And unlike if the agency was not at the request of the plaintiff, for he consented to the nullification of his right by not requesting an agent knowing that the principal might become absent and he cannot litigate with him. He said: And contingent upon his knowledge; meaning the dismissal of the agent is contingent upon his knowledge. Al-Shafi'i in the most correct view, Malik in one narration, and Ahmad in one narration said: The dismissal of the agent does not depend on his knowledge, because the principal, by dismissing the agent, is waiving his own right, for agency is the principal's right, and a person alone may waive his own right, and it is like divorce and manumission, which are valid without the knowledge of the woman or slave. And we (Hanafis) argue: Dismissing him without his knowledge causes him harm, because he might act based on being an agent, pay the price from the principal's wealth, or deliver the sold item and thus become liable. Also, dismissal is an address obliging the agent to refrain from acting, and the effect of an address does not be established on the addressee until he knows of it. Like the address of the Law, for if it is abrogated, the effect of abrogation is not established until the obligated person is informed. The same applies to the agent for marriage and other matters. And the messenger is dismissed before his knowledge of the dismissal, because he is a conveyor of the sender's statement, so his dismissal is the sender's revocation of the offer.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the chapter of dismissal of the agent from Al-Wiqayah. The author begins by explaining why the chapter on dismissal comes after the chapter on agency, because dismissal presupposes agency and revokes it, so it is natural to mention it after. The principal has the right to dismiss his agent at any time, since agency is a revocable contract. However, an exception is made when the agent was appointed for litigation at the request of a plaintiff who is absent; dismissing the agent in that case would harm the plaintiff's right, so it is not permitted. This is analogous to the agency stipulated in a mortgage contract, where the mortgagor cannot dismiss the agent because it would harm the mortgagee's right. If the plaintiff is present, his right is not harmed because he can still sue the principal. If the agency was not at the plaintiff's request, he is deemed to have consented to the risk of dismissal. The second part discusses whether dismissal takes effect without the agent's knowledge. The Hanafi school requires the agent's knowledge for the dismissal to be effective, whereas the Shafi'i, Maliki (in one narration), and Hanbali (in one narration) schools hold that knowledge is not required, analogizing to divorce and manumission which take effect without the knowledge of the wife or slave. The Hanafi proof is twofold: (1) Dismissal without knowledge harms the agent, who may continue acting and cause losses for which he becomes liable. (2) A legal address only binds the addressee upon conveyance, just as abrogation of a legal ruling only applies after it reaches the mukallaf. An exception is made for a messenger (rasul), who is dismissed without knowledge because he merely conveys the principal's words.

Key Terms
عزل ('azl): Dismissal of an agent by the principal, revoking the agency agreement.
وكيل (wakīl): A person appointed by another (principal) to act on his behalf in a specific matter, such as litigation, sale, or marriage.
موكل (muwakkil): The principal who appoints an agent (wakil) to act on his behalf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does the dismissal of an agent take effect?
A) Immediately upon the principal's pronouncement.
B) Only after the agent acknowledges receipt.
C) Upon the agent's knowledge of the dismissal.
D) Only if the agent agrees to the dismissal.

Key Takeaway
Dismissal of an agent is effective only when the agent learns of it, because a legal address binds only upon conveyance and to prevent harm to the agent.
