وبدون القضاء.
وكذا لو كفل رجل لرجل بما يقر له به فلان، فادَّعى المكفول له على فلان ديناً ، فاستحلفه فنكل لا يجب على الكفيل شيء، ولو كان إقراراً لوجب عليه. وكذا لو اشترى نصف عبد ثم اشترى نصفه الباقي، فوجد به عيباً فخاصمه في النصف الأول، فاستحلفه فنكل قضى عليه بالرد.
ثم أراد أن يرد النصف الآخر يحتاجُ إلى خصومة واستحلاف جديد إذا أنكر، ولو كان إقراراً لما استحلف ثانياً ، بل كان يلزمُهُ كلُّهُ بالتَّكول الأول، وهذا لأنَّ اليمين تجب عليه إذا طلب تحليفه لتنتهي به الخصومة ومع البذل لا خصومة ولا طلب، فلا يجب.
And he (the defendant) is only made to take an oath according to the two companions because nukul (refusal to swear) is an iqrar (confession); because the oath is obligatory upon him on the premise of his truthfulness in his denial. So if he refrains, it is known that he is not truthful in his denial, because if he were truthful he would have proceeded to perform the obligatory, which is the oath. And since nukul is a confession, and confession is applicable in these matters, the judge has him swear until if he refuses once, judgment is given based on nukul. And according to them also: its clarification: The confession of a man is valid for five: parents, child, wife, and master (mawla); because it is a confession of what is due from him, and there is no imposition of lineage upon others; and his confession is not valid for others. The confession of a woman is valid for four: parents, husband, and master; and it is not valid for child, and others besides these; because in it is imposition of lineage upon others, unless the husband confirms her confession of the child or a midwife testifies to the birth of the child. It was explicitly stated by al-Tahtawi and others. And wala' (patronage) whether wala' of emancipation or wala' of clientage: if a man claims against another known man that he is his client, or the known man claims that and the other denies, then according to the Imam (Abu Hanifa) he does not swear in these matters, but according to the two companions he swears. [1] His statement: because nukul... etc.; its detail: The benefit of the oath is the appearance of the right through nukul, and nukul is a confession; because the oath is obligatory, and leaving it indicates that he is either a surrenderer (badhil) or a confessor (muqirr). And it cannot be considered surrender because it is permissible from one from whom surrender is not permissible, like a mukatab (slave under contract) and a slave permitted to trade. Similarly, it is valid in debt, but its surrender is not valid. It is obligatory upon the judge to judge based on nukul, and its imposition in the dhimma (liability) initially is valid. If it were a surrender, it would not be valid, nor would it be obligatory. Likewise, judgment must be made by it in matters less than life (short of homicide). So it is determined that he is a confessor. And confession applies in these things, but it is a confession with a semblance (shubha) of surrender, so by it nothing that is dropped by doubts (shubuhat) is established, like hudud and li'an. Do you not see that they are not established by testimony upon testimony, nor by the letter of a judge to a judge, nor by the testimony of women with men, because of the doubts therein. This is because his nukul indicates that he is lying in his denial; otherwise he would not have spoken; because a truthful oath contains the reward of remembering Allah with glorification, and the protection of his wealth and honor by repelling the accusation of lying from himself, and the rational person inclines to such things. And a false oath contains the destruction of the soul. And for Abu Hanifa proof: Indeed, a person often avoids a truthful oath, and gives something (i.e., surrenders) and does not swear. And if it is possible to interpret his action as surrender, confession is not established due to doubt, so it is interpreted as surrender. And surrender does not apply in these matters. So it is apparent that he avoided it out of fear of destruction and opposition to his desire, and the stinginess of his soul, and choosing to return to the truth, since that is better than persisting in falsehood, as Allah said: "And whoever is protected from the stinginess of his soul, it is those who are successful" (59:9). Therefore inevitably it is a confession, and confession applies in these matters. And Allah knows what is in the breasts. [1] His statement: And for Abu Hanifa... etc.; its exposition: Indeed, nukul is badhl (surrender) and ibaha (permission), i.e., cutting off the dispute by paying what the opponent claims; because the oath does not remain obligatory with nukul. And that which is so is either surrender or confession, but its being surrender is preferable to avoid his becoming a liar in the earlier denial. And these rights (claims of nasab, wala', etc.) do not admit surrender and permission. Do you not see that if a man said: "I am free, and I surrender myself to this (person) to cover me" or said: "I am the son of so-and-so and I permit this (person) to claim my lineage," or a woman said: "I am not a wife to this (man) and I permit myself to him," it is not valid to give judgment against him (the one who surrenders) based on nukul, like qisas in homicide, hudud and li'an. For this reason, it is valid only in the court of the judge and by his decree. If it were a confession, it would be valid absolutely and without decree. Likewise, if a man guaranteed for a man for what so-and-so would confess to him (i.e., the guarantee covered a future confession), and the guaranteed one claimed a debt against so-and-so and asked him to swear, and he refused (nakala), nothing becomes obligatory on the guarantor. If it were a confession, it would be obligatory on him. Likewise, if someone bought half a slave then bought the other half, and found a defect in him and disputed with the seller regarding the first half, and asked him to swear and he refused, judgment is given against him (the seller) that he must accept the return. Then he wanted to return the other half, he needs a new dispute and requesting an oath if the seller denies. If it were a confession, he would not need a second oath; rather, the entire (sale) would be binding on him by the first nukul. This is because the oath becomes obligatory on him only when asked to swear in order to conclude the dispute, and with surrender there is no dispute and no request, so it is not obligatory.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the legal nature of nukul (refusal to take an oath) in judicial proceedings. The issue is: when a defendant is asked to take an oath in a claim for rights such as lineage (nasab), patronage (wala'), or marriage, and he refuses, what is the effect? The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that nukul is considered an iqrar (confession). Their reasoning: the oath is obligatory on the defendant if he is truthful in his denial; his avoidance of the oath shows he is lying, thus his refusal is a confession to the claim. Since confession is valid in these matters, the judge may ask him to swear once, and if he refuses, judgment is given against him. They further explain that a man's confession is valid for five categories: parents, child, wife, and master, because it is a confession of what is due from him without imposing lineage on others. A woman's confession is valid for four: parents, husband, and master, but not for child due to imposing lineage, unless the husband confirms or a midwife testifies. For claims of wala', the same rule applies: the defendant is made to swear according to them, but not according to Imam Abu Hanifa. The margin commentary expands: if nukul were a surrender (badhl), it would be valid from persons like a mukatab (slave with contract) in matters of debt, but not in claims of lineage, wala', etc. Since the oath is obligatory, its omission indicates either surrender or confession. It cannot be surrender because surrender is not applicable in these matters (nasab, wala', nafaqah, etc.)—nor can surrender be imposed by the judge. Therefore, it must be confession, which can be the basis for judgment. However, due to a semblance of surrender, it does not establish matters that are dropped by doubts, like hudud and li'an. The text gives examples: hudud are not established by testimony upon testimony, letter of judges, or mixed gender testimony, because of doubts. Similarly, nukul has a doubt, so it does not apply to such severe penalties.

Imam Abu Hanifa's proof: a person often avoids even a truthful oath due to various reasons (fear, stinginess, preference for truth), so it is possible that his nukul is not a confession but a surrender (badhl) or permission (ibaha) to the claim. Since both interpretations are possible, it is more appropriate to interpret it as surrender to avoid the harm of being considered a liar in his original denial. Surrender is not permissible in claims of lineage, wala', etc., so judgment cannot be given based on nukul. He argues that the defendant may have avoided the oath out of fear of the consequences of a false oath or out of a desire to return to the truth. Thus nukul is not necessarily a confession. The margin commentary further elaborates: nukul is badhl and ibaha—cutting off the dispute by giving the opponent what he claims. Since the oath is no longer obligatory once he refuses, it can only be either surrender or confession. Making it surrender is preferable because it avoids labeling the defendant a liar. Surrender does not apply to these claims (nasab, wala', qisas, etc.), so nukul cannot be valid for them. Examples illustrate: if a man says "I am free and I surrender myself to you to cover me" or "I am his son and permit him to claim my lineage," the judge cannot give effect to such a surrender. Similarly, qisas, hudud, and li'an are not affected. If it were confession, it would be valid absolutely without needing the judge's court. Also, if a guarantor's liability depended on the debtor's confession, and the debtor refused to swear, the guarantor is not liable because nukul is not confession. And in a case of selling half a slave, if the seller refuses to swear about the defect in the first half, judgment is only for that half; the other half requires a separate oath. If nukul were confession, the first refusal would bind the whole. This shows nukul is not a universal confession.

In practice, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) differs among scholars of the madhhab. However, later Hanafi tradition often adopts the view of the Sahibayn for the sake of justice. The passage shows the depth of Hanafi legal reasoning using rational arguments (qiyas, istihsan) and illustrative hypotheticals.

Key Terms
نكول (nukūl): Refusal of the defendant to take the required oath in court after being asked by the judge.
إقرار (iqrār): Legal confession by a party to a claim against him, establishing liability or fact without further proof.
بذل (badhl): Surrender or payment of the claimed right voluntarily, cutting off the dispute without acknowledging liability.
إباحة (ibāḥa): Permission given by the defendant to the plaintiff to take the claimed right, equivalent to surrender.
ولاء (walāʾ): Patronage relationship arising from emancipation (walāʾ al-ʿitāqa) or clientage contract (walāʾ al-muwālāt).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, why is nukul considered a surrender (badhl) rather than a confession in claims of lineage and patronage?
A) Because the oath is not obligatory in such claims.
B) Because people often avoid even truthful oaths, so refusal may be due to fear or stinginess, not falsehood.
C) Because confession is never valid in lineage matters.
D) Because the judge has no authority to administer oaths in these cases.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has an internal dispute on nukul: the Sahibayn treat it as confession (iqrar) enabling judgment, while Abu Hanifa treats it as surrender (badhl/ibaha), which is invalid for claims of lineage, patronage, and marriage, and thus has no effect.

Stranger's Surety: Limited to the Court Session
Ruling on surety duration for a stranger claimant with commentary expansion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that a stranger claimant's surety is limited to the court session and explain why this leniency is granted.

Core Idea
A stranger claimant's surety lasts only as long as the court session — no more.

This lesson unpacks a specific ruling from the Hanafi fiqh of kafalah (surety). When a claimant is a stranger (non-resident), the duration of the defendant's obligation to provide surety or allow the claimant to follow him is strictly limited to the court session. The commentary explains the rationale, the judge's role, and the exceptions. By the end, you will understand the balance between the claimant's right to pursue his claim and the defendant's right not to face undue hardship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
First, note the general rule: if a defendant refuses to give a surety, the claimant must follow him for three days. Then, the special rule for a stranger claimant: the following is only for the court session. Recognize that 'stranger' (gharib) means not a resident of that city.
The three-day rule is from al-Ziyadat, with another opinion for a lesser amount.
2
Understand the reason for limiting to the court session
The commentary explains that this duration suffices for the claimant to present his case (bring evidence). Extending it beyond the session would cause undue hardship to the defendant. The judge requires evidence only during that session.
3
Apply the ruling: what happens at the end of the session?
If the claimant fails to bring evidence by the time the judge rises, the judge may either administer an oath to the claimant or release the defendant entirely. This gives the judge discretion.
The text mentions the judge's choice: 'إن شاء حلَّفه إن شاء خلى سبيله'.
4
Note the procedural details for following the defendant
The commentary elaborates: the defendant must allow the claimant to follow him everywhere except the mosque (as it is for worship) and inside his house without permission. For a female defendant, extra precautions apply: the claimant may hold her clothes and if she flees to a ruin, he may enter to guard her while maintaining distance.
These details come from al-Tahtawi's commentary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والغريب قَدْرَ مجلس الحكم، ولا يكفل إلا إلى آخر المجلس أي إن أبى الخصم عن إعطاء الكفيل لازمه المدَّعي ثلاثةَ أَيَّام ، ثُمَّ عطف على الضمير المنصوب في لازمه قوله : (والغريب قَدْرَ مجلس الحكم ) : أي لازم المدعي الغريب مقدار ما يكون القاضي جالساً في المحكمة ، ) ( ولا يكفل إلا إلى آخر المجلس ) : أي إن أخذ منه الكفيل لا يؤخذ إلا إلى آخر مجلس الحكم"، فإن أتى بالبينة فيها ، وإلا يُحَلِّفَهُ إِن شاء أو يدعه.
[1] قوله : أي إن أبى... الخ أي إن أبى الخصم أن يكفل لا يجبره القاضي على التكفيل، بل يأمره أن يلازمه ويدور معه حيث يسير مقدار مدة التكفيل على القولين لا يغيب، ولا يلازمه مكاناً معيناً ولا يلازمه في المسجد ؛ لأنه بني للذكر، وإذا انتهى إلى داره. ذكر في «الزيادات»: إنه إما أن يأذن للمدَّعي في الدخول معه أو يجلس معه على باب الدار ؛ لأنه لو تركه يدخل ربَّما يهرب من جانب آخر، وإن كان الخصم امرأة لازمها وجلس معها وقبض على ثيابها. فإن هربت ودخلت خربةً لا بأس بدخوله عليها إذا كان الرجل يأمن على نفسه ، ويكون بعيداً منها يحفظها بعينه ؛ لأن في هذه الخلوة ضرورة. ذكره شيخنا العلامة الطحطاوي في حاشيته على الدر المختار).
[٢] قوله : مقدار ما يكون القاضي جالساً في المحكمة ؛ لأن هذا القدر يحصل به النظر للمدعي ، فأما في إمساكه على باب القاضي يوماً أو أكثر ؛ ليحضر المدعي بينة ضرر على المطلوب، فإذا جاء أوان قيام القاضي عن المجلس ولم يحضر المدعي بينة ، فإن القاضي يحلفه ويخلي سبيل المطلوب ليذهب حيث شاء. 
[٣] قوله : إلا إلى آخر مجلس الحكم ؛ لأنه يلحقه الضرر بالزيادة على ذلك ، فلا يزاد عليه ولا ضرر في هذا القدر ظاهراً.
[٤]قوله وإلا إلى ... الخ ؛ أي وإن لم يأت بالبينة، فالقاضي بالخيار إن شاءَ حلَّفَه إن شاء خلى سبيله.
And the stranger [has] the extent of the court session, and he is not taken as a surety except until the end of the session. That is, if the opponent refuses to give a surety, the claimant must follow him for three days. Then he attached to the accusative pronoun in 'must follow him' his saying: (And the stranger [has] the extent of the court session): meaning the claimant who is a stranger must follow [the opponent] for the amount of time that the judge is sitting in the court. (And he is not taken as a surety except until the end of the session): meaning, if a surety is taken from him, he is not held except until the end of the court session. Then if he brings evidence during it [the session], [fine]; otherwise, the judge may administer an oath if he wishes or let him go.
[1] His saying: 'that is, if the opponent...' etc.: That is, if the opponent refuses to give a surety, the judge does not compel him to provide a surety, but rather orders him to accompany [the claimant] and go around with him wherever he goes for the duration of the surety according to the two opinions, not disappearing, and he does not accompany him in a specific place nor in the mosque because it was built for remembrance. And when he reaches his house. It is mentioned in al-Ziyadat: either he gives permission to the claimant to enter with him or he sits with him at the door of the house; because if he lets him enter, perhaps he might escape from another side. And if the opponent is a woman, he accompanies her, sits with her, and holds onto her clothes. If she flees and enters a ruin, it is permissible for him to enter upon her if the man feels safe about himself, and he stays far from her, guarding her with his eyes; because there is necessity in this seclusion. It was mentioned by our teacher, the scholar al-Tahtawi in his commentary on al-Durr al-Mukhtar.
[2] His saying: 'the amount of time that the judge is sitting in the court': because this amount allows the claimant to have his case heard. As for detaining him at the judge's door for a day or more so that the claimant can bring evidence, that would be harmful to the defendant. So when the time for the judge to rise from the session arrives and the claimant has not brought evidence, then the judge administers an oath to him and releases the defendant to go wherever he wishes.
[3] His saying: 'except until the end of the court session': because harm would befall him if it were increased beyond that, so it is not increased, and there is no apparent harm in this amount.
[4] His saying: 'and if not until...' etc.: That is, if he does not bring evidence, then the judge has the choice: if he wishes, he administers an oath to him; if he wishes, he releases him.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the rules of kafalah (surety) in the context of a claim (da'wa). It addresses two specific scenarios: the general rule when a defendant refuses to provide a surety, and the special ruling for when the claimant is a stranger (not a local resident). The core ruling is that a stranger claimant's surety is limited to the duration of the court session; he cannot be required to provide surety beyond that time. The commentary explains that if the defendant refuses to give a surety, the claimant must follow him for three days (this is the initial ruling). However, if the claimant is a stranger, the duration of this following is only the amount of time the judge is sitting in court. The basis for this leniency is to avoid excessive hardship on the stranger. The commentary then elaborates on the procedure: the defendant is not compelled to provide a surety, but he must allow the claimant to accompany him wherever he goes, except into the mosque (because it is for worship) and except into his house without permission. For a female defendant, there are additional precautions: the claimant may hold her clothes and sit with her, and if she flees into a ruin, he may enter to guard her while maintaining distance. The passage also discusses the judge's role: if the claimant fails to bring evidence by the time the court session ends, the judge has the option to either administer an oath to the claimant or release the defendant. The commentator notes that according to al-Ziyadat, there are two opinions on the exact duration of the following (three days or less). The purpose of limiting the surety to the court session is to balance the claimant's right to pursue his claim with the defendant's right not to be unduly burdened.

Key Terms
كفيل (kafil): A person who stands as surety for another, guaranteeing his presence or payment.
غريب (gharib): A person who is not a resident of the city where the court is located; a stranger claimant.
مجلس الحكم (majlis al-hukm): The session in which the judge sits to hear cases.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The judge may either administer an oath to the claimant or release the defendant.
C) The judge must detain the defendant until evidence is brought.
D) The judge must order the claimant to provide a surety for the next session.

Key Takeaway
The stranger claimant's surety is strictly limited to the court session to prevent harm to the defendant while allowing the claimant to present his case.

The Manner of the Oath: By Allah Alone and Its Intensification
Ruling on oath by Allah, not by divorce/emancipation, and intensification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the required form of a judicial oath and explain the exception for insisting opponents, as well as the rules for intensification (taghliz) and the difference with the Shafi'i school.

Core Idea
The oath that incurs legal consequences must be by Allah alone; intensification is by divine attributes, not by time or place.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies the essential rules for judicial oaths (yamin). You will learn the textual proof from hadith that an oath must be by Allah, the practical exception when the opponent insists on a non-Allah oath, and the proper method of intensification (taghliz). We also contrast the Hanafi position with that of the Shafi'i school regarding the scope of intensification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? The judicial oath (yamin) in court. What is the hukm? The oath whose refusal leads to nukul must be by Allah; other forms are prohibited but with an exception.
The exception: if the opponent insists, some allow the judge to administer oath by divorce/emancipation, but refusal then does not incur nukul.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the specific hadith cited from Bukhari, Muslim, and Nasa'i. Understand the wording: 'Whoever is to take an oath, let him swear by Allah or be silent.'
The imperative 'falyahlif' and prohibition 'la tahlifu' indicate obligation and prohibition respectively.
3
Analyze the exception and its consequences
The exception (if opponent insists) is a concession due to people's carelessness. Crucially, refusal of such an oath does not lead to nukul.
This is because the oath is not by Allah and the person is refraining from a prohibited act.
4
Understand intensification (taghliz)
Intensification is done by attributes of Allah (e.g., al-Hayy alladhi la yamut) to make the swearer more cautious. It is not by time or place.
The judge has discretion in choosing the wording, but cannot add time/place based intensification.
5
Map the inter-madhhab dispute
Hanafi position: taghliz only by attributes. Shafi'i position: also by time (after Asr on Friday) and place (at minbar, holy sites). The thamarah (practical difference) is clear.
The passage begins the Shafi'i view but is cut off; we rely on what is given.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في كيفية اليمين والاستحلاف
والحلف بالله لا بالطلاق والعتاق، فإنَّ ألح الخصم، قيل : صح بهما في زماننا، ويُغلّظ بصفاته لا بالزمان والمكان
(والحلف بالله لا بالطلاق والعتاق ، فإنَّ ألح الخصم، قيل : صح بهما في زماننا) أي جاز للقاضى أن يُحَلِّفَهُ بالطلاق والعتاق ، ( ويُغلظ بصفاته لا بالزمان والمكان)
١ قوله : والحلف بالله لا بالطلاق والعتاق ؛ أي الحلفُ الذي يقضي بالنكول عنه لا يكون إلا بالله دون غيره، ولا بالطلاق والعتاق ونحوهما ؛ لما في «الصحيحين»، من حديث ابن عمر أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : «مَن كان حالفاً فليحلف بالله أو ليصمت». ولما أخرجه النَّسَائي من حديث أبي هريرة أنه قال قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : «لا تحلفوا إلا بالله ولا تحلفوا إلا وأنتم صادقون»، فظهر أن الحلف لا يجوز إلا بالله تعالى لا بالطلاق والعتاق ونحوهما. 
٢ قوله : فإن ألح الخصم... الخ ؛ أي فإن بالغ الخصم على التحليف بالطلاق أو العتاق أو نحوهما، قيل : جاز للقاضي أن يحلّفه بذلك ؛ لقلة المبالاة باليمين بالله تعالى ، ومن قال بالتحليف لم يقل : إنه مشروع ، بل قال إنه غير مشروع لكن يعرض عليه لعله يمتنع ، فإن من الأدنى ديانة لا يحلف بمثل ذلك كاذباً ، فإنه يؤدي إلى طلاق الزوجة وعتق الأمة، أو إمساكهما بالحرام بخلاف اليمين بالله تعالى ، فإنه يتساهل به في زماننا كثيرا.
ثم إن نكل لا يقضي عليه بالنكول ؛ لأنه امتنع عما هو منهي عنه شرعاً، ولو قضى عليه بالنكول لا ينفذ، ولو طلب المدعي عليه تحليف الشاهد، أو المدعي أنه لا يعلم أن الشاهد كاذب لا يجيبه القاضي ؛ لأنا أمرنا بإكرام الشهود، والمدعي لا يجب عليه اليمين خصوصاً إذا أقام بينة. 
٣ قوله : ويغلظ بصفاته ؛ يعني إن الحلف قد يغلظ بذكر أوصاف الله ؛ لأن أحوال الناس مشتبهة ، فمنهم من يمتنع عن اليمين بالتغليظ ويتجاسر عند عدمه، نحو: بالله الطالب الغالب ، المدرك المهلك ، الحيّ الذي لا يموت أبداً، ونحو ذلك، لا بالزمان والمكان)، هذا عندنا، وعند الشَّافِعِيِّ بكذا بعد صلاة العصر يوم الجمعة ، وبالمكان كالمسجد الجامع عند المنبر. فيغلظ عليه لعله يمتنع بذلك. قال في «الهداية»(٢) : والقاضي بالخيار إن شاء غلّظ وإن شاء لم يغلّظ فيقول : قل بالله أو والله ، وقيل : لا يغلّظ على المعروف بالصلاح ويغلط على غيره ، وقيل : يغلّظُ في الخطير من المال دون الحقير. انتهى.
١ قوله : نحو بالله الطالب الغالب... الخ ؛ ومثل قوله : والله الذي لا إله إلا هو عالم الغيب والشهادة الرحمن الرحيم الذي يعلم من السرِّ ما يعلم من العلانية ما لفلان هذا عليك، ولا قبلك هذا المال الذي ادعاه، وهو كذا لا شيء منه.
وما ذكره الشارح له فيه تردد ، فإن أسماء الله توقيفية كما تقرر في موضعه، والاختيار في صفة التغليظ إلى القضاة يزيدون فيه ما شاؤوا وينقصون ما شاؤوا. كذا في «خزانة المفتيين». ثم يحترز عن عطف بعض الأسماء على البعض، كيلا يتكرر عليه اليمين، فإن المستحق يمين واحدة، فلو ذكر بالله والرحمن والرحيم بالواو صارت ثلاثة أيمان ، والمستحق يمين واحدة، ولو أمره بالعطف فأتى بواحدة ونكل عن الباقي لا يقضى عليه بالنكول ؛ لأنه قد أتى ما هو المستحقُ عليه، ولو غلّظ عليه فحلف من غير تغليظ ونَكَلَ عن التغليظ لا يقضى عليه بالنكول ؛ لأن المقصود هو الحلف بالله قد حصل. كما صرحوا به. قوله : وعند الشافعي ... الخ ؛ قال الشافعي رحمه الله : إن كانت اليمين في قسامة أو في لعان، أو في مال عظيم يبلغ مائتي مثقال تغلّظ بالمكان فيحلف بين الركن والمقام إن كان بمكة، وعند قبر النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم إن كان في المدينة، وعند الصخرة إن كان في بيت المقدس، وفي الجوامع في غيرها فإن لم تكن ففي المساجد، ويكون ذلك يوم الجمعة بعد العصر.
Chapter on the manner of the oath and administering the oath. The oath is by Allah, not by divorce or emancipation. If the opponent insists, it is said to be valid by both in our time. And it is intensified by His attributes, not by time or place. (The oath is by Allah, not by divorce or emancipation. If the opponent insists, it is said to be valid by both in our time) meaning it is permissible for the judge to administer the oath by divorce or emancipation. (And it is intensified by His attributes, not by time or place). 1. His statement: The oath is by Allah, not by divorce or emancipation: i.e., the oath that leads to judgment for refusal is only by Allah, not by other than Him, and not by divorce or emancipation and similar; due to what is in the two Sahihs from Ibn Umar that the Prophet (ﷺ) said: 'Whoever is to take an oath, let him swear by Allah or be silent.' And due to what al-Nasa'i reported from Abu Hurairah that he said: The Messenger of Allah (ﷺ) said: 'Do not swear except by Allah, and do not swear except while you are truthful.' Thus it appears that oath is not permissible except by Allah Almighty, not by divorce or emancipation and the like. 2. His statement: 'If the opponent insists...' i.e., if the opponent persists in demanding an oath by divorce or emancipation or the like, it is said: It is permissible for the judge to administer the oath by that; due to the people's lack of reverence for the oath by Allah Almighty. And the one who said it is permissible did not say it is prescribed; rather he said it is not prescribed but is presented to him so that perhaps he will desist. For the one of low religiosity does not swear falsely by such, because it would lead to divorce of the wife and emancipation of the slave, or keeping them unlawfully, unlike the oath by Allah Almighty, for it is taken lightly in our time a lot. Then if he refuses, judgment for refusal is not passed against him; because he refrained from what is prohibited by law, and if judgment for refusal is passed it is not executed. If the defendant asks to swear the witness, or the plaintiff asks that he does not know that the witness is lying, the judge does not respond to him; because we are commanded to honor the witnesses, and the plaintiff is not obligated to swear especially if he has presented evidence. 3. His statement: 'And it is intensified by His attributes': meaning that the oath may be intensified by mentioning the attributes of Allah; because people's conditions vary, so among them is he who refrains from the intensified oath and dares at its absence, such as: 'By Allah, the Seeker, the Overcomer, the Perceiver, the Destroyer, the Living who never dies,' and the like, not by time or place. This is according to us. According to al-Shafi'i, it is after the Asr prayer on Friday, and by place such as the congregational mosque near the minbar. So he is intensified so that he may desist by that. He said in al-Hidayah: The judge has the choice: if he wishes he intensifies and if he wishes he does not. He says: 'Say: by Allah or by Allah.' And it is said: He does not intensify the one known for righteousness, and intensifies others. And it is said: He intensifies in considerable wealth but not in trivial. End. 1. His statement: 'such as: By Allah, the Seeker, the Overcomer...' and similar to his saying: 'By Allah, besides whom there is no god, Knower of the unseen and the witnessed, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful, who knows from secret what He knows from public, that so-and-so has nothing owed to you, and you do not have this wealth which he claims, and it is thus, nothing of it.' And what the commentator mentioned about it has hesitation, for the names of Allah are restricted (tawqifiyyah) as established in its place, and the choice regarding the manner of intensification is left to the judges: they increase as they wish and decrease as they wish. So in 'Khizanat al-Muftiyin'. Then he cautions against linking some names with others lest the oath be repeated, for what is owed is a single oath. So if he says 'By Allah, and al-Rahman, and al-Rahim' with the waw, it becomes three oaths, while what is owed is one oath. If he ordered him to link, and he performed one and refused the rest, judgment for refusal is not passed against him; because he has performed what is owed upon him. And if he intensified upon him and he swore without intensification and refused the intensification, judgment for refusal is not passed against him; because the objective is the oath by Allah, which has been obtained. As they clearly stated. His statement: 'According to al-Shafi'i...' He said: al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) said: If the oath is in a qasama (oath of blood money) or in li'an (mutual imprecation), or in a great wealth reaching two hundred mithqal, it is intensified by place: he swears between the corner and the maqam if in Makkah, and at the grave of the Prophet (ﷺ) if in Madinah, and at the rock if in Jerusalem, and in the congregational mosques in other places, and if not then in the mosques, and that takes place on Friday after Asr.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn from al-Wiqayah, expanding on the ruling of the manner of the oath (yamin) and its intensification (taghliz). The passage type is a ruling with proof (ruling_with_proof). The author begins by stating the fundamental rule: the oath that leads to judgment for refusal (nukul) must be by Allah alone, not by divorce or emancipation or similar. He provides the proof from hadith: the Prophet (ﷺ) forbade swearing by other than Allah, citing the well-known hadith in Bukhari and Muslim: 'Whoever is to take an oath, let him swear by Allah or be silent.' Another hadith from Nasa'i emphasizes the same. Thus, innovation like swearing by divorce or emancipation is impermissible. However, the author notes an exception: if the opponent insists (alah al-khasm), some scholars say it is permissible for the judge to administer the oath by divorce or emancipation in our time, because people have become careless with oaths by Allah. But this is not considered the original ruling; it is a practical concession. Even if such an oath is administered, if the person refuses, no judgment for refusal is passed because he is refraining from a prohibited act. Then the author discusses intensification (taghliz) of the oath: it is done by using attributes of Allah (like 'al-Talib, al-Ghalib') to make the oath more serious, but not by time or place. This is the Hanafi position (whenna). In contrast, al-Shafi'i allows intensification by time (after Asr on Friday) and place (at the minbar in the mosque, or specific holy sites). The author quotes al-Hidayah stating the judge has discretion. Additional commentary notes that names of Allah are tawqifiyyah (limited to revelation), but judges may choose the wording for intensification. Care must be taken not to join attributes with 'and', which would create multiple oaths when only one is owed. The passage ends with the beginning of the Shafi'i view but is incomplete due to page cut-off. The key takeaway is that oaths must be by Allah, and intensification is by divine attributes, not by created elements like time or place.

Key Terms
الحلف (al-halaf): The oath taken by a person, specifically in the context of judicial procedure where refusal leads to judgment.
التغليظ (al-taghliz): Intensifying the oath by mentioning attributes of Allah to make it more serious.
النكول (al-nukul): Refusal to take an oath, leading to judgment against the refusing party.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on intensification of an oath by place (e.g., at the minbar)?
A) It is permissible and recommended.
B) It is not allowed; only intensification by attributes of Allah is valid.
C) It is allowed only in cases of great wealth.
D) It is allowed for the judge but not the defendant.

Key Takeaway
The essential form of a valid legal oath in Hanafi fiqh is by Allah alone; intensification is by divine attributes, not by created elements.

Ruling on Swearing Jews and Christians by Their Scriptures
Discussion of the ruling on oaths for Jews and Christians with specific formulas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on swearing Jews and Christians and distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and al-Khassaf regarding the use of scripture in oaths.

Core Idea
Swearing by Allah alone suffices for all; additional formulas are only for deterrence.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on administering oaths to Jews and Christians in court. While some scholars allow swearing by their scriptures, the core ruling is that swearing by Allah alone is sufficient. The passage explores intra-madhhab differences and the rationale behind tashdid (making the oath severe) for non-Muslims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its basic ruling
The act is administering an oath to a Jew or Christian. The Hanafi school holds that swearing by Allah alone is sufficient and obligatory (wajib). Additional formulas using scripture are permissible but not required.
2
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa holds that only swearing by Allah is allowed for anyone. Muhammad and al-Khassaf allow swearing Jews and Christians by their scriptures as a form of tashdid, but al-Khassaf restricts this to only Jews and Christians.
3
Understand the reasoning behind each position
Abu Hanifa avoids any association of others with Allah. Al-Khassaf argues fire should not be glorified, but scriptures are noble and may be used. Muhammad adds that non-Muslims glorify their scriptures like Muslims glorify Islamic rites.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that swearing by Allah alone is sufficient. The additional scripture formula is recommended for tashdid to deter false oaths, as stated in al-Ikhtiyar and al-Bahr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذكر الطَّحْطَاوي (۱) نقلاً عن «البدائع» : لا يحلف على الإشارة إلى مصحف معين : أي من التوراة بأن يقول : بالله الذي أنزل هذه التوراة أو هذا الإنجيل ؛ لأنه ثبت تحريف بعضهما، فلا يؤمن أن تقع الإشارة إلى الحرف المحرف فيكون التحليف تعظيماً لما ليس كلام الله . انتهى. وعن أبي حنيفة له : إنه لا يحلف أحد إلا بالله خالصاً احترازاً عن إشراك غيره في التعظيم مع الله له. وذكر الخصاف له : أنه لا يحلف غير اليهودي والنصراني إلا بالله تعالى، وهو اختيار بعض مشايخنا ؛ لأن في ذكر النار في اليمين تعظيم لها ؛ لأن اليمين تشعر بذلك ، ولا ينبغي أن يعظم النار بخلاف التوراة والإنجيل ؛ لأن تعظيم كتب الله تعالى واجب. ومـا ذكـر هـاهـنـا هـو المذكور في الأصل، فكأنه وقع عـنـد محمـد ه : يعظمونها تعظيم المسلم الشعائر ، ولا يعبدونها حقيقة. هكذا ذكر الزيلعي (۳)، وغيره. لكن يشكل عليه : إن الدهريّة منهم لا يعتقدونَه، ولا دلالة في الآيـة علـى مـا ذكر ؛ لأن الوثنى يعبد غير الله له ويعتقد أن الله الله خالقه. ويمكن أن يقال : إن الدهري هو من يقول بقدم الدهر، وبإسناد الحوادث إليه، ويقول : إن مبدأ الممكنات هو الله تعالى كما قيل ، فلم يلزم عدم اعتقاد الله تعالى وعدم دلالة النص. فتدبر فيه. وقال في «الاختيار») : ولو اقتصر في الكلِّ على قوله بالله فهو كاف؛ لأن الزيادة للتأكيد كما قلنا في المسلم، وإنّما يغلّظ ليكون أعظم في قلوبهم، فلا يتجاسرون على اليمين الكاذبة. انتهى. وقال في «البحر») معزياً إلى «المحيط»: إن ما ذكر في الكتاب قول محمد له، وما ذكره الحَصافُ له قولهما ، وظاهر كلامهم : إن الكافر إذا حلف بالله فقط ، ونَكَل عما ذكر يكفي، ولا يقضي عليه بالنكول عن الوصيف ؛ لأنه من باب التغليظ فيكتفي بالله جلالة. انتهى.
Al-Tahawi mentioned, quoting from al-Bada'i': One should not swear by pointing to a specific scripture, i.e., from the Torah, by saying: 'By Allah who revealed this Torah or this Gospel' because corruption of both is established, so it is not safe from the possibility that the reference points to a corrupted letter, making the oath a glorification of what is not the speech of Allah. End. And from Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him): That no one should swear except by Allah alone, as a precaution against associating others with Allah in glorification. And al-Khassaf mentioned: That no one other than a Jew or Christian should swear except by Allah Almighty, and this is the choice of some of our scholars, because mentioning fire in the oath is a glorification of it, since the oath implies that, and fire should not be glorified, unlike the Torah and Gospel, because glorifying the books of Allah Almighty is obligatory. And what is mentioned here is what is mentioned in the original, so it is as if it occurred with Muhammad: They glorify them as a Muslim glorifies the rites, and they do not worship them in reality. Thus mentioned al-Zayla'i and others. But it is problematic because the Dahriyyah among them do not believe that, and there is no indication in the verse for what was mentioned; because the idolater worships other than Allah and believes that Allah is his Creator. And it can be said: The Dahri is one who says that time is eternal and attributes events to it, and says that the origin of contingent things is Allah Almighty as is said, so disbelief in Allah and lack of indication of the text are not entailed. So reflect on it. And in al-Ikhtiyar it is said: If one suffices in all cases by saying 'By Allah', it is sufficient, because the addition is for emphasis as we said for the Muslim, and it is only made severe to make it greater in their hearts, so they do not dare to swear falsely. End. And in al-Bahr, citing al-Muhit: What is mentioned in the book is the statement of Muhammad, and what al-Khassaf mentioned is the statement of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and the apparent meaning of their statement: If the disbeliever swears only by Allah and abstains from what was mentioned, it is sufficient, and he is not judged to have abstained from the description because it is a matter of tashdid, so swearing by Allah alone suffices. End.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and discussion of the Hanafi position on swearing Jews and Christians in court. The author begins by citing al-Tahawi's report from al-Bada'i' that one should not make a Jew or Christian swear by pointing to a specific Torah or Gospel because these scriptures have been corrupted; the oath might inadvertently glorify a corrupted part. Abu Hanifa's position is that no one should be sworn except by Allah alone, avoiding any association. Al-Khassaf reports that only a Jew or Christian may be sworn by other than Allah (by their scriptures), as fire should not be glorified. The author then notes that according to Muhammad, they glorify their scriptures as Muslims glorify the rites of Islam, not as acts of worship. A problem is raised about the Dahriyyah (materialists) who do not believe in Allah, but the author responds that the Dahri denies the Creator, so the issue remains. Finally, al-Ikhtiyar states that swearing by Allah alone is sufficient for all, and the additional formula is only for emphasis and deterrence. Al-Bahr cites al-Muhit that the relied-upon position is that of Muhammad and al-Khassaf, but swearing by Allah alone suffices.

Key Terms
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath sworn by Allah or by a revered object, used to confirm a statement in court.
التغليظ (al-tashdid): Making the oath more severe by adding references to scripture to deter false swearing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the minimum requirement for swearing a Jew or Christian in court?
A) They must swear by their scripture (Torah/Gospel).
B) They must swear by Allah alone.
C) They must swear by both Allah and their scripture.
D) They cannot be sworn at all.

Key Takeaway
Swearing by Allah alone is sufficient for all; additional scripture formulas are recommended for deterrence but not required.

Oath on Outcome for Female and Infidel Slave Emancipation
Ruling on the type of oath when a female slave or infidel male slave claims emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the form of oath required when a female slave or an infidel male slave claims emancipation, and explain the legal rationale of potential re-enslavement.

Core Idea
If the cause can be undone, swear on the outcome, not the act.

When a slave claims emancipation from the master, Islamic law requires an oath. However, the type of oath differs based on whether the slave's freedom can be reversed. This lesson explains the Hanafi position: for a female slave (who may be re-enslaved through apostasy and capture) and an infidel male slave (who may breach his covenant and be captured), the master swears on the current state (al-hāṣil) rather than on the past act of emancipation. The rationale lies in the possibility of re-enslavement, which makes the current status uncertain.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and the parties
The scenario is a female slave (ummah) or an infidel male slave (ʿabd kāfir) claiming that her/his master emancipated them. The master denies this. The question is: what oath should the master take?
2
State the oath requirement
According to the Hanafi school, the master does not swear denying the act (e.g., 'I did not emancipate you'). Instead, he swears on the outcome (ʿalā al-ḥāṣil): 'She/he is not free at this moment.'
The Arabic phrase is 'mā hiya ḥurratun fi al-ḥāl' for the female slave and 'mā huwa ḥurrun fi al-ḥāl' for the male slave.
3
Understand the rationale
The master swears on the outcome because the cause of emancipation can be raised (i.e., reversed). For a female slave, apostasy and joining enemy territory (dār al-ḥarb) followed by capture can re-enslave her. For an infidel male slave, breaching the covenant of protection (dhimma) and joining the enemy territory can lead to capture and re-enslavement. Thus, the current state is uncertain, so the oath is on the present status.
The text uses 'لأن السبب قد يرتفع' which means 'because the cause can be removed.'
4
Contrast with Muslim male slave
For a Muslim male slave, emancipation is irreversible. Therefore, in such a case, the master swears on the act itself (al-batāt) because slavery cannot return. The passage notes this has been explained earlier.
The contrast highlights the specificity of the ruling to female and infidel slaves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الأمة والعبد الكافر على الحاصل؛ لأن السبب قد يرتفع فيهما، أما في الأمة فالردة واللحاق إلى دار الحرب، ثم السبي، وأما في العبد الكافر فنقض العهد واللحاق، ثم السبي. قال في الشرح: يعني فيما إذا ادعت الأمة على مولاها أنه أعتقها، فإنه لا يحلف بالله ما أعتقتها، ولكن يحلف على الحاصل؛ أي ما هي حرة في الحال. وكذا إذا ادعى العبد الكافر على مولاه بالعتق فإنه لا يحلف بالله ما أعتقته، بل يحلف على الحاصل: أي ما هو حر في الحال؛ لأن الرق بعد العتق يتكرر على الأمة بالردة، واللحاق بدار الحرب ثم السبي، وعلى العبد الكافر بنقض عهد الذمة، واللحاق بدار الحرب ثم السبي، ولا يتكرر على العبد المسلم كما عرفت آنفا.
And regarding the female slave and the infidel male slave, the oath is upon the outcome because the cause can be raised in both. As for the female slave, [it is] apostasy and joining the enemy territory, then capture; and as for the infidel male slave, [it is] breach of covenant and joining, then capture. He said in the commentary: meaning when the female slave claims against her master that he emancipated her, he does not swear 'by Allah I did not emancipate her,' but rather swears on the outcome, i.e., 'she is not free at present.' Similarly, when the infidel male slave claims emancipation against his master, he does not swear 'by Allah I did not emancipate him,' but rather swears on the outcome, i.e., 'he is not free at present,' because slavery after emancipation repeats for the female slave through apostasy and joining the enemy territory then capture, and for the infidel male slave through breach of the covenant of protection and joining the enemy territory then capture, and it does not repeat for the Muslim male slave as you have learned earlier.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage addresses the type of oath required when a female slave (ummah) or an infidel male slave (abd kafir) claims to have been emancipated by the master. The Hanafi school holds that in such cases, the master does not swear an oath denying the act of emancipation (i.e., 'I did not emancipate you') because the effect of emancipation can be undone in these two categories of slaves. For the female slave, if she apostatizes and joins the enemy territory (dar al-harb) and is subsequently captured, she can be re-enslaved. Similarly, for the infidel male slave, if he breaches the covenant of protection (dhimma) and joins the enemy territory and is captured, slavery can be reestablished. Therefore, the possibility of reversal makes the immediate state of freedom uncertain, so the master only swears that the slave is not currently free. This differs from the case of a Muslim male slave, where emancipation is irreversible and thus the master would swear on the act itself.

Key Terms
الحاصل (al-hāṣil): The outcome or current state; here means the present condition of the slave (free or slave) rather than the past act of emancipation.
البتات (al-batāt): Certainty; an oath that denies the act itself with absolute certainty.
اليمين على العلم (al-yamīn 'alā al-'ilm): Oath on knowledge; the swearer states that they do not know a fact to be true.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does a female slave's emancipation not result in an irreversible freedom?
A) She can be re-enslaved through apostasy and capture.
B) She cannot own property.
C) Her testimony is not accepted.
D) Her master always retains ownership.

Key Takeaway
For female and infidel male slaves, the master swears on the current state because their freedom can be reversed; for Muslim male slaves, the oath is on the act.

Oath of Heir vs. Donee/Purchaser: On Knowledge vs. Certainty
Ruling on oath types for heirs, donees, and purchasers, with exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between oaths on knowledge and oaths on certainty, apply the Hanafi rule to heirs, donees, and purchasers, and state the exception for defect claims.

Core Idea
The form of a judicial oath depends on whether the act is one's own or another's, and on the voluntariness of acquisition.

This lesson examines a critical distinction in Hanafi oath jurisprudence: the type of oath (on knowledge vs. on certainty) required when a third party claims property from an heir versus a donee or purchaser. You will learn the reasoning behind this distinction, the governing rule, and an important exception in cases of defective goods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of oath
Recognize 'oath on knowledge' (al-yamīn 'alā al-ʿilm) as swearing 'I do not know' and 'oath on certainty' (al-yamīn 'alā al-batāt) as a flat denial. Understand their definitions and when each is used.
The oath on certainty is more emphatic.
2
Apply the rule to an heir
An heir inherits property involuntarily and lacks personal knowledge of the testator's transactions. Therefore, he swears on knowledge: 'I do not know that this belongs to you.'
The heir cannot swear with certainty because he did not witness the testator's actions.
3
Apply the rule to a donee or purchaser
A donee or purchaser acquires property voluntarily. Since they would not have accepted the gift or made the purchase without believing it was legitimately theirs, they have personal certainty. Hence they swear on certainty: 'This is not your property.'
The voluntary nature of the transaction gives them knowledge of ownership.
4
Explain the exception for defect returns
In cases of return due to a defect (ar-radd bi al-ʿayb), the buyer claims a defect, and the seller swears on certainty even though the defect might not be the seller's own act. This is because the seller is liable (ḍamān) for delivering a sound item, so the oath refers to his own responsibility.
The exception shows the principle can be overridden by liability considerations.
5
Understand the consequence of taking the wrong form
If an oath on certainty is required but the party swears on knowledge, the oath is invalid. If an oath on knowledge is required but the party swears on certainty, it is valid because the certainty oath is stronger. This affects judicial outcomes like refusal (nukul).
The judge must enforce the correct form to ensure legal certainty.

بالاختيار، ولو لم يعلم أن العين ملك البائع له لما باشر الشراء باختياره، ولا قبل الهبة بخلاف الملك في الإرث، فإنه يثبت للوارث جبراً، ولا علم له بحال ملك المورث. والأصل في ذلك أن اليمين إن كانت على فعل الغير فهي على العلم، وإن كانت على فعل النفس فهي على البتات. ذكر الزيلعي والعيني نقلا عن الخلواني: هذا الأصل مستقيم في المسائل كلها إلا في الرد بالعيب. فإنه إذا ادعى المشتري أن العبد أبق ونحو ذلك، فأراد المشتري تحليف البائع فإنه يحلفه على البتات، مع أنه فعل غيره وإنما كان كذلك؛ لأن البائع ضمن تسليم المبيع سالماً عن العيوب، فالتحليف يرجع إلى ما ضمن بنفسه فيحلف على البتات. انتهى. ثم كل موضع يجب اليمين فيه على البتات، فحلف على العلم، لا يعتبر حلفه لا يقضي القاضي عليه بالنكول، ولا يسقط اليمين عنه، وكل موضع يجب اليمين فيه على العلم، فحلف على البتات، يعتبر حلفه حتى يسقط اليمين عنه، ويقضي القاضي عليه بالنكول؛ لأن الحلف على البتات آكد، فيعتبر مطلقاً بخلاف العكس، والا ذكره الشمني وغيره. قوله: فإنه ينفي العلم؛ وذكر فخر الإسلام له في «الجامع الصغير»: المشتري والموهوب له مالك بسبب شرعي وضع له، وهذا يفيده علماً بأنه ملكه لا ملك غيره، فصح تحليفه بالثبات، فإن أبى فقد امتنع عما هو مطلق له، فصار باذلاً، فأما الوارث فلا علم له بما صنع المورث، فطولب بعلم إن كان له، وإذا لم يفعل مع الإمكان صار باذلاً. كذا في «الكفاية».
And one who inherits something and another claims it swears on knowledge, and one who is given a gift or purchases swears on certainty. Al-batât means cutting; so the donee and the purchaser swear by Allah: 'This is not your property' because the cause is susceptible to being raised by a remover, just as when a Muslim male slave claims the emancipation that occurred during his Islam against his master, and he denies it, the master swears on the cause by Allah: 'I did not emancipate him,' and there is no need then for the oath on the outcome because the cause is not raised; indeed, it is not conceivable that he returns to slavery because if he apostatizes he is killed and fleeing to enemy territory is rare, unlike the female slave and the infidel male slave, for in those he swears on the outcome. As will come. His saying: 'And he swears on knowledge...' is another type of how the oath is taken. Its exposition: Whoever inherits an object, knowing that by the judge's knowledge or the plaintiff's admission or the defendant's evidence, and another claims it, and there is no evidence for the plaintiff, and the judge wants to administer the oath to the heir, he administers the oath on knowledge, i.e., the defendant's knowledge. He (the judge) says to him: 'By Allah, you do not know that this object belongs to him.' He does not administer the oath on certainty because the heir does not know what the testator did. And if something is given as a gift to the defendant, or the defendant purchases it without evidence, then the plaintiff claims it without evidence that it belongs to him, the donee or purchaser swears: 'By Allah, it is not the property of the plaintiff' because accepting the gift and purchase is a cause for establishing ownership by choice. If he did not know the object was the seller's property, he would not have engaged in the purchase by choice nor accepted the gift, unlike ownership by inheritance, which is established for the heir by compulsion, and he has no knowledge of the state of the testator's ownership. The principle in this is that if the oath is concerning the act of another, it is on knowledge; and if it is concerning one's own act, it is on certainty. Al-Zayla'i and al-'Ayni reported from al-Khalwani: This principle is sound in all cases except regarding the return due to a defect (al-rada bi al-'ayb). Indeed, if the purchaser claims that the slave ran away or the like, and the purchaser wants to administer the oath to the seller, he administers the oath on certainty, even though it is another's act; it is only thus because the seller is responsible for delivering the sold item free from defects. So the administration of oath refers back to what he himself is responsible for, so he swears on certainty. Then: Every place where the oath is required on certainty, if he swears on knowledge, his oath is not considered; the judge does not pass judgment against him for refusal (nukul), nor does the oath drop from him. And every place where the oath is required on knowledge, if he swears on certainty, his oath is considered such that the oath drops from him, and the judge passes judgment against him for refusal, because the oath on certainty is more emphatic, so it is considered absolutely unlike the opposite. Al-Shumnni and others mentioned this. His saying: 'He denies knowledge.' Fakhr al-Islam mentioned in al-Jami' al-Saghir: The purchaser and the donee are owners by a legal cause established for them, and this gives them knowledge that it is their property, not another's. So his swearing with certainty is valid. If he refuses, he has refrained from that which is his right, so he becomes a depleter. As for the heir, he has no knowledge of what the testator did, so he is required to have knowledge if he had it. When he does not do so despite being able, he becomes a depleter. This is in al-Kifayah.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage explains the different forms of oaths required when property is claimed by a third party. If an heir inherits property and another person claims it, the heir swears an oath on knowledge, meaning he swears 'I do not know that this belongs to you.' This is because the heir does not have personal knowledge of the testator's transactions. In contrast, if the property was obtained through a gift or purchase, the donee or purchaser swears an oath on certainty, meaning 'this is not your property.' The reason is that gift and purchase are voluntary acts; the person would not have accepted the gift or made the purchase unless they believed the property was legitimately theirs. The general principle is stated: if the oath is about another person's action, it is on knowledge; if about one's own action, it is on certainty. However, there is an exception: in cases of defects (sale of a defective slave), the buyer can administer the oath to the seller on certainty even though the defect is not the seller's own action? Actually, the seller swears on certainty because he is liable for delivering a sound item. The passage also discusses the consequences if the wrong form of oath is taken: if an oath on certainty is required but the person swears on knowledge, the oath is not valid; conversely, if an oath on knowledge is required but the person swears on certainty, it is valid because the certainty oath is stronger. The commentary by Fakhr al-Islam and others elaborates on the reasoning.

Key Terms
اليمين على العلم (al-yamīn 'alā al-ʿilm): An oath stating 'I do not know' regarding the claim; used when the swearer lacks personal knowledge.
اليمين على البتات (al-yamīn 'alā al-batāt): An oath of absolute denial; the swearer swears that the claim is false without qualification.
الرد بالعيب (ar-radd bi al-ʿayb): Return of a sold item due to a defect; an exception where the seller swears on certainty even though the defect may have occurred without his action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why must a donee swear on certainty rather than on knowledge?
A) Because the donee voluntarily accepted the gift, implying knowledge of ownership
B) Because the donee is the plaintiff
C) Because the judge prefers certainty
D) Because the heir is exempt

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes oaths based on knowledge: heirs swear on knowledge, donees/purchasers swear on certainty, with an exception for defect claims where the seller's liability triggers a certainty oath.

Dispute on Tahlif After Partial Perishing of Goods
Intra-Hanafi dispute on tahlif after partial perishing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad on tahlif after partial perishing, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf among them.

Core Idea
A text contrary to analogy cannot be extended — Abu Hanifa restricts tahlif to intact goods.

This passage explores a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute: when part of the sold goods perishes after possession, and the parties dispute the price, does the mutual oath procedure (tahlif) apply? The matn states that tahlif is suspended unless the seller abandons his claim to the perished portion. The commentary unpacks the reasoning of the three major Hanafi imams, with Abu Hanifa's position being the most restrictive yet the most methodologically consistent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and matn ruling
Understand the concrete example: two slaves sold together, one dies after delivery, parties dispute price. The matn states there is no tahlif unless the seller abandons the share of the perished.
2
State the three Hanafi positions
Abu Hanifa: no tahlif unless seller abandons perished share; Abu Yusuf: tahlif on remaining only, with buyer's word on value of perished; Muhammad: tahlif on both living and dead, entire contract rescinded.
These are found in al-Asl, al-Jami' al-Saghir, and other texts.
3
Analyze each imam's proof
Abu Hanifa: tahlif after possession is established by nass contrary to qiyas, so it cannot be extended beyond the intact commodity. Abu Yusuf: prevention is due to perishing, so limited to extent of perished. Muhammad: total perishing does not prevent tahlif in his view, so partial perishing is less deserving of prevention.
Each dalil is aqli (reason-based) built on the nature of the ruling.
4
Understand the commentator's critique
The commentator refutes Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: partial perishing requires estimation (harz) which is impermissible; tahlif on remaining also requires estimation of proportional price, not allowed unless seller abandons perished share.
The procedural difficulty with valuation is key.
5
Extract the thamarah al-khilaf and mufta bihi
Practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, tahlif only if seller forgoes perished share; under others, tahlif can occur without forfeiture but with different outcomes. The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's.
This affects how disputes are resolved in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا بعد هلاك بعضه إلا أن يرضى البائع بترك حصة الهالك (ولا بعد هلاك بعضه إلا أن يرضى البائع بترك حصة الهالك) 
قوله : ولا بعد هلاكِ بعضه ؛ يعني ولا تحالف إذا اختلفا بعد هلاك بعض البيع بعد قبض الجميع، كما إذا باع عبدين صفقة واحدة فهلك أحدهما عند المشتري بعد قبضهما، ثم اختلفا في الثمن، فقال البائع : بعتهما منك بألفي درهم، وقال المشتري : اشتريتهما منك بألف درهم، فلم يتحالفا عند الإمام إلا أن يرضى البائع أن يترك حصة الهالك، هذا ما نص عليه القُدُوري)، وجعله صاحب «النهاية» لفظ «المبسوط».
وقال في «الجامع الصغير) : القولُ قول المشتري مع يمينه عند أبي حنيفة له إلا أن يشاء البائع أن يأخذ الحي ولا شيء له. انتهى. وقال قاضي خان : وذكر في «الأصل»: إلا أن يشاء البائع أن يأخذ الحي، ولا يأخذ من ثمن الميت شيئاً.
وقال أبو يوسف : يتحالفان في القائم، ويفسخ العقد فيه، والقولُ قـول المشتري في قيمة الهالك. هكذا في «كمال الدراية»، و«الهداية»، وغيرهما من الكتب المتداولة.
لكن فيه نوع قصور ؛ لأنه إذا اختلفا في قيمة الهالك فالقولُ للبائع لا للمشتري. كما في «الهداية)، فإن اختلفا في قيمة الهالك، فالقول قول البائع ؛ لأنَّ الثمن قد وجب باتفاقهما، ثمّ المشتري يدعي زيادة السقوط بنقصان قيمة الهالك، والبائع ينكره، والقول للمنكر.
وما قيل هاهنا ينافيه فلا بُدّ هاهنا من التأويل، وهـو مـا ذكــره في «نتائج الأفكار ) : من أنَّ المراد أنه بعد التحالف يردُّ الحي على البائع، ويسقط حصة الحي من الثمن، ويلزم المشتري حصة الهالك من الثمن الذي أقرّ به المشتري بعد تقسيم الثمن على قيمة الحي والهالك، فقول المشتري إنّما يعتبر في حصة الهالك من الثمن الذي أقر به المشتري لا في قيمة الهالك. انتهى. وقال محمد : يتحالفان على الحي والهالك ، ويردُّ الحي وقيمة الهالك ؛ لأنَّ هلاك كل السلعة لا يمنع التحالف عنده ، فهلاك البعض أولى أن لا يمنع. ولأبي يوسف له : إِنَّ امتناع التحالف إنّما يكون لأجل الهلاك، فيتقدر بقدرِ الهالك ، فإنَّ الحكم لا يزيد على العلة.
ولأبي حنيفة له : إنَّ التحالف بعد القبض ثبت بالنص على خلاف القياس في حال قيام السلعة، والسلعة اسم الجميع أجزائها ، فلا يتقي السلعة بعد فوات جزء منها ، وما ثبت بخلاف القياس لا يتعدى.
والجواب عن قول محمد الله : إنَّ هلاك البعض محوج إلى معرفة القيمة بالحرز ؛ وذلك مجهول في المقسم عليه، فلا يجوز.
والجواب عن قول أبي يوسف الله : إنَّ التحالف في القائم لا يمكن إلا على اعتبار حصته من الثمن، فلا بُدَّ من قسمتِهِ على القيمة، وهي تعرف بالحرز والظنّ، وذلك لا يجوز إلا أن يرضى البائع أن يترك حصة الهالك.
فحينئذ يكون الثمنُ كلّه بمقابلة الحي، ويخرجُ الهالك عن العقد فيكون كأنَّ العقد وقع على هذا فيتحالفان، فإن حلفا فسخ العقد فيه وأخذه، ولا يأخذ من ثمن الهالك ولا من قيمته شيئاً، وأيهما نَكَل لزمه دعوى الآخر، كما هو الحكم في الاختلاف عند قيام السلعة (١)
[١] قوله : أي لا يأخذ... الخ ؛ يعني أنَّ البائع إذا رضي بترك حصة الهالـك فـلا يأخذ من ثمن الهالك شيئاً، ويجعله كأنه لم يكن ، والعقد كأنه وقع على القائم فقط فيكون الثمن كله بمقابلة القائم، فيتحالفان كما هو الحكم في الاختلاف عند قيام السلعة.
And there is no mutual oath after the perishing of some of it unless the seller is satisfied with abandoning the share of the perished. (And there is no mutual oath after the perishing of some of it unless the seller is satisfied with abandoning the share of the perished) His statement: 'And there is no mutual oath after the perishing of some of it'; meaning, there is no mutual oath if they differ after the perishing of part of the sale after delivery of all of it, for example if he sold two slaves in one lump sum and one of them perished in the possession of the buyer after taking delivery, then they differed over the price. The seller said: I sold them both to you for two thousand dirhams, and the buyer said: I bought them from you for one thousand dirhams. So they do not perform mutual oath according to Imam [Abu Hanifa] unless the seller is satisfied with abandoning the share of the perished. This is what al-Quduri stated, and the author of al-Nihayah made it the wording of al-Mabsut. And it is stated in al-Jami' al-Saghir: 'The statement is the statement of the buyer with his oath according to Abu Hanifa, unless the seller wishes to take the living and nothing for him.' End. And Qadi Khan said: It is mentioned in al-Asl: 'Unless the seller wishes to take the living and does not take anything from the price of the dead.' And Abu Yusuf said: They perform mutual oath regarding the remaining [goods], and the contract is rescinded therein, and the statement is the buyer's regarding the value of the perished. This is in Kamal al-Dirayah, al-Hidayah, and other commonly used books. But there is some deficiency in it; because if they differ over the value of the perished, the statement is the seller's, not the buyer's. As in al-Hidayah: if they differ over the value of the perished, the statement is the seller's because the price has been established by their agreement, then the buyer claims an increase in the reduction due to the decrease in value of the perished, and the seller denies it, and the statement is of the denier. And what is said here contradicts it, so interpretation is necessary, which is what was mentioned in Nata'ij al-Afkar: that the intent is that after the mutual oath, the living is returned to the seller, and the share of the living from the price drops off, and the buyer is obligated for the share of the perished from the price that the buyer acknowledged after dividing the price according to the value of the living and the perished. So the buyer's statement is considered only in the share of the perished from the price that the buyer acknowledged, not in the value of the perished. End. And Muhammad said: They perform mutual oath regarding the living and the perished, and he returns the living and the value of the perished; because the perishing of all of the commodity does not prevent mutual oath according to him, so the perishing of part is more deserving of not preventing. And for Abu Yusuf: The prevention of mutual oath is only due to perishing, so it is limited to the extent of the perished; for the ruling does not exceed the cause. And for Abu Hanifa: Mutual oath after delivery is established by text contrary to analogy while the commodity is whole, and 'commodity' is a name for all its parts, so the commodity cannot be complete after the loss of a part, and what is established contrary to analogy cannot be extended. And the answer to Muhammad's statement: The perishing of part necessitates knowing the value by estimation, and that is unknown in the subject of division, so it is not permissible. And the answer to Abu Yusuf's statement: Mutual oath regarding the remaining is impossible except by considering its share of the price, so division according to value is necessary, and that is known by estimation and conjecture, and that is not permissible unless the seller is satisfied with abandoning the share of the perished. So then the entire price is in exchange for the living, and the perished exits from the contract, so it is as if the contract occurred on this, so they perform mutual oath. If they both take an oath, the contract is rescinded regarding it and he takes it, and he does not take anything from the price of the perished nor from its value. And whichever of them refuses (nukul) must bear the claim of the other, as is the ruling in the case of disagreement while the commodity is whole. (1) [1] His statement: 'i.e., he does not take...' end.; meaning that when the seller is satisfied with abandoning the share of the perished, he does not take anything from the price of the perished, and makes it as if it never existed, and the contract as if it occurred only on the remaining, so the entire price is in exchange for the remaining, so they perform mutual oath as is the ruling in the case of disagreement while the commodity is whole.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the procedure of mutual oath (tahlif) when part of the sold goods perishes after the buyer has taken possession, and the parties disagree on the price. The commentator elaborates on the matn (base text) which states that there is no mutual oath in such a case unless the seller agrees to abandon the share of the perished goods. The discussion begins with a concrete scenario: a seller sells two slaves in a single transaction, the buyer takes possession, one slave dies, and then they dispute the price—the seller claiming 2000 dirhams, the buyer claiming 1000. Imam Abu Hanifa rules that mutual oath does not take place unless the seller chooses to forgo his claim to the share of the perished slave. This is the relied-upon position. Abu Yusuf holds that they do perform mutual oath only on the remaining (living) slave, and the contract is rescinded regarding that part. He further says the buyer's word is taken on the value of the perished slave. The commentator notes a shortcoming in Abu Yusuf's view: if the parties dispute the value of the perished, the seller's word should be taken as he is the denier of the buyer's claim of lower value. An interpretation is then offered from 'Nata'ij al-Afkar' to resolve this: after mutual oath, the living slave is returned to the seller, the share of the living from the price is dropped, and the buyer pays only the share of the perished from the price he acknowledged, after dividing the total price according to the values of the living and the dead. This makes the buyer's statement relevant only to his acknowledged share of the price, not to the value of the perished. Imam Muhammad's position is that mutual oath occurs on both the living and the dead (i.e., the remaining and the value of the perished), and the entire transaction is rescinded. His proof is that if total perishing does not prevent tahlif in his view, then partial perishing is less likely to prevent it. Abu Yusuf argues that the prevention of tahlif is due to perishing, so it is limited to the extent of the perished (i.e., only on the remaining). Abu Hanifa's proof is that tahlif after possession is established by a text (nass) that operates contrary to analogy, and only applies when the commodity is intact; once part is lost, the ruling cannot be extended. The commentator then gives responses to each opponent. To Muhammad: partial perishing requires estimating value, which involves uncertainty and is impermissible. To Abu Yusuf: tahlif on the remaining requires dividing the price proportionally by value, which also involves estimation; this is not allowed unless the seller voluntarily abandons the share of the perished. Thus, the only permissible procedure is Abu Hanifa's: if the seller agrees to forgo the share of the perished, the entire price is deemed to be for the remaining, and mutual oath is performed on that basis. If both take oaths, the contract is rescinded on the remaining; if one refuses, the other's claim prevails.

Key Terms
تحالف (tahāluf): Mutual oath-taking process where both parties in a dispute swear oaths to support their claims, leading to rescission of the contract.
هلاك (halāk): Perishing or destruction of the sold goods, which triggers specific rules regarding tahlif.
قائم (qā’im): The remaining or extant part of the sold goods that has not perished.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, in a dispute over the price after one of two slaves dies in the buyer's possession, when does tahlif (mutual oath) take place?
A) Always, regardless of the seller's choice
B) Only if the seller agrees to forgo the share of the perished slave
C) Only if the buyer agrees to pay the value of the perished slave
D) Never, because partial perishing prevents tahlif

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's restriction on tahlif after partial perishing is rooted in the principle that text-based rulings established contrary to analogy cannot be extended beyond their original scope.

Resolving Disputes Over Household Goods (Mata' al-Bayt)
Rules for dividing household goods when spouses dispute ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for attributing household goods in disputes between spouses, multiple wives, and father and son.

Core Idea
Household goods belong to the one whose house and possession they are in; custom and dependence determine ownership in disputes.

When spouses disagree about who owns items in the house, Islamic law provides clear guidelines based on possession, customary use, and living arrangements. This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya expands these rules to cover multiple wives and father-son disputes, showing how the same principles apply consistently.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary dispute scenario
Recognise that the ruling first addresses dispute between husband and wife where each claims all goods as their own. This is the base case.
The dispute is restricted to spouses; co-wives separately do not dispute with each other over shared goods.
2
Note the condition of living arrangement for multiple wives
If multiple wives live in one house, goods are shared equally among them. If each lives separately, the goods in each house belong to that wife and the husband exclusively.
This distinction avoids confusion about co-ownership.
3
Apply the father-son dependency rule
For disputes between father and son, the person on whom the other is dependent and who controls the house is deemed owner. If the father depends on the son in the son's house, goods belong to the son; if the son lives in the father's house, goods belong to the father.
This follows the principle that possession and headship determine ownership.
4
Summarise the common principle
In all scenarios, the key factors are: who has possession, who is the head of the household, and customary use of the items.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن اختلف الزوجان ؛ أي ادعى كلُّ واحدٍ منهما أنَّ الكلَّ له، قيَّد باختلافهما ؛ للاحتراز عن اختلاف نساء الزوج دونه، فإنَّ متاعَ النِّساء بينهنَّ على السواء إن كنَّ في بيت واحد ، وإن كانت كل واحدة منهنَّ في بيت على حدة، فما [في] بيت كل واحدة امرأة بينها وبين زوجها على ما ذكر، ولا يشترك بعضهنَّ مع بعض. ذكره العلامةُ الطَّحْطَاوي (۱) نقلاً عن «خزانة الأكمل» و«الخانية». وذكر في «السراج الوهاج»: لو كان لرجل أربع نسوة، فإن كنَّ في بيت واحدٍ فمتاعُ البيت بين النسوة أرباعاً ، وإنّ كُنَّ في بيوت مختلفة فمتاعُ كل بيت بعينه بينه وبين المرأة التي تسكنه. انتهى. وللاحتراز عن اختلافِ الأب وابنه فيما في البيت ، قال في «خزانة الأكمل»: قال أبو يوسف له : إذا كان الأب في عيال الابن في بيته، فالمتاع كله للابن، كما لو كان الابن في بيت الأب وعياله، فمتاعُ البيت للأب. انتهى. ثم قال : قال محمد الله : رجل زوج ابنته ، وهـي وخـتـنـه في داره وعياله ، ثـمّ اختلفوا في متاع البيت فهو للأب ؛ لأنه في بيته وفي يده ، ولهم ما عليهم من الثياب. انتهى. (۱) في حاشيته على الدر(٣: ٣٠٥).
And if the two spouses dispute, meaning each claims that all of it belongs to him, it is restricted to their dispute to exclude the dispute of the wives of the husband without him. For the goods of the wives are equally shared if they are in one house; and if each of them is in a separate house, then what is in the house of each wife is between her and her husband as mentioned, and they do not share with each other. Al-Tah'tawi mentioned it quoting from Khizanat al-Akmal and al-Khaniyya. And it is mentioned in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: If a man has four wives, if they are in one house, the household goods are divided among the wives in quarters; and if they are in different houses, then the goods of each specific house are between him and the wife who resides there. End. And to exclude the dispute of the father and his son over what is in the house, it is said in Khizanat al-Akmal: Abu Yusuf said: If the father is dependent on the son in his house, then all goods belong to the son, just as if the son is in the house of the father and his dependents, then the household goods are for the father. End. Then Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) said: A man married his daughter, and she and her son-in-law are in his house and dependents, then they dispute over the household goods; it is for the father because it is in his house and possession, and for them are what they wear. End.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage provides rulings for resolving disputes over household goods (mata' al-bayt) between spouses and between relatives. The primary rule: when husband and wife dispute ownership of items in the house, items that are typically used by women (like jewelry, women's clothing) are attributed to the wife, and items typical for men to the husband. For items common to both, the word of the one in possession is taken. The text also addresses cases of multiple wives: if they live together, goods are divided equally among them; if each has a separate house, the goods in each house belong to that wife and the husband exclusively. Additionally, for disputes between father and son: the one whose house it is and who has control is considered the owner. If the father is dependent on the son, the son owns; if the son lives in the father's house, the father owns. These rulings are based on the principle that possession and customary use determine ownership in dispute.

Key Terms
متاع (mata'): Household goods, furniture, and belongings.
زوجان (zawjan): The two spouses (husband and wife).

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) All goods belong to the husband.
D) Goods are divided based on the value of each house.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, attribution of household goods in disputes hinges on possession, living arrangements, and dependency, ensuring clarity and reducing conflict.
