Determining Ownership of Household Goods in Spousal Disputes (Hanafi Rules)
Ruling and commentary on spousal disputes over household goods without evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for spousal disputes over household items when neither spouse has evidence, including the wedding night exception.

Core Idea
Without evidence, items fit for women go to her; items fit for men or both go to him, but custom may reverse this on the wedding night.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains how a Hanafi judge resolves disputes between spouses over household goods (mataa') when neither can produce evidence. The default presumption (zaahir) assigns items based on suitability, but custom (urf) and the husband's dominant hand (yad) play decisive roles. However, the wedding night creates an exception rooted in prevailing custom, illustrating how Hanafi fiqh blends textual presumption with social reality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute scenario
The passage describes a conflict over possessions (mataa') during marriage or after death, with no evidence for either party.
2
Apply the suitability test
Classify each item: exclusively for women? exclusively for men? suitable for both? The presumption favors the one to whom the item is naturally suited.
3
Consider the 'hand' principle
For items suitable for both, the husband is believed because his hand is dominant over the wife's possessions (her hand is within his hand). But this is a qiraa (presumption), not absolute.
Qiraa = ظاهر
4
Check for custom override on wedding night
If the dispute arises on the wedding night, custom (urf) dictates that bedding, chests, and servants typically come with the wife, so she is believed for those items.
Custom overrides general presumption
5
Handle admissions and special cases
If the wife admits the husband bought the item, she needs evidence to prove transfer. Also, if the husband is known to trade in such items, he is believed.

But al-Akmal said in al-Khazana: If the woman dies on the night she was wedded to him in his house, it is not appropriate to make the items of bedding, women's jewelry, and what befits them for the husband, unless the man is known to trade in a type thereof, then it is for him. End. Likewise if they differ during life regarding what suits both, the statement is for him except if the difference is on the wedding night, then the statement is for her regarding bedding and the like, due to the prevailing custom that bedding, chests, and servants come with the woman; this should be relied upon for fatwa, unless there is a text regarding its ruling on the wedding night to the contrary, then it is followed. Thus in al-Bahr. [2] His statement: And no evidence for either of them. He conditioned it because if either has evidence, judgment is given by what it proves. (1) In his Hashiya (3:305). Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn that deals with disputes between spouses over household goods (متاع) when neither has evidence. The general rule is: items exclusively for women go to the woman with her oath; items exclusively for men or suitable for both go to the man with his oath. The presumption (ظاهر) is the key evidence, but this presumption can be overridden by custom or if the man is a merchant of such items. The commentator elaborates on exceptions, particularly the wedding night, where custom dictates that items like bedding are usually brought by the wife, so she is believed. The passage also addresses when the woman admits the husband bought the item—then she needs evidence to prove transfer of ownership. This is a typical Hanafi approach: combining legal presumption with urf (custom).

Key Terms
متاع (mataa'): Household goods and possessions as used in marital dispute context.
ظاهر (zaahir): The apparent presumption or external evidence that indicates ownership based on common use or possession.
يد (yad): Possession or control; here, the husband's hand is considered dominant over the wife's possessions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Never; the husband is always believed.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, spousal disputes over household goods without evidence are resolved by the suitability presumption and hand dominance, but custom can create exceptions like the wedding night.

Equal Division for Claimants with Similar Evidence
Proof for dividing property equally when both claimants have evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two claimants present equal evidence for ownership and cite the Sunnah proof for equal division.

Core Idea
When evidence is equal, the property is divided equally — not by lots, because lots resemble gambling.

In disputes over ownership, what happens when both sides bring equally strong evidence? The Hanafi school addresses this through prophetic precedents. This lesson explores why the judge divides the property equally rather than using lots, and how this ruling is grounded in authentic hadith. We will see how the school distinguishes between using lots for initial entitlement (which is prohibited) and for dividing already-owned property (which is permissible).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Dispute Scenario
Recognize the situation: two claimants each present valid evidence (bayyinah) that a specific item is their exclusive property. The evidence is equal in strength and cannot be reconciled.
The item must be divisible (e.g., a camel, land); indivisible items (e.g., a marriage contract) have different rules.
2
Examine the Hanafi Proof
The author cites three narrations: (1) Tamim ibn Tarafa: Prophet divided the camel between the two. (2) Abu Musa al-Ash'ari: Prophet divided the camel in half. (3) Abu al-Darda: divided a horse in half. These form the main evidence.
The hadith about the Prophet casting lots is dismissed as weak (mursal, weak narrator).
3
Understand the Rejection of Lots
Using lots (qur'ah) to determine initial entitlement is akin to gambling (qimar), which was abrogated. The Hanafi school avoids lots in ownership disputes because they create risk without underlying right.
This distinguishes the Hanafi position from schools that allow lots in such cases.
4
Distinguish Division of Joint Property
If the property is already jointly owned (e.g., inheritance), the judge can use lots to assign shares as a means to appease the parties and avoid bias—this is not considered gambling because the entitlement is already established.
Thus lots are permissible in partition but not in initial ownership contests.
5
Apply the Ruling
When two credible witnesses from each side testify, and no other evidence outweighs, the judge must divide the property equally. If the property is indivisible, it is sold and the proceeds divided.
This ruling is mufta bihi (operative for fatwa) in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زبدة النهاية لعمدة الرعاية فساهم بينهما رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وقال : اللهم اقض بينهما). ورواه عبد الرزاق أيضا مرسلاً ، ورواه أبو داود في «مراسيله»، قال في «نصب الراية»: ومن جهته ذكره عبد الحق في «أحكامه» ، وقال : هذا مرسل وضعيف، قال إن إبراهيم بن أبي يحيى الأسلمي متروك. انتهى. (1) ولنا : ما روى ابن أبي شيبة في «مصنِّفه»: عن أبي الأحوص، عن سماك، عن تميم بن طرفة : «إنّ رجلين ادعيا بعيراً، فأقام كلُّ واحدٍ منهما البينة أنه له، فقضى النبي به بینهما (۳). وما أخرجه أبو داود في سننه»، وأحمد في مسنده»، والحاكم في «مستدركه»، وقال : صحيح على شرط الشيخين، وقال المنذري : رجال إسناده كلهم ثقات ، ولفظهم : عن همام، عن قتادة، عن سعيد بن أبي بردة، عن أبيه، عن جده أبي موسى الأشعري : «إن رجلين أدعيا بعيراً على عهد النبي ، فبعث كل واحد منهما شاهدین ، فقسمه النبي بينهما نصفين (٤) ، وما رواه إسحاق بن راهويه في مسنده» : عن وكيع عن سفيان، عن علقمة، عن مرتد عن عبد الرحمن بن أبي ليلى قال : جاء رجلان يختصمان إلى أبي الدرداء في فرس، أقام كل واحد البينة أنها نتجت عنده، فقضى به بينهما نصفين، ثم قال : ما أحوجكما إلى مثل سلسلة بني إسرائيل، كانت تنزل فتأخذ عنق الظالم انتهى. واستعمال القرعة في وقت كان القمار ،مباحاً ، ثم انتسخ ذلك بحرمة القمار ؛ لأنَّ تعيين المستحق بمنزلة الاستحقاق ابتداء، فكما أن تعليق الاستحقاق بخروج القرعة يكون قماراً، فكذلك تعيين المستحق بخروج القرعة يكون قماراً أيضاً. بخلاف قسمة للمال المشترك، فللقاضي هناك ولاية التعيين من غير قرعة، وإنما يقرع تطيباً لقلوبهما، ونفياً لتهمة الميل عن نفسي، فلا يكون ذلك في معنى القمار،
Cream of the End for Assistance of the Care. Then the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, cast lots between them and said: 'O Allah, decide between them.' And Abd al-Razzaq also narrated it as mursal, and Abu Dawud narrated it in his Marasil. Their wording is: From Hammam, from Qatada, from Sa'id ibn Abi Burda, from his father, from his grandfather Abu Musa al-Ash'ari: 'Two men claimed a camel during the time of the Prophet, peace be upon him, and each sent two witnesses, so the Prophet divided it between them in half.' (4) And what Ishaq ibn Rahawayh narrated in his Musnad from Waki', from Sufyan, from Alqama, from Murthid, from Abd al-Rahman ibn Abi Layla, he said: Two men came to Abu al-Darda disputing about a horse. Each brought evidence that it was born in his possession, so he judged it between them in half. Then he said: 'How much you need something like the chain of the Children of Israel, which would descend and seize the neck of the oppressor.' End. And the use of lots at a time when gambling was permissible, then that was abrogated by the prohibition of gambling, because assigning the rightful owner is like initial entitlement. Just as suspending entitlement on the outcome of lots is gambling, similarly assigning the rightful owner by the outcome of lots is also gambling. Unlike the division of joint property, because the judge there has authority to assign without lots, and he only uses lots to pacify their hearts and to dispel the suspicion of bias from himself, so that is not equivalent to gambling.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hanafi school on how to resolve a dispute when two claimants present equal evidence for ownership of a single property (e.g., a camel or horse). The author first cites a hadith where the Prophet cast lots between two claimants, but then dismisses that narration as weak (mursal and with a weak narrator). He then presents three stronger narrations: one from Tamim ibn Tarafa where the Prophet judged that the camel be shared between them; another from Abu Musa al-Ash'ari where the Prophet divided the camel in half; and a third from a case decided by Abu al-Darda where he divided a horse in half. These establish that the proper Hanafi ruling is equal division, not by lots. The author then addresses an objection: some might argue that using lots (qur'ah) is permissible based on the first hadith, but the response is that even if lots were used in the past, that was when gambling (qimar) was permissible. Since gambling is now forbidden, using lots to determine entitlement (i.e., who is the rightful owner) would be akin to gambling and is therefore not allowed. However, the author distinguishes the division of joint property (e.g., inheritance) where the judge has the authority to assign shares without lots; he may still use lots to satisfy the parties and avoid accusations of bias, and that is not considered gambling because it does not determine initial entitlement. Thus, the Hanafi position is clear: when two parties present equal evidence for exclusive ownership, the property is shared equally, and lots are not used. This ruling is based on the principle that the item is capable of being shared (as opposed to marriage, which cannot be shared).

Key Terms
قرعة (qur'ah): Casting lots to determine entitlement or allocation.
قمار (qimar): Gambling; prohibited after originally being permissible (in this context).
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence, typically two male witnesses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling when two claimants present equal evidence for ownership of the same camel?
A) The camel is given to the one who produces the older evidence.
B) The camel is divided equally between them.
C) The judge casts lots to decide the owner.
D) The camel is kept in trust until a decisive proof appears.

Key Takeaway
When two claimants present equal evidence for a divisible property, the Hanafi school rules it be divided equally, rejecting lots as resembling gambling.

Preference for External Evidence and Exception for Offspring
States principle, hadith exception, and analogy for non-recurring causes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default rule in Hanafi evidence law regarding external versus possessor evidence and identify the exception for offspring and analogous non-recurring causes.

Core Idea
External evidence is preferred, but the Prophet's ruling on a camel's offspring creates an exception for all non-recurring ownership claims.

In disputes over possession, Hanafi law generally favours the evidence of the party not in possession. However, a specific hadith about a camel's offspring established an exception. This lesson unpacks that hadith, the principle it establishes, and how later jurists extended it to similar non-recurring claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default rule
Understand that the original principle in Hanafi law is to prefer the evidence of the external claimant (bavyinat al-kharij) over the possessor.
This is the baseline rule from which the exception is derived.
2
Examine the hadith exception
Read the hadith of Jabir about the camel's offspring. Note that the Prophet judged in favor of the possessor despite external evidence.
The hadith is well-known (mashhur) but ahad, so it creates an exception rather than a new rule.
3
Understand the reasoning for the exception
The exception is based on the nature of offspring (nitaaj) as a unique, non-recurring claim of original ownership.
The hadith is specific, but the 'illa (effective cause) is the non-recurring nature of the claim.
4
Apply the analogy to other non-recurring causes
Identify cases like first-time weaving, milking, cheese-making, and shearing wool as analogous to offspring because they involve claims of original ownership that cannot be repeated identically.
These are included in the exception by qiyas.
5
Distinguish recurring causes
Understand that recurring causes (e.g., shearing, building, farming) do not fall under the exception; external evidence is preferred there.
If unclear, experts are consulted; if still ambiguous, external evidence prevails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنَّ القضاء ببينة الخارج هو الأصل، وإنّما عدلنا عنه بخبر النتاج ، وهو ما روى جابر بن عبد الله له : «إنّ رجلاً ادّعى ناقة في يد رجل، وأقام البينة أنها نتجت عندها، وأقام الذي هي في يده أنها ناقته نَتَجَها فقضى بها رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم للذي هي في يده»)، وهذا حديث مشهور، فصارت مسألة النتاج مخصوصة، كما في «البحر» نقلا عن «المحيط». وألحقوا بالنتاج ما لا يتكرر سببه ؛ لكونه في معناه ؛ لأنه دعوى أولية الملك كالنسج في الثياب التي لا تنسج إلا مرَّةً كالثياب القطنية، وغزل القطن، وحلب اللبن، واتخاذ الجبن، وجز الصوف. وإن كان سببا يتكرّر لا يكون في معناه، فيقضى به للخارج بمنزلة المطلق، مثل الجز، والبناء، والغرس، وزراعة الحنطة والحبوب، فإن أشكل يرجع إلى أهل الخبرة ، فإن أشكل عليهم قضى به للخارج ؛ لأنَّ القضاء بينته هو الأصل، وإنما عدلنا بخبر النتاج كذا في «المنح»، وغيره.
Know that judgment based on the external person's evidence is the original principle, and we only deviate from it due to the hadith of the offspring, which is what Jabir ibn Abdullah narrated: 'A man claimed a she-camel in the hand of another man, and brought evidence that it gave birth in his presence, and the one in whose hand it was brought evidence that it was his own she-camel that he had bred, so the Messenger of Allah صلى الله عليه وسلم judged in favor of the one in whose hand it was.' This is a well-known hadith, so the case of offspring became an exception, as in 'al-Bahr' quoting from 'al-Muhit'. And they analogized to offspring what does not have a recurring cause, because it is in its meaning; since it is a claim of original ownership, like weaving in garments that are only woven once, such as cotton garments, cotton spinning, milking milk, making cheese, and shearing wool. And if the cause is recurring, it is not in its meaning, so judgment is given for the external person as in the absolute case, such as shearing, building, planting, and cultivating wheat and grains; if it is unclear, experts are consulted; if it remains unclear to them, judgment is given for the external person because judgment based on his evidence is the original principle, and we only deviate due to the hadith of offspring. Thus it is stated in 'al-Munah' and others.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, establishing the general principle and its exception in Hanafi fiqh. The author states that the default rule in disputes over possession is to prefer the evidence of the external claimant (بينة الخارج), i.e., the person not in possession. This is derived from the general principles of evidence. However, an exception is made based on the famous hadith of Jabir ibn Abdullah regarding a camel's offspring. In that case, the Prophet judged in favor of the person in possession (ذي اليد) when both parties brought evidence of the camel giving birth in their ownership. Hence, in cases involving offspring (نتاج) or similar non-recurring causes of original ownership, the evidence of the possessor is preferred. The commentator then explains that other non-recurring causes, such as weaving a garment for the first time, spinning cotton, milking, cheese-making, and shearing wool—which cannot be repeated identically—are analogized to offspring. Conversely, recurring causes like shearing (which can be repeated yearly), building, planting, and cultivation are not treated as exceptions, and the external's evidence prevails. If there is doubt, experts are consulted; if still unclear, the external's evidence is accepted. This is a clear example of istihsan based on the specific hadith.

Key Terms
بينة الخارج (bayyinat al-kharij): Evidence presented by the claimant who does not possess the object in dispute.
النتاج (al-nitaaj): The act of an animal giving birth; used to refer to the offspring itself in legal context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The possessor's evidence is always preferred.
B) The external claimant's evidence is preferred.
C) The evidence of the one who proves offspring is preferred regardless.
D) The case is decided by expert opinion.

Key Takeaway
The default preference for external evidence yields to the possessor's evidence in offspring and analogous non-recurring ownership claims, based on the hadith and qiyas.

Priority of Offspring Evidence in Court (al-Hidayah)
Expansion of rulings on offspring evidence priority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule that offspring evidence takes precedence over general ownership evidence and explain when a prior judgment can be overturned due to new offspring evidence.

Core Idea
Offspring evidence proves original ownership — it overrides general ownership proof and can overturn a final judgment.

In this passage from al-Hidayah and al-Qunyah, we examine the powerful evidentiary status of offspring (natal) evidence in Hanafi law. We see that when one party offers proof of ownership (like a sale) and another proves that the animal or property was born from his stock, the offspring evidence wins. Moreover, even after a judgment, new offspring evidence can overturn the decision. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of evidence
Recognize that general ownership evidence (e.g., purchase deed) is different from offspring evidence (i.e., the thing was born from one's property). Offspring evidence is considered more definitive.
2
Understand the principle of awwaliyyat al-milk
Offspring evidence proves that the claimant had original ownership of the thing. The other party, if they have a claim, could only have acquired it from that original owner. This makes offspring evidence stronger.
Original ownership is the root; any transfer is secondary.
3
Apply to the possessor vs. outsider scenario
If the possessor brings offspring evidence, he wins over an outsider with general ownership proof. The same rule applies if both are outsiders; the one with offspring evidence wins.
The rule is consistent regardless of who is in possession.
4
Apply to post-judgment third party scenario
After a judgment in favor of the possessor based on offspring, a third party not involved in the first case can bring his own offspring evidence and win the property, unless the first possessor re-presents his evidence.
The third party is not bound by the prior judgment.
5
Apply to a losing party bringing new offspring evidence
If a person lost a case based on general ownership evidence, he can later bring offspring evidence to overturn the judgment. Offspring evidence is like a definitive text (nass), while the earlier judgment was based on ijtihad.
This shows the high evidentiary weight of offspring evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي «القنية»: تقدم بينة ذي اليد إذا ادّعى أوليَّة الملك بالنتاج عنده، فكذا إذا ادعاه عند مورثه. انتهى. (۱) قوله : أو برهنا ...الخ ; ولو أقام أحدهما البينة على الملك، والآخر على النتاج، فصاحب النتاج أولى أيهما كان؛ لأنَّ بينته قامت على أولية الملك، فلا يثبتُ الملك للآخر إلا بالتلقي من جهته، وكذلك إذا كان الدعوى بين خارجين، فبينة النتاج أولى لما ذكرنا. ولو قضى بالنتاج لصاحب اليد ، ثم أقام ثالث البينة على النتاج يقضى له إلا أن يعيدها ذو اليد ؛ لأنَّ الثالث لم يصر مقضيّاً عليه بتلك القضية، وكذا المقضي عليه بالملك المطلق إذا أقام البينة على النتاج تقبل، وينقض القضاء به ؛ لأنه بمنزلة النص، والأول بمنزلة الاجتهاد. كذا في «الهداية).
And in 'al-Qunyah': The evidence of the possessor is given precedence when he claims original ownership through offspring in his possession, likewise if he claims it through his predecessor. End. Then his saying: 'or they prove... etc.'; And if one of them brings evidence of ownership and the other brings evidence of offspring, then the one with offspring evidence is given precedence, whichever of them it is, because his evidence is based on original ownership, so ownership for the other can only be established by receiving from him. Similarly, if the dispute is between two external claimants, the offspring evidence is given precedence for what we mentioned. And if judgment is given for offspring in favor of the possessor, then a third party brings evidence of offspring, judgment is given for him unless the possessor brings it again, because the third party was not a defendant in that judgment. Likewise, the one against whom judgment was given for absolute ownership, if he brings evidence of offspring, it is accepted and the judgment is overturned, because it is like a text, and the first is like ijtihad. Thus in 'al-Hidayah'.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of rulings related to offspring evidence. The author first quotes from al-Qunyah that the possessor's evidence is preferred when he claims original ownership through offspring, whether the offspring occurred in his own possession or that of his predecessor (like a seller). Then, the commentator explains a scenario: if one party proves general ownership (e.g., through purchase) and the other proves offspring, the offspring evidence prevails because it indicates original ownership that cannot be transferred except through the owner. This applies whether the possessor is the one with offspring or an external claimant. Next, if a judgment has been given in favor of the possessor based on offspring, and then a third party (not involved in the first case) brings evidence of offspring, that third party gets the object unless the possessor re-presents his evidence. The reasoning is that the third party was not bound by the prior judgment. Similarly, if a judgment was given against a person based on general ownership (i.e., he lost), and then he brings evidence of offspring, that evidence is accepted and the judgment is overturned. This is because the offspring evidence is considered strong (like a definitive text), whereas the prior judgment was based on weaker inference (ijtihad). This shows the high evidentiary value of offspring evidence in Hanafi law.

Key Terms
أولية الملك (awwaliyyat al-milk): Original ownership, as opposed to acquired ownership through transfer.
مقضى عليه (maqdi 'alayh): The party against whom a judgment is given, i.e., the defendant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The previous judgment stands, and the new evidence is inadmissible.
B) The new evidence is accepted, and the judgment is overturned.
C) The new evidence is only accepted if it is from the same court.
D) The judgment is upheld, but the loser can appeal.

Key Takeaway
Offspring evidence is the strongest indicator of original ownership and can overturn even a final judgment in Hanafi law.

Classifying Causes of Ownership: Recurring vs Non-Recurring
Defines and classifies causes of ownership for evidentiary preference

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given cause of ownership as recurring or non-recurring and state the evidentiary consequence for disputes.

Core Idea
A non-recurring cause of ownership is a stronger indicator of original possession than a recurring one.

In ownership disputes, the type of cause by which a person claims an item can determine whose claim is preferred. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya defines and distinguishes two types of ownership causes: recurring and non-recurring. Understanding this classification is essential for applying the Hanafi rule of evidence preference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Read the passage and note that the author explicitly divides causes of ownership (sabab al-milk) into two types: non-recurring (lā yatakarrar) and recurring (yatakarrar).
2
Distinguish the legal characteristic
Non-recurring causes cannot be performed again identically by another person after the first act; they grant a unique claim. Recurring causes can be repeated, opening the possibility of multiple concurrent claims.
The distinction is legal-probabilistic, not physical.
3
Apply to the given examples
For each example in the passage (weaving cotton, spinning, milking, making cheese – non-recurring; silk brocade, building, planting, cultivating – recurring), explain why it fits its category.
Note that 'offspring' (al-nitaaj) is the key non-recurring example for the following ruling.
4
Note the evidentiary consequence
Non-recurring causes are stronger proof of original ownership; recurring causes are weaker and may lead to competing claims. This affects which party's evidence is preferred in a dispute.
This classification is used in legal maxims such as 'al-kharāj bi'l-ḍamān' but here it is applied to initial ownership.
5
Connect to the immediate usage
The passage concludes by defining 'offspring' (nitaaj) as the act of parturition, which is a non-recurring cause. This sets up the rule that the offspring belongs to the owner of the mother.
The legal ruling itself is covered in a separate lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٢]قوله : على سبب ملك لا يتكرر ; اعلم أنَّ السبب في الملكِ على نوعين : ١. سبب لا يتكرر كالنسج في الثياب التي لا تنسج إلا مرة، كنسج الثياب القطنية، وغزل القطن، وحلب اللبن، واتخاذ الجبن والكبد. ٢. وسبب يتكرّر كالخز ، فإنّه ينسج مرَّةً بعد مرة ؛ لأنه ينسج ثم يكتب، فينغزل ثم ينسج ثانياً، وكالبناء، والغرس، وزراعة الحنطة والحبوب، فإنَّ النخل مثلاً يغرس ثمَّ غير مرة، فقد يغرس إنسان ثم يقلعه غيره، ويغرسه، والزرعُ قد يكون غير مرة، فإنَّ الحنطة قد تزرع في الأرض ، ثم يعزل التراب، فيميز الحنطة منها، ثم تزرع ثانياً. كذا في «الكافي». [٣] قوله : كالنتاج ; النتاج بالكسر ، اسم يشتملُ وضع البهائم من الغنم وغيرها، وإذا وَلِيَ الإِنسانُ ناقة ماخِضَاً حتى تضع ، يقال : نَتَجَها نتجاً من باب ضرب، فالإنسانُ
[2] His saying: 'on a non-recurring cause of ownership'; Know that the cause of ownership is of two types: 1. A non-recurring cause, like weaving in garments that are only woven once, such as weaving cotton garments, spinning cotton, milking milk, making cheese and liver. 2. A recurring cause, like silk brocade (khazz), for it is woven time after time; because it is woven, then written [؟], then unraveled and woven again; and like building, planting, and cultivating wheat and grains. For example, palm trees are planted then [replanted] more than once: a person may plant, then another uproots and replants. And cultivation may be more than once: wheat may be sown in the earth, then the soil is removed, the wheat is separated from it, then sown again. Thus in 'al-Kafi'. [3] His saying: 'like offspring'; Offspring (al-nitaaj, with kasra) is a term encompassing the birth of animals from sheep and others. When a person attends to a pregnant she-camel until she gives birth, it is said: natajaha natajan (from the verb daraba), so the person...
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of causes of ownership, relevant to the earlier discussion of evidence preference. The commentator explains that causes of ownership are of two types: non-recurring and recurring. Non-recurring causes cannot be repeated in the exact same way by another person; examples include first-time weaving of a cotton garment (which cannot be woven again once finished), spinning cotton, milking an animal (only one act), making cheese, and even 'liver' (likely a specific item with a single original creation). These are analogized to offspring because they indicate original ownership that cannot be replicated. Recurring causes, on the other hand, can be performed repeatedly by different owners; examples include 'khazz' (a fabric that can be woven, undone, and re-woven), building (which can be demolished and rebuilt), planting (trees can be uprooted and replanted), and cultivation (wheat can be harvested, separated, and resown). These do not carry the same evidentiary weight because they do not prove original ownership as decisively. Finally, the commentator defines 'نتاج' (offspring) as a term covering the birth of animals, with the root verb 'نتج' meaning to assist in parturition. This clarifies the core concept used in the legal ruling.

Key Terms
سبب الملك (sabab al-milk): The event or act by which ownership in a specific item is originally established.
النتاج (al-nitaaj): The giving birth of animals; also the young produced.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Planting a date palm.
B) Weaving a cotton garment for the first time.
C) Building a house.
D) Cultivating wheat.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, causes of ownership are divided into recurring and non-recurring; non-recurring causes (like the birth of animals) carry stronger evidentiary weight in ownership disputes.

Divisions in Claims: 'Awal vs Mudaraba
Distinguishing 'awal and mudaraba in joint property claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the methods of 'awal and mudaraba in dividing joint property and identify which method applies in a given scenario.

Core Idea
'Awal reduces shares when total exceeds denominator; mudaraba divides by right ratio in co-owned property.

When two parties have claims on the same property, Islamic law provides two proportional division methods. This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah clarifies when each is used. Understanding the distinction is key to correctly resolving inheritance and debt claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two methods
The passage introduces 'awal and mudaraba as methods for dividing property when multiple claims exist.
2
Analyze the right structures
Distinguish between rights in a shared part versus rights in the whole. The method depends on whether the right is in the dhimmah (liability) or in an undivided share.
3
Associate method with scenario
When rights are in the same shared part, division is analogous to inheritance and uses 'awal. When one party has the whole right, it is not inheritance and mudaraba is used.
The passage contrasts these two scenarios.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الدعوى باب دعوى الرجلين
وهما: اعتبرا طريق العول والمضاربة
وكذا [إذا كان حق أحدهما في البعض الشائع، وحقُّ الآخر في الكل لم يكن في معنى الميراث؛ لأن صاحب الكل يزاحم صاحبَ البعض في كلِّ شيء، أما صاحب البعض لا يزاحم صاحبه في الكلّ، فلم يكن في معنى الميراث؛ ولأنَّ حق كل واحد منهما إذا كان في البعض الشائع وما يأخذ كلّ واحدٍ منهما بحكم القسمة مفرز، وإنه غير الشائع كان المأخوذ بدلّ حقه لا أصل حقه، فيكون في معنى الميراث والتركة التي اجتمعت فيهما الديون.
وفي مسائل القسمة إنّما وجبت بحق ثابت في الذمة؛ لأن حق كلّ واحدٍ منهما في موجب الجناية، وموجبها يكون في الذمة، فكانت القسمة فيها عولية، فعلى هذا تخرّج المسائل. ذكره شيخنا العلامةُ الطَّحْطَاوي في «حاشيته على الدر المختار». قوله: اعتبرا طريق العول؛ وذلك إذا اجتمع في مخرج فروض كثيرة بحيث لا يكفي المجموع، فيحتاج إلى العول كما في امرأة ماتت وتركت زوجاً وأختاً لاب وأم وأختاً لأب، للزوج النصف، وللأخت لأب وأم النصف، وللأخت لأب السدس، تكملة للثلثين، فتعولُ الفريضة إلى سبعة، وكانت في الأصل من ستة.
والعولُ في اللغة يستعمل بمعنى الميل؛ أي الجور، يقال: فلان يعول علي؛ أي يميل جائراً، وبمعنى الغلبة: يقال عِيل صَبْرُه؛ أي غلب، وبمعنى الرفع يقال: عالَ الميزان إذا رفعه.
وفي الاصطلاح: أن يزاد على المخرج شيء من أجزائه عن فرض، وغرضه أن يدخل النقصان في فرائض جميع الورثة على نسبة واحدة، وتفصيله في رسائل الفرائض.
Book of Claims, Chapter of the Claim of Two Men. They: considered the method of 'awal and mudaraba. Likewise, if the right of one of them is in a shared part, and the right of the other is in the whole, it is not in the sense of inheritance; because the owner of the whole competes with the owner of the part in everything, while the owner of the part does not compete with his companion in the whole, so it is not in the sense of inheritance. And because the right of each one of them when it is in a shared part, and what each one of them takes by virtue of division is separated, and it is not the shared part, so what is taken is a substitute for his right, not the original right, so it is in the sense of inheritance and the estate that combines the two debts. In cases of division, it is only established by a right fixed in the dhimmah (liability); because the right of each one of them is in the obligation of the injury, and its obligation is in the dhimmah, so the division therein is 'awaliyyah, and upon this the cases are derived. Mentioned by our teacher, the scholar al-Tahṭāwī in his gloss on al-Durr al-Mukhtār. His saying: They considered the method of 'awal; that is when many prescribed shares combine in a denominator such that the total does not suffice, so 'awal is needed, as in a woman who died leaving a husband, a full sister, and a consanguine sister: for the husband half, for the full sister half, for the consanguine sister one-sixth as a completion of two-thirds, so the obligation increases to seven, while originally it was from six. 'Awal linguistically is used to mean inclination, i.e., injustice; it is said: so-and-so ya'ūlu 'alayya, i.e., leans unjustly. And to mean overcoming: it is said: 'īla ṣabruhu, i.e., was overcome. And to mean raising: it is said: 'āla al-mīzān, if he raised it. Technically: it is to increase the denominator by something from its fractions due to a share, and its purpose is to introduce deficiency into the shares of all heirs in one proportion. Its detail is in treatises on inheritance.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory expansion from the commentary on the Wiqayah. It begins by quoting the matn: 'They considered the method of 'awal and mudaraba.' The commentator then clarifies the application of these two methods in claims and division. The ruling states that when one party has a right in a shared portion and the other in the whole, it is not analogous to inheritance because the owner of the whole competes with the owner of the part in everything, but not vice versa. However, if both have rights in a shared part and take separated portions by division, what they take is a substitute for their right, analogous to inheritance and debts. In division cases, the right is in the dhimmah (liability) because it arises from an injury obligation, so division follows the method of 'awal. The commentator then defines 'awal both linguistically and technically: linguistically it means inclination, overcoming, or raising; technically, it is increasing the denominator to accommodate fractions when shares exceed the total, reducing all shares proportionally. The example given is a woman leaving a husband (½), a full sister (½), and a consanguine sister (1/6 as completion of 2/3), totaling 7/6, so the denominator is raised from 6 to 7. This is the standard Hanafi position on 'awal in inheritance.

Key Terms
العول (al-'awal): Increasing the denominator of prescribed shares to accommodate a total exceeding the unit, reducing each share proportionally.
المضاربة (al-muḍārabah): A method of proportional division where each claimant receives a share commensurate with his right, often used in partnership or when rights are in the same property.
شائع (shā'i'): Undivided or shared (as in a portion of property not yet allocated).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is division classified as 'awaliyyah?
A) When one claimant's right is in the whole and the other's in a shared part
B) When both claimants' rights are in a shared part and fixed in the dhimmah
C) When property is divided by mutual consent
D) When there is only one claimant

Key Takeaway
The choice between 'awal and mudaraba depends on whether the right is in the dhimmah (liability) or in a shared part of property.

The Mechanism of 'Awal in Inheritance
Defining and demonstrating 'awal in inheritance division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the linguistic and technical definitions of 'awal and calculate the 'awal denominator in a given inheritance scenario.

Core Idea
'Awal is increasing the denominator when shares exceed the total, reducing all shares proportionally.

After understanding the distinction between 'awal and mudaraba, this passage provides a detailed definition of 'awal, including its linguistic roots and technical application. The example of a woman dying and leaving a husband, full sister, and consanguine sister illustrates the arithmetic.

Guided Path
1
Learn the linguistic meanings
The passage gives three meanings: inclination/oppression, overcoming, and raising. These help contextualize the technical term.
2
Understand the technical definition
'Awal technically means increasing the denominator by adding its own fractions to accommodate prescribed shares that exceed the total.
3
Work through the example
For a woman leaving husband (1/2), full sister (1/2), consanguine sister (1/6), the total is 1/2+1/2+1/6 = 7/6. The original denominator 6 is increased to 7.
All shares are reduced proportionally: husband gets 3/7, sister 3/7, consanguine sister 1/7.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الدعوى باب دعوى الرجلين
وهما: اعتبرا طريق العول والمضاربة
وكذا [إذا كان حق أحدهما في البعض الشائع، وحقُّ الآخر في الكل لم يكن في معنى الميراث؛ لأن صاحب الكل يزاحم صاحبَ البعض في كلِّ شيء، أما صاحب البعض لا يزاحم صاحبه في الكلّ، فلم يكن في معنى الميراث؛ ولأنَّ حق كل واحد منهما إذا كان في البعض الشائع وما يأخذ كلّ واحدٍ منهما بحكم القسمة مفرز، وإنه غير الشائع كان المأخوذ بدلّ حقه لا أصل حقه، فيكون في معنى الميراث والتركة التي اجتمعت فيهما الديون.
وفي مسائل القسمة إنّما وجبت بحق ثابت في الذمة؛ لأن حق كلّ واحدٍ منهما في موجب الجناية، وموجبها يكون في الذمة، فكانت القسمة فيها عولية، فعلى هذا تخرّج المسائل. ذكره شيخنا العلامةُ الطَّحْطَاوي في «حاشيته على الدر المختار». قوله: اعتبرا طريق العول؛ وذلك إذا اجتمع في مخرج فروض كثيرة بحيث لا يكفي المجموع، فيحتاج إلى العول كما في امرأة ماتت وتركت زوجاً وأختاً لاب وأم وأختاً لأب، للزوج النصف، وللأخت لأب وأم النصف، وللأخت لأب السدس، تكملة للثلثين، فتعولُ الفريضة إلى سبعة، وكانت في الأصل من ستة.
والعولُ في اللغة يستعمل بمعنى الميل؛ أي الجور، يقال: فلان يعول علي؛ أي يميل جائراً، وبمعنى الغلبة: يقال عِيل صَبْرُه؛ أي غلب، وبمعنى الرفع يقال: عالَ الميزان إذا رفعه.
وفي الاصطلاح: أن يزاد على المخرج شيء من أجزائه عن فرض، وغرضه أن يدخل النقصان في فرائض جميع الورثة على نسبة واحدة، وتفصيله في رسائل الفرائض.
Book of Claims, Chapter of the Claim of Two Men. They: considered the method of 'awal and mudaraba. Likewise, if the right of one of them is in a shared part, and the right of the other is in the whole, it is not in the sense of inheritance; because the owner of the whole competes with the owner of the part in everything, while the owner of the part does not compete with his companion in the whole, so it is not in the sense of inheritance. And because the right of each one of them when it is in a shared part, and what each one of them takes by virtue of division is separated, and it is not the shared part, so what is taken is a substitute for his right, not the original right, so it is in the sense of inheritance and the estate that combines the two debts. In cases of division, it is only established by a right fixed in the dhimmah (liability); because the right of each one of them is in the obligation of the injury, and its obligation is in the dhimmah, so the division therein is 'awaliyyah, and upon this the cases are derived. Mentioned by our teacher, the scholar al-Tahṭāwī in his gloss on al-Durr al-Mukhtār. His saying: They considered the method of 'awal; that is when many prescribed shares combine in a denominator such that the total does not suffice, so 'awal is needed, as in a woman who died leaving a husband, a full sister, and a consanguine sister: for the husband half, for the full sister half, for the consanguine sister one-sixth as a completion of two-thirds, so the obligation increases to seven, while originally it was from six. 'Awal linguistically is used to mean inclination, i.e., injustice; it is said: so-and-so ya'ūlu 'alayya, i.e., leans unjustly. And to mean overcoming: it is said: 'īla ṣabruhu, i.e., was overcome. And to mean raising: it is said: 'āla al-mīzān, if he raised it. Technically: it is to increase the denominator by something from its fractions due to a share, and its purpose is to introduce deficiency into the shares of all heirs in one proportion. Its detail is in treatises on inheritance.

الفرض (al-farḍ): A prescribed share in inheritance fixed by the Qur'an or Sunnah.

Priority Based on Physical Control (Yad) in Disputes
Rulings on who is more entitled based on physical control

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule of priority in possession disputes and identify when physical control is outweighed by external evidence.

Core Idea
The one with stronger physical possession (yad) is entitled, but evidence (bayyinah) overrides mere control.

In Hanafi law, when two people dispute over an item, the one who has more obvious physical control (like wearing a garment or riding an animal) is given priority. This rule prevents arbitrary decisions. However, if either party brings evidence (bayyinah), that evidence takes precedence over possession. This lesson explores the specific scenarios and reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parties and their physical actions
Look at what each person is doing with the item. For example, one is wearing it, the other holds a part; or one is riding, the other holds reins.
The actions determine the strength of possession (yad).
2
Compare the strength of control
Wearing is stronger than holding; riding is stronger than holding reins; being on the saddle is stronger than being a pillion rider.
The more direct and integral the control, the stronger the presumption of ownership.
3
Recognize the exception for evidence
If both parties produce evidence (bayyinah), the external person's (one not in possession) evidence is given priority because it is an absolute proof.
Physical control alone is not proof; it is a circumstantial indicator.
4
Apply the rule to specific cases
Decide each scenario: wearer vs sleeve-holder, rider vs rein-holder, saddle-rider vs pillion rider, tail-holder vs rein-holder.
The manat (effective cause) is the apparentness of the control.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

فصل في التنازع بالأيدي
(واللابس أحق من آخذ الكم، والراكب من آخذ اللجام، ومن في السرج من رديفه
[١]قوله : واللابس... الخ ؛ يعني إذا تنازعا في قميص أحدهما لابسه، والآخر آخذ بكمه، فاللابس أولى من الآخذ، وكذا إذا تنازعا في دابة أحدهما راكبها والآخر آخذ لجامها ، فالراكب أولى من الآخذ ؛ لأن تصرف اللابس والراكب أظهر، فإنّه يختص بالملك ، فكانا صاحبا يد، والمتعلق خارج، فكانا أولى. بخلاف ما إذا أقاما البينة حيث تكون بينةُ الخارج أولى ؛ لأنها حجة مطلقاً، وبينة الخارج أكثر إثباتاً كما قررناه سابقاً، وأما التعلّق والأخذ ليس بحجة، وكذا التصرف، لكنه يستدل بالتمكن من التصرُّف على أنه كان في يده ، واليد دليل الملك، حتى جازت الشهادة له بالملك ، فيترك في يده حتى تقوم الحجج والتراجيح.
وكذا لو كان أحدهما راكباً على السرج ، والآخر رديفاً له كان الراكب أولى ؛ لأن تمكنه من ذلك الموضع دليل على تقدم يده، بخلاف ما إذا كانا راكبين على السرج ، حيث يكون بينهما ؛ لاستوائهما في التصرف.

Chapter on disputes regarding hands (possession). And the wearer is more entitled than the one holding the sleeve, and the rider than the one holding the reins, and the one on the saddle than his pillion rider, and the owner of its load than the one who hung his jug on it. Chapter on disputes regarding hands. And the wearer is more entitled than the one holding the sleeve, and the rider than the one holding the reins, and the one on the saddle than his pillion rider. His statement: 'And the wearer... etc.' means that if two persons dispute over a shirt, one wearing it and the other holding its sleeve, the wearer has priority over the holder. Likewise if they dispute over an animal, one riding it and the other holding its reins, the rider has priority over the holder because the action of the wearer and rider is more apparent, for it pertains to ownership, so they are possessors of the hand, and the one attached is external, so they have priority. Contrary to when they produce evidence; then the evidence of the external person is given priority because it is an absolute proof, and the evidence of the external person is more establishing as we have explained previously. As for attaching and taking, they are not proof, likewise action, but it is inferred from the ability to act that it was in his hand, and the hand is a proof of ownership, so he is left in his hand until evidence and preponderances are established. Likewise, if one is riding on the saddle and the other is a pillion rider behind him, the rider has priority because his ability to occupy that place indicates precedence of his hand, contrary to if both are riding on the saddle, then it is between them because of equality in action. And if one is holding its tail and the other holding its reins, they said: it should be judged for the one holding its reins because only the owner usually holds the reins, unlike holding the tail. The sleeve of a shirt is known, its plural is akmām and kamamah like 'inabah. The rein of a horse—it is said Arabic, and said Arabized, its plural is lujum like kitāb and kutub. From it is called the cloth that a menstruating woman ties around her waist a lujām. The saddle of an animal is known, its diminutive is surayj and a man is named by it. The pillion rider is one you carry behind you on the animal; it is said ˀardaftuhu ˀirdāfan and ˀirtadaftuhu, so he is radīf and radif. As in al-Miṣbāḥ al-Munīr.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This is a passage from the chapter on disputes regarding possession (yad). The matn states the general rule: the one with more obvious physical control (like wearing a garment or riding an animal) is preferred over someone merely touching or holding. The commentary explains the rationale: such control demonstrates stronger possession and likelihood of ownership. However, if both parties present evidence (bayyinah), the external person's evidence prevails because it is absolute proof, whereas mere physical control is only circumstantial. The passage also covers specific scenarios: a rider on the saddle vs. a pillion rider (rider wins), both riding on saddle (equal), and tail-holder vs. rein-holder (rein-holder wins because reins are typically held by owners). The commentary concludes with lexical notes on garments and animal equipment.

Key Terms
اللابس (al-lābis): The person who is wearing the garment in dispute.
آخذ الكم (ākhidh al-kumm): The person holding the sleeve of the garment.
الراكب (al-rākib): The rider of the animal in dispute.
آخذ اللجام (ākhidh al-lijām): The person holding the reins of the animal.
الرديف (al-radīf): The pillion rider—one who sits behind the main rider.

Key Terms from Source Passage

In possession disputes, the person with stronger physical control is entitled unless the other party presents overriding evidence.

Prioritizing Claims in Wall Disputes: Beams, Connection, and Istihsan
Hanafi commentary on priority in wall disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on wall disputes to determine ownership based on the number of beams and the type of connection, distinguishing between the qiyas and istihsan positions.

Core Idea
The wall belongs to the owner of three or more beams; istihsan overrides qiyas because greater use indicates stronger ownership.

When two parties claim a wall, Hanafi law does not automatically split ownership. Instead, it considers the degree of usage and connection. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains that three beams grant ownership, while fewer only reserve the beam's place, and that a square connection can trump beams entirely. Understanding this hierarchy is crucial for property disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the competing claims
Determine the basis of each party's claim: beams (number and placement) or connection (simple or square). This sets the stage for applying the relevant ruling.
2
State the qiyas position
The default analogy (qiyas) would treat all parties with physical presence (yad) equally, leading to equal division of the wall.
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf initially held this view.
3
Explain the istihsan position
Hanafi jurists preferred istihsan: the party with three or more beams demonstrates greater use (tasarruf), which is stronger than mere hand (yad). Thus the wall goes to them, and others only keep their beam's location.
This is the mufta bihi view from al-Nawazil.
4
Apply the three-beam rule
If one party has three or more beams and the other has fewer, the wall belongs to the one with three. The other only retains the exact spot of his beam as a right of use.
If both have three, the wall is shared equally.
5
Note the exception: square connection
A square connection (ittisal tarbi')—where the bricks interlock forming a square—gives the connection owner priority over even beam owners. This is the highest rank in the hierarchy of claims.
See al-Muhit for the full hierarchy: square connection > beams > alignment.

For the other is a water jug suspended from it, where the donkey belongs to the owner of the load, not the owner of the attachment, as you have learned. If one of them has beams upon it, and the other has a screen or a wall, then the disputed part — which is the lower part — belongs to the owner of the beams, and the screen belongs to its owner, like a lower floor for a man who has an upper floor for another. The owner of the screen is not ordered to remove the screen unless the claimant of the wall proves his entitlement to the wall by evidence; then he is ordered to remove it. If one of them has beams on the disputed wall, and the other has a connection to this wall from one side, then in our view: the owner of the beams is more entitled. Thus in the Fatawa of Qadi Khan. In it also: If one has one or two beams (less than three), and the other has three or more beams, it is mentioned in al-Nawazil that the wall belongs to the owner of the three, and the owner of less than three has the place of his beam. He said: This is istihsan, and it is the saying of Abu Hanifa later and of Abu Yusuf later. Abu Yusuf said: The qiyas is that the wall should be half between them, and that was what Abu Hanifa used to say initially, then he returned to istihsan. End. Square connection: the connection of a wall to a wall such that the bricks of this wall interlock with the bricks of that wall. It is called square connection because they are built to enclose, along with two other walls, a square place, not for one who has a horizontal beam. Al-judhu': plural of jidh', which is the trunk of a palm tree, or a beam of the ceiling, and is also called a rafter. Thus in al-Misbah al-Munir. [1] His statement: square connection. He said in al-Muhit: Hands in a wall have three ranks: square connection, contact and adjacency, placement of beams, and alignment of building. The most entitled is the owner of square connection, then the owner of beams, then the owner of alignment. End. The great scholar Qadi Khan said in his Fatawa: They differed on the interpretation of square connection. Al-Karkhi said in his interpretation: The interlocking of half bricks from both sides of the disputed wall with two walls belonging to one of them, and the two walls are connected to his wall opposite the disputed wall, such that it becomes a square like a dome, making the whole in the ruling of one building; and some masters adopted this. From Abu Yusuf: The interpretation of square connection by which the owner of connection is given priority over the owner of beams is the connection of both sides of the disputed wall through the interlocking of half bricks with a wall belonging to one of them. As for the connection of the two walls to another wall opposite the disputed wall, it is not considered; and upon this are most masters, among them Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi. So he (owner of connection) is more entitled than the owner of beams, and the owner of beams is not ordered to remove the beams because the owner of connection has earned the disputed wall by an apparent type, so he does not earn through it the removal of beams over the owner of beams. End. And it is mentioned in al-Kifayah, quoting from al-Dhakhira, the interpretation of tarbi': if the wall is of mud or brick, that half bricks of the disputed wall interlock with half bricks of the non-disputed wall, and half bricks of the non-disputed wall interlock with the disputed wall; and if the wall is of ...
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya is a commentary on al-Wiqaya, focusing on disputes over walls in claims. It begins with a continuation of a previous case about a donkey and a suspended water jug, but quickly moves to the main topic: when two parties claim a wall. The text presents several scenarios: (1) If one has beams (jiz') on the wall and the other has a screen or wall underneath, the lower part (where beams are) goes to the beam owner; the screen owner is not forced to remove it unless the wall claimant proves ownership. (2) If one has beams and the other has a connection (ittisal) from one side, the beam owner is preferred according to the relied-upon view (Zahir al-Riwayah). (3) A key ruling from al-Nawazil: if one has fewer than three beams and the other has three or more, the wall belongs to the one with three; the other only gets the spot of his beam. This is istihsan (juristic preference) overriding the qiyas of equal sharing. The text records that Abu Hanifa initially held the qiyas view then reverted to istihsan, and Abu Yusuf also ended with istihsan. (4) If both have three beams, the wall is shared equally; no consideration beyond three. (5) Regarding connection vs. beams: the Hanafi position prefers the beam owner over a simple connection (one side), but a square connection (ittisal tarbi') gives priority to the connection owner over beam owners, as per the hierarchy in al-Muhit: square connection, then beams, then alignment. The passage concludes with detailed definitions of square connection, showing disagreement among Hanafi scholars: al-Karkhi requires interlocking from both sides forming a square like a dome, while Abu Yusuf only requires interlocking from the two sides of the disputed wall. The most aut`horitative view, per most mashayikh including al-Sarakhsi, is that square connection means the disputed wall's bricks interlock with another wall belonging to one party, making him more entitled, but he cannot force the beam owner to remove beams because his entitlement is by a 'apparent type' (which is weaker than ownership).

Key Terms
جذع (jidh'): A beam, often a palm trunk, placed on a wall to support a roof; also called سهم السقف.
اتصال تربيع (ittisal tarbi'): Square connection: a wall connected to another such that bricks interlock, forming a square enclosure.
يد (yad): Hand: physical possession or control over property.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference: setting aside qiyas for a stronger evidence or custom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They share the wall equally.
B) The one with five beams gets the wall; the other only gets the spot of his beams.
C) The one with two beams gets the wall because he has fewer objects.
D) The wall is given to the one with the longer beam.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the owner of three or more beams on a wall is entitled to the whole wall; the owner of fewer beams only retains the spot of his beam, due to istihsan overriding qiyas.

Shared Wall Possession: Haradi, Bawari, and Jadh'
Ruling on possession when one neighbour places beams or reeds on a shared wall.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on possession of a shared wall when one neighbour places haradi or bawari, and explain the ikhtilaf regarding a single jadh'.

Core Idea
Placing reeds or mats on a wall does not establish possession; only beams integral to the wall's structure matter.

Neighbours often dispute over a shared wall, especially when one has placed materials on it. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains what counts as establishing possession in the Hanafi school. The key distinction is between structural beams (jadh') and non-structural reeds (haradi) or mats (bawari). The ruling also touches on a related principle about multiple rooms in a courtyard.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested property and the dispute
Recognize that the passage deals with a wall (ha'it) between two neighbours when they disagree over ownership or usage rights. The default ruling is that the wall is shared equally unless one can prove a stronger claim.
The base text states: 'بل هو بين الجارين' – it is between them.
2
Distinguish between materials placed on the wall
The passage classifies materials into three types: haradi (reeds placed on beams), bawari (bamboo mats), and jadh' (wooden beams). Only jadh' is considered a valid use of the wall for possession purposes.
Haradi and bawari are placed for roofing independence, not for the wall's structural support.
3
Apply the general rule for haradi and bawari
If one neighbour has haradi or bawari on the wall and the other has nothing, the wall remains shared equally. These materials do not establish a 'hand' (yad) because the wall is built for supporting beams, not reeds or mats.
The text explicitly says: 'المرادي لا تعتبر' (haradi is not considered).
4
Analyze the ikhtilaf on a single beam (jadh')
If one neighbour has a single jadh' and the other has nothing, there is disagreement among Hanafi mashayikh. Some say the wall is shared equally because one beam is negligible; others give preference to the beam owner because walls are sometimes built for a single beam. The relied-upon view appears to be equality.
The text presents both views and does not strongly prefer one, but leans towards equality.
5
Consider the analogy of the courtyard
The passage includes a related ruling: the owner of one room in a house has the same rights to the courtyard as the owner of many rooms. This reinforces the principle that a greater number of claims does not automatically grant preference (لا يرجح بكثرة العلة).
This is a separate but parallel principle: possession is not weighted by quantity of use.

(وذو بيت من دار كذي بيوت منها في حق ساحتِها) ، بناءً على أن لا يرجح بكثرة العلة.

Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the possession of a shared wall (ha'it) between neighbours in a dispute. The base text states that the wall is between the two neighbours equally, and this commentary explains the conditions. The key issue is whether placing wooden beams (jadh') or reeds (haradi) and bamboo mats (bawari) on the wall establishes possession. The Hanafi position: haradi and bawari do not count because they are used for independent roofing, not for supporting the wall itself. Only jadh' (beams) that are integral to the wall's structure matter. If one neighbour has a single jadh' and the other has nothing, there is ikhtilaf among the shuyukh: some say they are equal because a single beam is negligible, while others give preference to the owner of the beam because walls are sometimes built for a single beam. However, if one has haradi or bawari and the other has nothing, the wall is shared equally, and haradi is not considered. The ruling applies when the wall is in both hands and no preference can be established by the reeds. The overall authority weight is from the later fatawa and sharh works (like 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah), but the principles derive from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
هرادي (harādī): In this text, haradi refers to the ends of reeds or canes placed on the wall during construction, used for roofing.
بواري (bawārī): Bamboo mats placed on the wall, also for roofing, not considered as establishing possession.
جذع (jidh‘): A wooden beam placed horizontally on a wall to support roofing; its presence indicates usage for the wall's purpose.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If one neighbour places only haradi on a shared wall and the other places nothing, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The wall belongs to the one who placed haradi.
B) The wall is shared equally.
C) The wall belongs to the one who placed nothing.
D) The wall is considered abandoned.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, possession of a shared wall is determined by structural use (beams), not by placing reeds or mats; a single beam is disputed but equality is the default.

Rules for Claiming Lineage When a Sold Slave Gives Birth
Expansion of the base matn on a sold slave giving birth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which the seller or buyer can validly claim lineage of a child born to a sold slave, and identify the legal consequences (umm walad, tahreer, istilad, wala') based on the time of birth.

Core Idea
A sold slave's child belongs to the seller if born within six months of sale; otherwise to the buyer.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains the Hanafi rulings on claiming lineage of a child born to a sold female slave. The key determinant is the time between sale and birth. The scholars distinguish between early birth (less than six months) and late birth (six months to two years), each with different rules for claims and their legal effects, such as the slave becoming an umm walad or the child becoming free-born.

Guided Path
1
Identify the time of birth relative to sale
Determine whether the birth occurred in less than six months from the sale, or at six months or more (up to two years). This is the primary condition that determines which rules apply.
The six-month threshold is based on the minimum period of pregnancy in Hanafi fiqh.
2
Identify who claims the child
Consider whether the seller claims, the buyer claims, both claim simultaneously, or they claim successively. The text provides specific rulings for each combination.
A claim by the seller requires either his explicit da'wa or two witnesses testifying to his acknowledgment.
3
Apply the corresponding rule for early birth (<6 months)
If birth is less than six months and the seller claims (or witnesses testify), the child's lineage is established from the seller, the sale is rescinded, the slave becomes his umm walad, and the price is returned to the buyer. If the buyer claims alone, his claim is valid and is considered a claim of tahreer (freeing), giving him wala' over the child. If both claim together, the seller's claim takes precedence; if successively, the earlier claimant prevails.
4
Apply the corresponding rule for late birth (≥6 months)
If birth is six months or more, the seller's claim is only valid if the buyer confirms it; otherwise it is rejected. The buyer's claim alone is valid and is considered a claim of istilad (making the child free-born), so the buyer gets no wala'. If both claim together or successively, only the buyer's claim is accepted.
The buyer's claim in late birth is one of istilad, not tahreer, so the child is free-born and the buyer has no patronage rights.
5
Determine the legal consequences
Based on the scenario, identify whether the slave becomes an umm walad, whether the child is free or free-born, whether the sale is rescinded, and who holds wala'.
Umm walad status means the slave gains freedom upon the master's death. Tahreer creates a wala' relationship; istilad does not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : مبيعة ... الخ ؛ توضيح المقام : إنَّ رجلاً إذا باعــت أمــة فــولدت عـنـد المشتري، فإن جاءت بالولد لأقل من ستة أشهر من وقت البيع وادعى البائع الولد أو شهد شاهدان على إقرار البائع به يثبت نسبه منه ، فتصير الجارية أم ولد له، وينتقض البيع ويردُّ الثمن على المشتري. وإن ادعاه المشتري صحت دعوته، ويثبت النسب منه ، وصارتِ الجارية أم ولد له ، وكانت دعوى المشتري دعوى تحرير ، حتى كان للمشتري ولاء على الولد ، وإن ادعياه معاً فدعوة البائع أولى ، وإن ادعياه على التعاقب فالسابق منهما أولى، أيهما كان. وإن جاءت بالولد بستة أشهر فصاعداً ما بينهما وبين سنتين من وقت البيع، وقد علم ذلك، فإن ادعى البائع نسب الولد وحده لا تصح دعوتُه إلا بتصديق المشتري ، وإن ادعاه المشتري وحده صحت دعوته، ويجب أن تكون دعوته دعوى استيلاد، حتى كان الولد حرَّ الأصل، ولا يكون للمشتري ولاء ، وإن ادعياه معاً أو متعاقباً تصح دعوة المشتري دون البائع.
His saying: 'A slave woman who gave birth' etc. Clarification of the context: When a man sells a slave woman and she gives birth at the buyer's place, if she delivers the child in less than six months from the time of sale, and the seller claims the child, or two witnesses testify to the seller's acknowledgment of it, his lineage from him is established, so the slave girl becomes an umm walad for him, the sale is rescinded, and the price is returned to the buyer. And if the buyer claims it, his claim is valid, lineage from him is established, and the slave girl becomes an umm walad for him, and the buyer's claim is a claim of freeing (tahreer), so that the buyer has wala' over the child. If both claim together, the seller's claim is prior. If they claim successively, the earlier of them is prior, whichever it may be. If she gives birth at six months or more, up to two years from the time of sale, and that is known, then if the seller alone claims the child's lineage, his claim is not valid unless the buyer confirms it. If the buyer alone claims it, his claim is valid, and his claim must be a claim of istilad, so that the child is free-born (hurr al-asl), and the buyer has no wala'. If both claim together or successively, the buyer's claim is valid, not the seller's.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage explains the fiqh ruling regarding a sold female slave who gives birth while in the buyer's possession. The key factor is the time between sale and birth. If the birth occurs less than six months after sale, lineage is attributed to the seller if he claims it (or if witnesses testify to his acknowledgment). The sale is then rescinded, and the slave becomes an umm walad for the seller. If the buyer claims the child, his claim is valid but considered a claim of freeing (tahreer), meaning the child is free and the buyer has wala' (patronage) over the child. If both claim, the seller's claim takes precedence. If they claim at different times, the first claimant prevails. If the birth occurs six months or more after sale (up to two years), the rules change: the seller's claim requires the buyer's confirmation; the buyer's claim is valid and is a claim of istilad (making the child free-born with no wala'). If both claim, the buyer's claim prevails. These nuanced rules reflect the Hanafi school's careful consideration of the minimum and maximum gestation periods and the implications for ownership and lineage.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master, thereby gaining freedom upon his death.
دعوى الاستيلاد (da'wa al-istilad): The buyer's claim that the child is his own offspring from the slave, making the child free-born.
ولاء (wala'): Patronage right of a former master over a freed slave, including inheritance in absence of blood heirs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a sold slave gives birth 6 months after the sale, and both the seller and buyer claim the child, whose claim is accepted?
A) The seller's claim
B) The buyer's claim
C) Both claims are accepted equally
D) Neither claim is accepted

Key Takeaway
The time of birth relative to sale determines lineage: less than six months favors the seller, six months or more favors the buyer, with corresponding legal effects on the status of mother and child.

The Seller's Claim of Paternity: Istihsan vs Qiyas
Inter-madhhab dispute on seller's paternity claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on a seller's claim of paternity after selling a slave girl and evaluate the opposing Zufar/Shafi'i argument using the concepts of istihsan and qiyas.

Core Idea
In hidden matters like paternity, apparent contradiction is excused, and istihsan overrides qiyas.

This lesson examines a classic Hanafi ruling where a seller claims paternity of a child born to a slave girl he sold. The Hanafi school accepts this claim based on istihsan (juristic preference), while Zufar and al-Shafi'i reject it based on qiyas (analogy). Understanding this dispute reveals how the Hanafi school prioritizes hidden realities over surface contradictions in matters of lineage.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify exactly what the Hanafi ruling is: the seller's claim is accepted, the child's lineage is established, the slave becomes umm walad, the sale is annulled, and the price is returned.
This applies even if the buyer also claims the child, as long as the seller's claim is simultaneous or earlier.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Zufar (a Hanafi scholar) and al-Shafi'i hold that the claim is invalid because the sale itself is an acknowledgment that the slave is not an umm walad, making the claim contradictory.
They compare it to a claim of emancipation after sale, which is similarly rejected.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The opponent uses qiyas: the seller's act of selling is like an admission that the child is not his; a Muslim does not sell his umm walad. The contradiction invalidates the claim.
They analogize to cases where the seller claims to have freed the slave before sale.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school counters with istihsan: lineage is built on hidden matters (mabna al-nasab 'ala al-khafa). Conception is hidden, so a person might not know initially. Therefore, contradiction is excused. They cite the analogy of a husband retracting li'an.
This distinguishes lineage from manifest acts like emancipation, which are not hidden.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Hanafi, the seller reclaims the child and the mother (as umm walad) and refunds the price; under the opposing view, the sale stands and the claim is rejected.
This affects property rights and lineage status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
